Development Plan for Cupcakes+Broccoli (Squarespace 7.1) and Lillier (Shopify OS 2.0)
1. Local Development Environment Setup
Squarespace (C+B) Local Setup: Since Squarespace 7.1 doesn’t support full Developer Mode, plan to build and test custom code locally, then inject it into Squarespace. Create a local project folder for C+B with subfolders for HTML prototypes, CSS, and JS. You can prototype key page layouts in plain HTML/CSS/JS on your computer, using the same classes and structure you’ll later use in Squarespace code blocks. While you cannot run the Squarespace site itself locally on 7.1, you can simulate pages by recreating their structure in static HTML and testing your custom code there. Once satisfied, you’ll embed this code into Squarespace via code injection or code blocks (detailed in Section 3)
. Use a staging Squarespace site or a trial site to safely paste and refine your code before the production site. Shopify (Lillier) Local Setup: Leverage Shopify’s CLI tools to develop the Lillier theme locally. Begin by installing the Shopify CLI and using it to initialize a new theme based on Shopify’s Dawn (Online Store 2.0 reference theme)
. For example:
npm install -g @shopify/cli @shopify/theme    # Install Shopify CLI
shopify theme init [theme-name]               # Clone Dawn into a new theme project
cd [theme-name]
This will create a local theme folder with Dawn’s files as a starting point. Next, create a Shopify development store (via the Shopify Partner dashboard) to preview your theme. Use the CLI’s development server to watch for changes and sync to your dev store in real-time
:
shopify theme dev --store your-dev-store.myshopify.com
This command uploads your local theme and allows live preview at a special development URL. As you edit files locally (Liquid, JSON, CSS, JS), changes will hot-reload in the browser. Keep this server running during development. Use version control (Git) for your theme code to track changes. Once the theme is ready, you can push it to the live store either by using shopify theme push
 or by connecting a GitHub repo in Shopify for CI deployment. Set up a theme development workflow: work on the development theme in the Shopify store, test thoroughly, then publish as the live theme when approved. This approach ensures you have a sandbox for Lillier that won’t affect customers until deployment.
2. Unified Design System & Component Architecture
To maintain subtle brand cohesion between C+B and Lillier, establish a unified design system that both sites will use. This includes a shared visual language (colors, typography, spacing, and UI components) and consistent UX patterns, implemented separately on each platform:
Define Design Tokens: Decide on a common palette (primary, secondary, neutral colors) and typography (font families for headings, body, accents) that both brands will use. For consistency, create a style guide document. For example, if both brands use a signature color (with C+B perhaps using a vibrant accent and Lillier a more muted tone), include those in the palette. Define CSS variables or LESS variables for these tokens in your code. Squarespace: The Custom CSS editor supports LESS, so you can define variables like @primary-color and reuse them
. Shopify: You can use CSS custom properties or SCSS (if you set up a build step) for the same effect. This way, a change in the design system (e.g. adjusting a color shade) can be mirrored on both sites by updating the respective variable.
Shared Components: Identify key UI components that will appear on both sites, and design them to have a similar look and feel. Examples: navigation bar, footer, buttons, form inputs, cards for content or products, modal dialogs, etc. Document the HTML/CSS structure for these components and implement them in each platform. For instance, define a button style (border radius, hover effect, font) and apply it via a CSS class .btn in both Squarespace (via custom CSS) and Shopify (in theme CSS). Ensure both sites’ buttons feel related (same shape and scale) even if colors differ slightly per brand. Another example: both sites might use a hero section with a large background image/video and a bold heading. The C+B hero might feature an educational tagline, while Lillier’s hero might show a product or lifestyle image – but the layout and typography can be alike.
Responsive, Mobile-First Design: Both sites should be designed mobile-first, given Lillier’s emphasis on mobile usage and C+B’s broad audience. Define breakpoints and make sure your CSS adapts components for smaller screens gracefully (e.g. nav collapses into a mobile menu, grids become single column). Test your design system components on mobile early. On Shopify, Dawn is mobile-first and already includes responsive grid and typography settings which you can customize; on Squarespace, use CSS media queries in the Custom CSS to adjust styling for mobile if needed (and Squarespace’s built-in styles will also handle responsiveness for standard blocks).
Subtle Brand Distinctions: While the overall structure and components are shared, allow for subtle differences to reinforce each brand’s identity. For example, C+B (education platform) might use more vibrant accent colors or playful imagery (reflecting “Cupcakes + Broccoli” – a mix of fun and healthy), whereas Lillier (marketplace) might use a refined, minimalist aesthetic (inspired by Aesop’s clean design). The typography could differ (e.g., a serif font for C+B’s editorial feel and a modern sans-serif for Lillier’s product focus), but still chosen to complement each other. Ensure these variations are captured in the design system: perhaps two theme variants of the style guide, one for each brand, that share base components but swap out certain styles.
Component Architecture: In code terms, break down pages into sections/components. For instance, a card component for previewing an article or product should have a consistent structure: image on top, title, maybe author or price info. You’ll implement it in Squarespace by either using a Summary Block styled via CSS or a custom code block, and in Shopify as a Liquid snippet or section for product cards. By planning these components in the abstract, you ensure both implementations follow the same pattern. This will also help in maintaining cohesion: if you adjust the design of a component in one site, you can mirror it on the other. Keep a checklist of components (hero, card, carousel, testimonial, etc.) that need implementation on both platforms.
3. Developing Cupcakes+Broccoli on Squarespace 7.1 (Education Platform)
Cupcakes+Broccoli (C+B) will be built on Squarespace 7.1, utilizing as much custom code as needed for unique features, while still leveraging Squarespace’s CMS for ease of client editing. Below is a step-by-step approach: 3.1 Choose Base Template & Structure: Start with a Squarespace 7.1 template (since all 7.1 templates share the same underlying framework, you can choose any that closely matches the navigation layout you want). Set up the basic site structure: pages for key sections like Home, About, Blog/Articles, maybe Tools, Events, etc., and ensure the navigation menu has the necessary links. Configure site style settings for basic fonts, text sizes, and colors to approximate your design system (this makes default blocks look on-brand without code). For example, set the base font to your chosen body font, set heading fonts/sizes to something close to your design, and adjust built-in color themes if needed. 3.2 Inject Global Custom Code (HTML/CSS/JS): Squarespace 7.1 doesn’t allow editing template files directly
, but it provides Custom Code Injection and Custom CSS options. Use these to add site-wide code:
Global CSS: Open Design > Custom CSS in Squarespace and add your stylesheet (you can paste your entire compiled CSS/LESS here)
. This CSS will override or augment the template’s styles globally. Here you will implement the design system: e.g., define your color variables (LESS: @primary-color: #...;), style for classes you plan to use (e.g., .btn { ... }, .hero-section { ... }), and overrides for Squarespace defaults (like making default buttons or headings match your design). Keep the CSS organized and commented for maintainability (e.g., section off “Header styles”, “Footer styles”, etc.).
Header Injection (Site-Wide): In Settings > Advanced > Code Injection, use the Header field to insert any scripts or <meta> tags that should load on every page
. This is the place for external libraries (for example, if you need a specific JavaScript library for interactive tools or a CSS framework reset) and for site metadata. For C+B, a likely use is inserting the Outseta membership script here (see Section 3.5) so that membership modals and gating apply globally
. Also include any needed polyfills or utility scripts. Make sure to wrap scripts in <script> tags and CSS in <style> if adding via this panel. (Tip: Only load what’s necessary globally to keep performance high. For instance, your AI tool script might only be needed on a specific page – that can be loaded via page-specific injection instead of globally.)
Footer Injection (Site-Wide): Still in Code Injection, use the Footer field for any scripts that should run after the page loads
. This is ideal for your custom JavaScript that enhances UI components or initializes plugins on the page, since it will load without blocking the main content. For example, if you have written a custom JS file for interactive behavior (like toggling UI elements or integrating with an API), you can place it here inside <script> tags or reference an external script URL. By loading in the footer, you ensure all DOM elements are present when the script runs, and it won’t delay page rendering.
3.3 Page-Specific Custom Code: For pages that need unique code (HTML or JS), use Squarespace Code Blocks or page header injection:
Code Blocks for Custom Sections: Squarespace’s Code Block allows inserting arbitrary HTML/JS into page content
. Use these blocks to create structured content where Squarespace’s native blocks are insufficient. For example, the C+B homepage might need a custom hero section with a background video and a marketing headline. You can implement this by adding a Code Block at the top of the page and pasting your HTML structure for the hero (e.g., a <section class="hero-section"> containing an <video> tag or an image and heading text). Then your global CSS (added earlier) will style .hero-section accordingly. Another example: if you have an AI tool page where users input text to get AI-generated output, you can create a Code Block that contains the HTML for the tool interface (input fields, results container) and a <script> that initializes the AI API call. Keep Code Block HTML clean and minimal, using classes and IDs that your CSS/JS target.
Page Header Injection (per-page): Squarespace also allows adding code that applies only to a specific page. In the page’s settings (Pages > gear icon > Advanced > Header), you can inject code that will be added to that page’s head or top of body
. Use this for scripts or style needed only on one page. For instance, if the AI tool uses a large third-party script that you’d prefer not to load on every page, you can include its <script src="..."> via that page’s header injection. This keeps it lightweight on other pages
. Likewise, if one page needs custom CSS tweaks not relevant elsewhere, you can add a <style> block in that page’s header. Remember that page-specific injection should be used sparingly and only for truly page-specific needs; for most styling and interactive behavior, it’s better to have it in global CSS/JS and just scope it with page-specific selectors (Squarespace gives each page a unique ID in the HTML which you can use in your CSS to target that page).
3.4 Using Squarespace Native Features vs Custom Code: To ensure the client can easily edit content, lean on native Squarespace content types when possible, and enhance them with custom code:
Blog for Articles or Courses: If C+B will publish articles, tutorials, or course content, use the built-in Blog or Pages collection for this content so the client can add entries in the Squarespace UI. You can then style the list of posts with CSS to achieve an editorial look. For example, a blog page can list entries with a featured image and excerpt – use CSS to style that list to match your design (or use a Summary Block to lay them out in a grid and style it). This way, adding new content doesn’t require coding. If courses need to be behind a paywall, you could still use a Blog or Collection for courses and simply restrict access (see 3.5 below).
Gallery or Summary Blocks: For any curated lists (e.g., a list of resources or tools on C+B), consider using a Collection (like a Gallery or Custom Collection) and a Summary Block to display it. Then apply CSS to that Summary Block to adjust the layout (e.g., remove unwanted padding, style titles). This gives the client a graphical way to add items (just add an item to the collection) while your code handles the presentation.
Built-in Forms and Integrations: If C+B needs forms (contact, sign-up for newsletter), use Squarespace’s Form Blocks for ease, and style them via CSS for consistency. If the design calls for something more custom-looking, you can still use the form block HTML (which has known structure) and just hide or rearrange elements with CSS/JS as needed, rather than coding an entire form from scratch.
In general, reserve pure custom code solutions for elements Squarespace cannot handle. This ensures after handoff the client can use Squarespace’s UI for most content updates, and the custom code acts as a layer of enhancement. 3.5 Membership & Subscription Integration (C+B): A core feature is a members-only area with subscription payments. Squarespace offers Member Areas natively, but you may opt for a third-party integration like Outseta (or MemberSpace) for more flexibility and lower fees
. Evaluate options and implement as follows:
Squarespace Member Areas (Built-in): This is the simplest to implement through the Squarespace interface. Enable Member Areas, set up subscription products and pricing in Squarespace, and assign pages to those member areas (thus gating them). Little custom code is needed; Squarespace handles login, account management, and paywall. Drawback: Squarespace charges a transaction fee (~9% on member payments)
 which is significant. Also design control on the login/account screens might be limited.
Outseta Integration: Outseta is an all-in-one membership toolkit that was used on Squarespace’s own site historically
. To integrate Outseta:
Sign up for Outseta and configure your membership plans (pricing, etc.) and connect Stripe in Outseta’s settings
.
Add Outseta’s embed script. Copy the Outseta Quick Start embed code (a script tag) from Outseta’s dashboard, and paste it into Squarespace’s global header code injection
. This script will enable Outseta’s auth system across your site.
Add membership trigger links/buttons. Outseta provides special links for sign-up, login, logout, and account profile pop-ups
. In your site’s header or nav, create buttons or links for “Login” / “Sign Up” and set their URLs to those Outseta-provided links (e.g. href="https://[yourcompany].outseta.com/auth/login" or a special <a data-outseta-modal="login"> format, per Outseta docs). Ensure these open in the same tab (not new tab) so they trigger the modal
.
Protect content: Outseta can restrict page access. Typically, you’d wrap members-only page content in a special div with attributes (if Outseta requires) or simply rely on Outseta’s ability to redirect users who aren’t logged in when they try to access certain URLs (configured in Outseta’s Protected Content settings). You might need to add a small script in page header injection for protected pages to check Outseta’s auth state and hide/show content accordingly (Outseta’s docs will provide the exact method). Test this thoroughly: try accessing a members page while logged out to ensure it prompts login or shows a “Members only” message.
Outseta also provides additional features (CRM, email). If needed, integrate those (for example, use Outseta’s email list instead of Squarespace’s newsletter).
MemberStack or MemberSpace: These are alternative membership solutions. MemberStack historically didn’t officially support Squarespace easily, and MemberSpace does support Squarespace by overlaying a login system. If you choose MemberSpace, you’d add their code snippet via code injection and configure protected pages in MemberSpace’s dashboard. The implementation would be similar: insert script, set up login/signup widgets (often MemberSpace can overlay a login form on top of Squarespace). The plan would be: sign up for MemberSpace, configure plans (it can integrate with Stripe), inject their script, and test page gating. Outseta is a more all-in-one solution, but MemberSpace might be simpler if only gating is needed. Note: Only choose one platform to avoid conflicts; Outseta is a strong choice given the client’s needs and its integration guide.
Custom Stripe + Custom Auth: Not recommended unless necessary. This would involve building your own membership system: embedding Stripe Checkout for subscription payments and writing custom code to create user accounts and limit content. Squarespace doesn’t give server-side code access, so you’d likely need an external database or service to track users. This approach is complex and beyond the scope of a quick launch. Instead, using the above services leverages their secure user authentication. If a truly custom approach is desired, consider building a separate web app for login and using the Squarespace site mainly as marketing, but that contradicts the requirement to use Squarespace. Thus, in this plan we stick with either Squarespace Member Areas or an integration like Outseta for subscriptions.
Implement the membership system early in development so you can incorporate any specific requirements (like adding a “My Account” page link, or showing/hiding certain menu items based on login state – which you can do with a bit of JS that checks if user is logged in via Outseta’s JS API or MemberSpace’s API). 3.6 Content & Features for C+B:
Educational Content Pages: Build out a template or consistent layout for content pages such as articles or course pages. If using a Blog for courses/articles, style the blog post page via CSS (Squarespace will output the content in a structured way, e.g., blog title as an <h1>, content in a rich text format). You can add a Code Block at the end of posts for special elements like an AI-powered widget (“Try this tool” embedded in an article, for example). Ensure code doesn’t conflict with the rich text content styling.
AI Tools Integration: If C+B offers AI tools (perhaps a chatbot or a content generator for entrepreneurs), implement these likely as separate pages or modals. For example, you might create a page “Business Idea Generator” that has a custom Code Block with a text input, a button, and a <div> for output. Use JavaScript (possibly with a third-party AI API like OpenAI) to process input and show results. Because this is custom, be mindful of API keys (don’t expose them publicly; you might need to use a serverless function or a proxy if the API calls require a secret). If the AI tool is complex, consider hosting it externally and embedding via an <iframe> or widget. If using JS directly, load the script only on that page (via page header code injection or inside the Code Block). Test on desktop and mobile (especially if using modals or large results).
Community/Convening Features: The brief mentions C+B is also a convening platform. If future community forums or events are planned, note that Squarespace has limited community features. A workaround could be embedding a third-party forum (like Circle or Discourse) via an iframe or link, or using commenting features on blog posts for Q&A. For events, Squarespace does have an Events collection you could use to list events. Keep the site structure flexible to add these sections later. Possibly include a “Community” or “Forum” link in the nav that can integrate later.
Testing and QA (Squarespace): As you integrate code, frequently preview the site on various devices. Use Squarespace’s built-in device preview or simply open on actual devices. Check that your code-injected elements do not break the layout. Pay attention to Squarespace updates: since you’re injecting custom code, ensure that if Squarespace changes some markup (especially for things like product or list pages
), your code still works. For example, the Beyondspace guide notes that Squarespace might update markup for product lists which can affect custom code
. Keep custom code relatively decoupled from internal DOM if possible (e.g., target high-level containers or use your own wrappers to avoid relying on Squarespace’s internal classes that could change).
4. Developing Lillier on Shopify Online Store 2.0 (E-commerce Marketplace)
Lillier will be a Shopify-based e-commerce site, taking advantage of Online Store 2.0’s flexibility and Shopify’s robust commerce features. The plan is to build a custom Shopify theme (likely starting from Dawn for efficiency) and implement all required features (product listings, collections, checkout, etc.) with a mobile-first, clean approach. Steps and best practices: 4.1 Starting the Theme (Dawn Customization): Using the Dawn theme as a foundation
 provides a well-structured starting point with OS 2.0 features (JSON templates, sections, etc.). After initializing the theme (as done in Section 1), review the Dawn directory structure (especially the sections, snippets, templates folders):
Templates (JSON): Dawn’s /templates folder contains JSON files for each page type (e.g., index.json for home, product.json, collection.json, page.json, etc.). These define which sections are present by default on those pages
. You will modify these or create new ones if needed. For instance, if Lillier needs a special About page layout, you might create page.about.json and a corresponding alternate template in Shopify so that the About page can have a custom section arrangement.
Sections: The /sections folder has Liquid files corresponding to each section (e.g., header, footer, main-product, featured-collection, etc.). Dawn comes with many generic sections. Decide which ones to keep and which to replace or heavily restyle. Likely you will:
Customize the Header section for the Lillier branding and navigation needs (e.g., include logo, menu, perhaps a quick cart icon or account icon if needed).
Customize the Footer section similarly (update links, add any newsletter sign-up form connected to Shopify’s customer list or a Mailchimp form).
Use Dawn’s existing product grid or list sections for collection pages, but apply CSS changes to declutter (Aesop-inspired means maybe more whitespace, hide extra UI like compare buttons or overly busy badges).
Remove sections not needed (for example, if Dawn has a slideshow or rich text section not in the design, you can omit them to streamline the theme).
Create new custom sections for any unique design elements. For Lillier’s curated, editorial feel, you might introduce sections like “Featured Brand Story” (a section combining text and image in an editorial layout) or “Journal Highlights” (if Lillier includes editorial content). Use the Shopify section schema to make these configurable.
4.2 Implementing Design System in Shopify Theme: Bring the unified design styles into the theme:
CSS Styling: Dawn uses a modern CSS setup (in newer versions it might use CSS variables and a single CSS file). Locate the main CSS file (often assets/base.css or theme.css). You can directly edit this, or if you prefer, set up a SCSS compilation step. Implement the design tokens here: for example, define :root { --primary-color: ...; --font-heading: ...; } if using vanilla CSS, or use SCSS variables. Update typographic styles (headers, body text) to use the chosen fonts and sizes. Ensure to set button styles, form input styles, etc., to match those defined in the design system.
Theme Settings: To allow the client to tweak some styling easily, use theme settings (in config/settings_schema.json). For instance, define a setting for primary color, secondary color, and fonts. Dawn already has a color scheme settings and font picker in its settings – modify or add to those if needed. Then use the Liquid {{ settings.color_primary }} (or similar) in your theme CSS or styles where appropriate (Dawn might bind these to CSS variables already). This way, if the client wants to adjust a color shade, they can do it from Shopify’s Theme Editor without touching code. It also future-proofs the design system if minor brand changes occur.
Layout and HTML: Use Liquid and HTML to structure content according to your components. For example, if you want a very minimal product card (image and product name only on collection pages), edit the product-card.liquid snippet (or Dawn’s equivalent) to remove unwanted elements (like remove reviews stars, etc.), and add any wrappers needed for styling. If adding new content elements, prefer to make them configurable: e.g., if a section has text overlay on an image, use the section’s schema to provide a text field and image picker for the merchant.
4.3 Shopify Online Store 2.0 Sections & Flexibility: OS 2.0 allows merchants to add or rearrange sections on most pages
. We want Lillier’s admins to have flexibility post-handoff:
Sections on Every Page: By default, all templates (except checkout) can have dynamic sections. Ensure any unique page designs are built as sections so they can be moved or removed. For instance, the homepage will consist of multiple sections (hero, product collection highlights, editorial content, etc.). Each should be a separate section file. In the theme editor, the client can re-order or remove them. Similarly, for regular content pages, you might rely on a rich-text section for general content, but you can also create a “Custom content section” that includes rich text and an image in a certain layout for editorial storytelling – the client could add that section to any page.
JSON Templates: Plan if multiple templates are needed. Perhaps all product pages share one layout, so product.json is fine. But if some products need a different template (maybe one category of products has additional info), you can create an alternate template file and assign it to those products in Shopify. The same goes for pages: if the About page layout is special, use a separate template. In JSON, define the default sections for that template. For example, page.about.json might by default include a banner image section and a rich text section pre-added. This ensures consistency in structure but still allows editing. Document for the client which templates are used for what, so they don’t accidentally switch to a wrong template.
Metafields for Dynamic Content: Shopify now lets you define metafields easily for products and other entities (no app needed)
. If Lillier’s design calls for extra structured data (e.g., a product needs to display a “Ingredient list” or “Designer bio” on the product page like an editorial touch), use metafields. For example, add a product metafield “Brand Story” (rich text) and expose it on the product page via Liquid ({{ product.metafields.custom.brand_story }}) in a section. The client can fill that metafield in each product’s admin page without needing to touch theme code
. This aligns with the curated storytelling aspect of Lillier.
4.4 Key Template Customizations:
Homepage: Design a modular homepage with multiple custom sections. Likely sections: Hero banner (with a big image or video and tagline – allow image/video selection and text in the section settings), Featured Products or “New & Noteworthy” (a section that displays a specific collection or hand-picked products; could use Shopify’s Collection Listing section or a custom one where the merchant can pick a few products), Editorial snippet (if there’s a blog, show latest article or a special story to engage users daily), perhaps a Newsletter signup section for building community. Implement each as a section file with appropriate schema fields so the client can edit text/images. For inspiration: Aesop’s site often has a clean hero and then a grid of products with lots of whitespace; emulate that spacious layout with CSS.
Collection Pages: Use the default collection template but simplify it. Dawn by default has a sidebar filter and product grid. If “not cluttered” is the goal, you might remove the sidebar (unless filtering is desired for UX). Simplify the header of the collection page to maybe just show the collection title and description with nice typography. The products grid: adjust number of products per row on mobile vs desktop for optimal display. Possibly use 2-column on mobile to mimic an Instagram-like grid if that suits, or 1-column for a editorial feel (though that would require lots of scrolling). You can configure this via CSS or theme settings.
Product Pages: Emphasize imagery and product story. Dawn’s product page layout can be tweaked to move elements around. For example, if you want a more editorial style, you might put the product description in a tab or below the images rather than all to the right side. Keep it clean: maybe hide overly commercial elements if not needed (like quantity selector style can be minimal, etc.). Ensure that the Add to Cart button is prominent even with a minimalist design (mobile-first means make it easy to tap). If repeating purchases or subscriptions are a goal (repeat-use UX), consider enabling Shopify’s native subscription apps integration if needed, or at least ensure customer accounts and re-ordering are easy. (Shopify has a “Re-order” feature on order status page or you can add a “Buy it again” section in customer account if desired via apps or Liquid).
Cart and Checkout: Shopify checkout is not theme-editable beyond logo and colors (unless on Plus). So focus on the cart page or drawer. Decide between using a cart page or an AJAX cart drawer for smoother UX. Dawn comes with a cart drawer functionality (sidebar). Customize its appearance to match the clean style (remove any clutter like item thumbnail if you want a text-only list, or vice versa). Ensure the cart icon is visible in the header (for mobile, maybe an icon in the nav that shows item count).
Additional Pages: About, Contact, FAQ, etc. These can use the page template with the rich text section or custom sections. You might design an About section that has an image and text side by side (for brand story). Implement that as a section with those fields so the client can edit the about content visually. For a Contact page, Shopify’s contact form can be enabled by creating a page with the contact template. Style that form through CSS to match the theme.
4.5 Shopify Liquid and Code Tips:
Liquid Templating: Use Liquid for dynamic content – for example, in a custom section that highlights a specific collection, you could allow the merchant to pick a collection via the section settings, then in Liquid do:
{% for product in collections[section.settings.collection].products limit:4 %}
  <!-- custom markup for product card -->
  <div class="product-card">
    <a href="{{ product.url }}">
      <img src="{{ product | image_url: width: 400 }}" alt="{{ product.title }}">
      <h3>{{ product.title }}</h3>
    </a>
  </div>
{% endfor %}
This loops through products and outputs a simple card. You would style .product-card in CSS. By using Liquid, the content stays dynamic (if the merchant updates that collection’s items, the homepage updates automatically, for instance).
Schema for Sections: Each custom section .liquid file should have a {% schema %} at the bottom that defines settings and blocks. Define user-friendly settings for anything the client might tweak:
Text content (like section headings or subtitles) – use a type: text or textarea setting.
Images – type: image_picker.
Select dropdowns for options (e.g., light/dark variant) if needed.
Blocks if the section is repeatable (e.g., a section “Logo Bar” might allow multiple logo images as blocks, or an FAQ section might allow multiple Q&A blocks).
Example schema (simplified) for a Hero section:
{% schema %}
{
  "name": "Hero Banner",
  "settings": [
    { "id": "background_image", "type": "image_picker", "label": "Background Image" },
    { "id": "heading_text", "type": "text", "label": "Heading Text", "default": "Welcome to Lillier" },
    { "id": "subtext", "type": "textarea", "label": "Subheading Text", "default": "Curated marketplace for Black-owned products." },
    { "id": "cta_label", "type": "text", "label": "Button Label", "default": "Shop Now" },
    { "id": "cta_link", "type": "url", "label": "Button Link", "default": "/collections/all" }
  ]
}
{% endschema %}
In the section HTML above this schema, you’d output these settings with Liquid, e.g. <h1>{{ section.settings.heading_text }}</h1> and a button with {{ section.settings.cta_label }}. This makes the hero content easily editable in the Theme Editor sidebar. Do similar for each custom section.
Third-Party Integrations (Shopify): Consider any apps or external code needed:
Analytics (Google Analytics, Facebook Pixel) – Shopify allows adding these via admin (Preferences). If using GA4, you might need to paste the GA4 snippet into theme.liquid head. We’ll cover more in SEO/analytics section.
Review app if needed (for product reviews, Shopify has a free app that embeds stars and forms; if part of site’s plan, integrate and ensure it fits design).
If any special functionality like a wishlist or loyalty program is needed for “repeat use”, plan to integrate an app (and possibly surface it as an icon in header). These typically inject code via script tags or require a snippet in theme – follow the app’s instructions and test styling integration.
Quality Assurance: Use Shopify’s preview and inspector. Check that Liquid logic covers edge cases (e.g., if a setting is left blank, does the section hide gracefully?). Check for translations or multi-currency if relevant (Shopify has those capabilities if expansion is planned). Also test the site in various browsers – ensure no layout shifts or console errors from your JS.
4.6 Performance Optimizations (Shopify): A fast site is important for user experience and SEO:
Dawn is quite optimized out of the box, with lazy loading images and minimal JS. Ensure that any custom images you add (like in hero sections) use Shopify’s built-in image filters to resize appropriately (e.g., use image_url with width params as shown above) so you’re not loading huge images on mobile unnecessarily.
Remove any unnecessary JavaScript from Dawn that you don’t need. For example, if you removed a slideshow section but Dawn’s JS still initializes it, you can safely remove that part from theme.js to lighten the load.
Use Shopify’s theme analyzer or Lighthouse to check performance. Aim to keep LCP low by having optimized images and not too many blocking scripts.
Leverage Shopify CDN for all assets (CSS/JS are served from Shopify, which is fine; any custom library you use, try to also host it or use a fast CDN link).
If you have long pages (like maybe a long editorial article), consider enabling built-in lazy loading for images (just add loading="lazy" if not already present via Liquid).
Mobile optimizations: on mobile, ensure tap targets (buttons, links) are appropriately sized. Also double-check that any custom fonts you use are loaded efficiently (preferably via Shopify’s font library or a CDN, and preconnect to the font source in <head> if external).
5. SEO, Analytics, and Performance Strategy
Both sites should be optimized for search engines, have analytics tracking, and deliver fast performance. Key considerations for each: SEO Best Practices (Both Sites):
Semantic HTML: Ensure that the structure of pages uses proper headings (<h1> for the page title, <h2> for sections, etc.) and that any custom code does not break this hierarchy. On Squarespace, much of this is handled by the template (e.g., page title is an <h1> by default). In custom code blocks, use semantic tags (e.g., <section>, <article> for blog posts, etc.) to improve SEO and accessibility.
Meta Tags: Squarespace allows editing page title and description in page settings – do this for each major page (especially the homepage, with a strong meta description containing keywords about education platform for Black/Brown/queer entrepreneurs). Shopify will automatically use the product or page title and description for meta tags, but you can customize them in the Shopify admin for each page/product (there’s an SEO section for each resource). Double-check that your Shopify theme outputs the {{ page.meta_description }} and other SEO tags in <head> (Dawn typically does) and include og: Open Graph tags and twitter:card tags for social sharing. If not present, add them in theme.liquid using Liquid tags for title, description, product images, etc.
URL Structure: Use clean, descriptive URLs. Squarespace generates URLs from page titles – choose short but meaningful page names. For blog posts or courses, use the collection settings to define a proper URL format (e.g., /blog/post-title). In Shopify, organize products into collections and avoid deep nesting in navigation to keep URLs user-friendly. Both platforms produce a sitemap (Squarespace auto-generates one at yoursite.com/sitemap.xml, Shopify at /sitemap.xml) – submit these to Google Search Console when live.
Structured Data: Shopify’s Dawn includes JSON-LD structured data for products (for rich results like price, availability). If you significantly altered product templates, ensure to keep that snippet (often in snippets/product-meta.liquid or integrated into theme.liquid). For Squarespace, you might rely on its built-in SEO (which might not output JSON-LD for content pages). If C+B has articles, consider adding an Article schema JSON-LD via code injection on blog posts pages for rich snippets (optional but nice). Ensure each image has alt text (client should be advised to add alt text in Squarespace image blocks; in Shopify, alt text is added per image in product media).
Content Strategy: Although not purely development, note that fresh content (blog posts on C+B, perhaps a “Journal” on Lillier highlighting Black-owned brands) will aid SEO. The dev role here is to ensure the blog is set up and easily usable. Encourage the client to use these sections.
Analytics Integration:
Google Analytics (GA): Both platforms support adding GA. For Squarespace, you can input a Google Analytics Tracking ID in Settings, or if using GA4, you might need to add the GA4 snippet via Code Injection (Squarespace’s settings may still only have UA field, so inserting the GA4 <script> in header injection is a way
). On Shopify, in Online Store > Preferences, there is a field for Google Analytics (including a checkbox for using GA4 now, or you can paste the GA4 global site tag). Use that for basic page tracking. If e-commerce tracking is desired, Shopify can also send purchase data to GA (enable “Use Enhanced eCommerce” in Shopify’s preferences and in GA settings).
Additional Analytics/Tracking: If the client needs Facebook Pixel, TikTok Pixel, etc., insert those via code injection on Squarespace and via Shopify’s preferences or theme code. Shopify’s preferences allow a Facebook Pixel ID natively. For other scripts, you can paste them into the theme.liquid just above </head> or in the Google Tag Manager (GTM) field if using GTM.
Testing analytics: After adding, use GA’s real-time reports or Pixel Helper extensions to ensure data is flowing. Also, for membership conversions on C+B, you might want to track sign-ups: with Outseta, you might rely on Outseta’s internal metrics, but you could fire a GA event when the signup modal is confirmed (if Outseta provides a JS callback/hook, you can use that in your custom JS to trigger gtag('event', 'signup', ...)). For Lillier, Shopify will handle purchase conversion tracking if GA is set up.
Performance and SEO (Combined Consideration):
Fast Loading: Search ranking benefits from fast sites. We already discussed optimizing theme code and media. Also implement caching where possible: Squarespace pages are served via their CDN – just avoid heavy client-side rendering. Shopify pages are mostly server-rendered and cached via CDN as well. Keep custom JavaScript lightweight to not hurt Time-to-Interactive.
Image Optimization: Both Squarespace and Shopify will serve images in optimized ways if used correctly. In Squarespace, when you upload images to an Image Block or Gallery, it will generate responsive versions. If you use a custom code <img> (e.g., in a code block for the hero), use the Squarespace responsive image URLs (you can get the URL of an uploaded image and add ?format=1500w for example to get a specific width). On Shopify, always use the | image_url filter with size parameters or | image_tag which includes srcset by default for responsive images. This ensures mobile gets smaller images.
Lazy Loading & Priority: For off-screen images (like further down the homepage), use lazy loading. Shopify does this out of the box for product grids. For any custom code images, add loading="lazy" attribute. For Squarespace, images in code blocks won’t lazy load by default, so you might consider a JS IntersectionObserver to load them or just accept initial load if few. Prioritize above-the-fold content in your HTML output order (especially on Shopify: e.g., hero text should be in HTML, not solely in an image).
SEO Audits: Before launch, run an SEO audit tool or Lighthouse. Check for any missing meta tags, broken links, etc. Also ensure each page has only one H1, alt attributes on all images, and so on.
Accessibility: This goes hand-in-hand with SEO and is important for the audience. Use accessible markup (labels on forms, aria labels if needed for icons, sufficient color contrast between text and background as per WCAG). Test with screen readers or accessibility checkers (Shopify’s theme analyzer, Chrome’s Lighthouse accessibility tests). This will benefit all users and ensure the sites are inclusive.
6. Handoff Strategy and Client Editability
A smooth handoff ensures the client team can maintain and grow the sites without breaking the design. Both Squarespace and Shopify are chosen for their user-friendly content management, so we need to maximize that strength: Editable Components on Squarespace (C+B):
After building the C+B site, prepare a short training for the client on using Squarespace’s editor. Highlight how they can add new pages or edit text without touching code. For example, if the homepage hero text is in a Code Block (HTML), the client would need to edit it in code view – which might be intimidating. To mitigate this, consider alternatives:
Could the hero text be a normal Squarespace Text Block overlaid on a banner? If not, and code is the only way, provide clear comments in the Code Block HTML like <!-- Hero Heading start --> and instructions in documentation on what to change.
Perhaps simpler: use Squarespace’s built-in Banner image and title for the page if it can achieve a similar effect, then you wouldn’t need custom code for just text. Custom code can then be limited to things that don’t require frequent changes.
Member Content Management: If using Member Areas, show the client how to tag pages as members-only and how to manage member lists. If using Outseta, train them on using Outseta’s dashboard (checking subscriptions, etc.) and emphasize that content gating rules are mostly set-and-forget in the code you added.
Adding Blog Posts/Articles: Ensure the blog is configured. Show them how to add a new post, and note that your CSS will automatically format it in the designed style. If there are any special classes they need to use (e.g., a CSS class to style a pull-quote in an article), document this in a guide. For instance, “to highlight a quote, wrap it in a <span class="pullquote"> via the code editor in the post body”.
Adding New Pages: If they want to add a new public page, they can do so through Squarespace normally. Your global CSS will style headings, paragraphs, etc., to match the design. If they need a fancy layout on that page, they have two options: use built-in sections/blocks (which your global styles have refined), or copy an existing Code Block from another page. Provide a few “template” code snippets in documentation – for example, give them a ready-made code for a two-column image/text section they can paste if needed.
Limitations: Caution the client that moving to Squarespace 7.1 meant we integrated custom code that should remain in place. For example, advise them not to disable code injection and not to remove certain code blocks that are critical. If they do, functionality could break. Mark important code blocks in the Squarespace editor with an HTML comment like <!-- Do not remove: custom hero section start --> so even if someone opens it later, they know it’s not default content.
Documentation: Provide a short guide (1-2 pages) listing the custom code pieces and their purpose, and simple instructions for common tasks (e.g., “To update the homepage hero image, go to Home -> Edit -> find the Code Block at top -> replace the image URL within <img src='...'> or contact developer if unsure”). Also mention any third-party accounts (Outseta login for membership management, etc.) the client will need to maintain.
Editable Components on Shopify (Lillier):
Theme Editor Familiarization: The client should be introduced to Shopify’s Theme Editor, where they can modify the content of the homepage and other section-enabled pages easily. Show them how to add a new section on the homepage, move sections up and down, and edit section settings (like changing text or images using the sidebar controls you built). Emphasize that Online Store 2.0 allows these changes without code
.
Custom Sections Usage: Document each custom section you created:
What it’s for (e.g., “Featured Article Section – to display a blog post excerpt on the homepage”).
Which settings it has (e.g., “you can select which blog article to feature by choosing a tag in the section settings” or however you coded it).
If some sections allow blocks (e.g., an FAQ section with Q&A blocks), explain how to add and reorder those blocks in the editor.
Adding Products/Collections: Provide guidance on managing the store: adding new products, creating collections, and how those appear on the site. For example, if the homepage features the “New Arrivals” collection automatically, let them know updating that collection’s products will update the homepage. If certain collections are used in sections or navigation, ensure they know how to update those references. (You might have set collection handles directly in Liquid if hardcoded, but ideally you used settings to pick them, which they can change.)
Blog Posts on Shopify: If Lillier includes an editorial blog, show how to add a blog post in Shopify and how to get it to show up on site (likely a section on some page). If it’s automatically showing latest, no action needed beyond adding the post. If there’s a curated “editorial picks” section, instruct how to flag or select posts to appear (could be by tag or manual selection in a section).
Menus and Navigation: The header and footer menus should be manageable via Shopify Navigation settings. Ensure any links are not hardcoded in theme (except maybe special ones like account or cart icons). So the client can add a new menu item (e.g., adding a new collection to main menu) via Shopify admin. Test this: if they do add an item, does the header design accommodate it (might need to limit number of items visually)? Document how to do it and any recommendations (like “Stick to short menu labels to maintain the clean look”).
Apps Management: If you installed any Shopify apps (for reviews, wishlist, etc.), let them know how those function. E.g., “We installed Shopify Product Reviews app – you can moderate reviews in the Shopify admin under Apps > Product Reviews. The theme will display reviews stars on product pages automatically if any exist.” This helps them maintain those features.
No-Code Customizations: Encourage the client to use theme settings for minor style tweaks rather than editing code. For instance, if they want to change a font or color later, they can go to Theme Settings and do so (assuming we exposed those settings). If something more drastic is needed, they can duplicate the theme and try edits or hire a dev – but on day-to-day, the theme editor suffices. Reassure them that adding new content (products, pages, blog posts) is intended to be code-free.
Final Handoff Checklist:
Access and Ownership: On Squarespace, transfer the site ownership or ensure the client’s account has proper roles to edit content and code injection (they’ll need a Business plan to retain code injection abilities). On Shopify, transfer the dev store to the client’s account (if you built it under a Partner account, use the Transfer ownership feature). This will prompt them to pick a plan.
Testing with Client: Walk through key tasks with the client (possibly a live demo): e.g., add a dummy blog post on C+B, change a hero image on Lillier, go through the purchase flow on Lillier to show orders coming into Shopify admin, and a membership sign-up on C+B to show how a new member is recorded.
Post-launch Support: Mention that you’ll be available for a stabilizing period to fix any issues that come up (like if any custom code has an unforeseen bug or if the client is unsure how to do something). Also note that for Shopify, updating the theme in the future will be manual (since it’s custom, they won’t click “upgrade” like an app, they’d integrate changes by code). Advise them to refrain from editing theme code directly without knowledge; instead, use the customization options provided or consult a developer for changes.
By following this plan, we ensure both Cupcakes+Broccoli and Lillier are built with robust custom code integrated into their respective platforms in a maintainable way. The design system keeps them visually cohesive, each platform’s strengths are utilized (Squarespace for content/membership, Shopify for commerce), and the client will be empowered to manage content and pages easily after handoff. Both sites will be ready for expansion – whether adding more courses on C+B or more products on Lillier – without needing significant redevelopment, thanks to the flexible, section-based architecture and solid groundwork. Yes — the clean way to “build once, ship twice” here is to treat Squarespace + Shopify as two renderers sitting on top of a shared design system + shared front-end layer (tokens, CSS, JS behaviors, component specs). That directly matches the brief: experiential cohesion, not platform unification

, while still delivering a shared design system

 and CMS-driven builds

Below is a practical setup that avoids double work.

What you can realistically share (and what you can’t)
Share (this is where you save the time)
Design tokens (colors, type scale, spacing, radii) → generate both CSS variables and LESS variables.

Base CSS + component CSS (buttons, cards, nav, grids, typography rhythm) aligned to the “editorial / minimal / mobile-first” constraints 
CnB and Lillier Website Brief_J…

 
CnB and Lillier Website Brief_J…

.

JS behaviors (nav drawer, tabs, accordions, modal, sticky header, search UI patterns). Same JS runs on both sites.

Component “specs” (a neutral template for each component) → output to:

Shopify: Liquid snippets/sections

Squarespace: HTML fragments for Code Blocks + CSS that styles native blocks

Can’t fully share
Shopify’s data templating (Liquid loops over products/collections/metafields) has no equivalent in Squarespace.

Squarespace’s editor-native block markup is not the same as Shopify’s sections.

So: you share the system + presentation layer, then do light mapping per platform.

The monorepo pattern that works
Create one repo with a shared package and two “apps”:

repo/
  packages/
    design-system/
      tokens/
        tokens.json
      src/
        tokens.css        # generated
        tokens.less       # generated
        base.css
        components/
          button.css
          card.css
          nav.css
        behaviors/
          nav.js
          tabs.js
          accordions.js
      build/
        build.mjs         # generates outputs
  apps/
    shopify-theme/        # Dawn-based theme
      assets/
      sections/
      snippets/
      layout/theme.liquid
    squarespace/
      snippets/           # HTML fragments to paste into Code Blocks
      injection/
        header.html       # what you paste into SS header injection
        footer.html       # what you paste into SS footer injection
Output strategy (key idea)
Build produces 2 bundles from the same source:

dist/cb.css + dist/cb.js (Cupcakes+Broccoli “theme variant”)

dist/lillier.css + dist/lillier.js (Lillier “theme variant”)

Both are 90% identical; only a small override layer differs.

Step 1 — Make the shared design tokens the single source of truth
packages/design-system/tokens/tokens.json (example)

{
  "color": {
    "bg": "#ffffff",
    "text": "#0b0b0b",
    "muted": "#6b6b6b",
    "border": "#e7e7e7",
    "accent": "#ff2d55"
  },
  "space": { "1": "4px", "2": "8px", "3": "12px", "4": "16px", "6": "24px", "8": "32px", "12": "48px" },
  "radius": { "sm": "8px", "md": "14px", "lg": "22px" },
  "type": {
    "fontBody": "system-ui, -apple-system, Segoe UI, Roboto, Arial",
    "fontDisplay": "system-ui, -apple-system, Segoe UI, Roboto, Arial",
    "sizeBase": "16px",
    "lhBase": "1.45"
  }
}
Then create two tiny brand override files:

brands/cb.json and brands/lillier.json that only override color.accent, maybe fontDisplay, etc.

This supports the “shared DNA but different usage patterns” requirement 
CnB and Lillier Website Brief_J…

.

Step 2 — Generate CSS variables + LESS from tokens
In build/build.mjs, generate:

dist/tokens.css:

:root { --color-bg: ...; --space-4: ... }

dist/tokens.less:

@color-bg: ...; @space-4: ...

Why: Squarespace Custom CSS supports LESS variables; Shopify is happier with CSS variables.

Step 3 — Build shared CSS once, then “theme” it twice
Structure:

shared.css imports tokens + base + components

cb.css = shared.css + cb-overrides.css

lillier.css = shared.css + lillier-overrides.css

Example:

/* shared.css */
@import "./tokens.css";
@import "./base.css";
@import "./components/button.css";
@import "./components/card.css";
@import "./components/nav.css";
/* cb-overrides.css */
:root { --color-accent: #ff2d55; }
/* lillier-overrides.css */
:root { --color-accent: #5a2cff; }
Now both sites automatically stay aligned to the same editorial rhythm and mobile cleanliness 
CnB and Lillier Website Brief_J…

.

Step 4 — Make JS behaviors shared + “DOM-adapter” per platform
Write JS that expects stable, your attributes:

data-cnb-nav-toggle

data-cnb-accordion

data-cnb-tabs

Then add a tiny adapter that “finds” Squarespace/Shopify elements and adds those attributes/classes.

Example: adapter pattern
// behaviors/adapter.js
export function adaptSquarespace() {
  // Example: turn Squarespace button blocks into your button class
  document.querySelectorAll('.sqs-block-button-element').forEach(btn => {
    btn.classList.add('cnb-btn');
  });
}

export function adaptShopify() {
  // Example: ensure Shopify add-to-cart button gets your class
  document.querySelectorAll('form[action*="/cart/add"] button[type="submit"]').forEach(btn => {
    btn.classList.add('cnb-btn');
  });
}
In your entry:

import { adaptSquarespace, adaptShopify } from './adapter.js';
import { initNav } from './nav.js';
import { initAccordions } from './accordions.js';

export function boot(brand) {
  if (brand === 'cb') adaptSquarespace();
  if (brand === 'lillier') adaptShopify();
  initNav();
  initAccordions();
}
This is the big unlock: you keep one JS codebase, only the adapters differ.

Step 5 — Shipping the same built assets into both platforms
Shopify (Lillier): copy build outputs into theme assets
Your build script copies:

dist/lillier.css → apps/shopify-theme/assets/lillier.css

dist/lillier.js → apps/shopify-theme/assets/lillier.js

Then in apps/shopify-theme/layout/theme.liquid, add these (exactly where Dawn normally loads CSS/JS):

<head>
  {{ 'base.css' | asset_url | stylesheet_tag }}
  {{ 'lillier.css' | asset_url | stylesheet_tag }}
</head>
<body class="brand-lillier">
  ...
  {{ 'lillier.js' | asset_url | script_tag }}
</body>
Squarespace (C+B): load the built assets via injection
Squarespace is easiest if you host the built files somewhere you control (Cloudflare Pages, Netlify, S3+CloudFront). Then you paste just references into Squarespace Code Injection.

Header injection (so CSS vars apply before paint):

<script>document.documentElement.classList.add('brand-cb');</script>
<link rel="stylesheet" href="https://your-cdn.example.com/cb.css">
Footer injection:

<script src="https://your-cdn.example.com/cb.js" defer></script>
<script>
  window.CNB && window.CNB.boot && window.CNB.boot('cb');
</script>
This gives you true single-source deployment: rebuild once → both sites update.

If the client insists on “no external hosting,” you can still use one codebase, but your last step becomes:

copy/paste cb.css into Squarespace Custom CSS (manual),

keep Shopify automated.
You still avoid double development work; only publishing is manual.

Step 6 — “Component once” workflow that still stays client-editable
Because the client must be able to add/edit pages easily 
CnB and Lillier Website Brief_J…

, your shared system should prioritize:

Shopify
Components become Sections and Snippets (editable via Theme Editor).

Squarespace
Components become (a) native blocks styled by your CSS + adapter, or (b) small Code Blocks for only the pieces Squarespace can’t do.

A good rule:

80%: style native Squarespace blocks (text, image, buttons, summaries) so the client edits without code.

20%: code blocks for unique layouts (hero, custom AI tool UI).

This supports “CMS-driven builds” and “long-term scalability” 
CnB and Lillier Website Brief_J…

 without sacrificing your custom-code preference.

Step 7 — Keep “shared form” without copying templates: use a mapping sheet
Create a simple internal mapping like:

Hero

Shopify: sections/hero.liquid

Squarespace: Section with Image + Text blocks OR code snippet squarespace/snippets/hero.html

Card Grid

Shopify: collection loop snippet

Squarespace: Summary Block styled as .cnb-grid

Editorial Article List

Shopify: blog section

Squarespace: Blog list page styled

This is how you stay aligned to “shared design system, different intents” 
CnB and Lillier Website Brief_J…

 without trying to force identical templating.

The day-to-day dev loop (fast + simultaneous)
Run these in parallel:

Design-system watch build

rebuilds CSS/JS on change

copies Lillier outputs into Shopify theme assets

rebuilds C+B outputs to CDN folder (or just dist folder)

Shopify CLI dev server

shopify theme dev for live theme preview

Squarespace staging site

you don’t get local runtime, so you iterate by:

using a local “playground” HTML page to validate CSS/JS fast

pushing updates to CDN

refreshing the staging Squarespace site

This gives you one codebase and one “UI truth,” with Shopify + Squarespace as delivery targets.

That fits Lillier’s intent (“daily, one-stop-shop marketplace… transactional e-commerce… designed for scale”)

CnB and Lillier Website Brief_J…

.

There are only a few realistic ways to do it without pain. Here’s the elegant architecture I’d push, with a phased rollout that keeps complexity under control.

The lowest-headache “best case”: all sellers are Shopify stores → Shopify Collective
If the sellers are already on Shopify (or can move there), Shopify Collective is almost literally built for this:

You import products from supplier Shopify stores into your Shopify store 

Inventory syncs in real time across connected stores 

When a customer checks out, the order is automatically split and routed to the relevant supplier stores 

Suppliers fulfill + ship direct to the customer, and tracking syncs back to your store 

Payments between retailer/supplier are handled within the system 

It’s available only for supported countries/currencies + eligibility rules 

Why this is “minimum headache”: it avoids custom inventory sync, custom order splitting, and custom vendor payout logic because Shopify already orchestrates it. You’re mainly doing curation + UX + merchandising, which aligns with “Amazon built by someone with taste” 
CnB and Lillier Website Brief_J…

.

Catch: Collective only works with Shopify-to-Shopify suppliers (and eligibility). So you need a plan for the non-Shopify long tail.

The “mixed world” solution (most realistic): Two-tier supplier model
You keep one Shopify storefront, but you support suppliers via two integration tracks:

Track A — Shopify suppliers (preferred)
Onboard them via Shopify Collective (fastest, lowest ops) 

Track B — Non-Shopify suppliers (long tail)
Use a lightweight order/inventory hub to normalize data and route orders. The cleanest low-code hub I’ve seen for this pattern is Order Desk:

It can import Shopify orders and split by vendor 

It can route each split order to a vendor via rules (email/API style flows) 

This creates a simple “vendor integration contract”:

Either connect via Collective (Shopify vendors)

Or provide a feed/API that Order Desk can ingest, and accept orders via email/API workflow

This hybrid approach is elegant because you don’t build 1,000 custom integrations. You build one vendor onboarding pipeline with two supported paths.

How to make “all inventory present” without losing your mind
You need a strict data model + onboarding rules, otherwise 1,000 catalogs becomes chaos.

1) Normalize every product into a single schema
Minimum required fields (enforced):

vendor_id (internal)

vendor_name (maps to Shopify “Vendor” field)

vendor_sku and a normalized SKU (recommend prefixing: V123-<vendor_sku>)

title, images, price, compare_at (optional), category/tags

fulfillment_profile (lead time, shipping regions, carrier rules)

return_policy_code

2) Choose your “source of truth” for inventory
Collective suppliers: supplier store is truth; inventory sync is handled 

Non-Shopify suppliers: pick one:

Vendor is truth → they push inventory updates (feed/API), you mirror into Shopify on schedule

Your hub is truth → Order Desk holds inventory + availability rules, you keep Shopify quantities conservative to prevent oversell

For “minimum headache,” I’d do Vendor-is-truth with scheduled updates.

3) Import products into Shopify
Collective: import inside Shopify (easy) 

Non-Shopify: bulk import (CSV) or Admin API (later), but enforce SKU rules so you never collide.

How to make “orders placed on Lillier go to the right seller(s)”
There are two practical routing patterns:

Pattern 1 — Use “already built” routing (simplest)
Collective handles routing for Shopify suppliers automatically 

Order Desk splits Shopify orders by vendor and routes them 

That’s the lowest headache stack.

Pattern 2 — Custom routing app (only if you must)
Shopify’s fulfillment architecture supports splitting by line items and quantities:

Fulfillment orders represent groups of items to be fulfilled from the same location 

There’s an Admin GraphQL mutation to split fulfillment orders (fulfillmentOrderSplit) 

Shopify can also split shipments automatically across fulfillment locations in some cases 

But: doing “one vendor = one Shopify location” doesn’t scale well to 1,000 vendors. So if you custom-build, you typically:

store vendor routing in metafields

listen to orders/create webhooks

create “sub-orders” in your own DB

notify vendors + collect tracking

update fulfillments back into Shopify via Admin API

This is doable, but it’s more headache than Order Desk unless you’re building a serious marketplace platform.

Recommended “elegant” rollout plan (keeps complexity contained)
Phase 1: Launch with Shopify-only suppliers (fast, polished)
Build Lillier’s UX, taxonomy, search, editorial merchandising

Onboard initial brands via Collective so inventory/routing is automated 

You get multi-brand checkout with order routing, minimal ops 

Phase 2: Add the long tail via a hub (Order Desk)
Define vendor integration contract (feed format + order notification format)

Implement “Split by Vendor” in the hub and route orders 

Start with the simplest fulfillment comms (vendor emails + portal link), then API later

Phase 3: If scale demands it, swap the hub for a heavier OMS (optional)
If she actually gets to hundreds/thousands of active sellers with real volume, you may outgrow lightweight routing and need a more formal OMS/orchestration layer (Pipe17 positions itself as order ops orchestration) 
. Only do this when volume justifies it.

If she insists on a “true marketplace” (vendors log in, manage inventory, payouts)
Then you’re in multi-vendor marketplace app territory on Shopify (vendors manage listings inside your store). Examples:

Webkul MultiVendor Marketplace (vendor tooling, payouts, integrations) 

Puppet Vendors (vendor portal + commissions/payouts) 

Garnet Multivendor Marketplace (split orders/payouts) 

This can work, but it’s a different operating model: vendors are “tenants” inside your Shopify instance, and you still have the complexity of tax/shipping/returns/payout operations.

The single most important decision (that determines headache level)
Before building anything, lock one policy choice:

Are you the merchant of record (customer pays you, you pay vendors), or are you just referring customers out?

If she wants “one checkout,” you’re basically merchant of record (Collective automates the supplier payment flow in that specific model) 

If she’s flexible, the absolute minimum headache is “curated catalog + link out,” but that’s not what she asked for.

If you tell me which world she’s actually in (even roughly):

“Most sellers are on Shopify already” vs “Most are random sites,” and

“She wants one checkout no matter what” vs “She’s open to link-outs for some vendors,”

…I’ll give you a concrete blueprint with:

exact data model (metafields/tags),

vendor onboarding flow,

the operational playbook (inventory cadence, tracking capture),

and either the Collective-first implementation checklist or the Order Desk hub configuration plan.

You said:
Does shopify collective require the other vendors to do anything? Or can we just pull in their inventory from our end? That's all we want, is for their inventory to be searchable, sortable, shoppable as part of our own page's collection. Without them having to opt in.
ChatGPT said:

Thought for 12s
No — you can’t just “pull in” other vendors’ inventory into Shopify Collective unilaterally.

What Shopify Collective actually requires
To use Collective, the supplier side has to participate in some way:

A supplier must connect with you (either they invite you, or you invite them and they accept). 

Suppliers share products via price lists (private or public), and retailers import from those lists. 

Shopify explicitly frames it as “stores on Shopify team up,” starting with an invitation to connect and a price list. 

Is there any way to import without supplier approval?
Only in one limited scenario: public price lists.

Shopify notes that some suppliers create public price lists that let eligible retailers “instantly import products… without having to connect with or be approved by the supplier.” 

But that still requires the supplier to:

be on Shopify,

be using Collective,

and intentionally publish a public price list.

It’s not a “scrape any store” feature, and it won’t help with “a thousand independent seller sites” unless they’re already opted into Collective.

If your real requirement is “searchable + shoppable catalog without vendor opt-in”
Then Collective is the wrong tool. You’re describing one of these:

Option A — Affiliate/Referral marketplace (lowest headache)
You index product listings from independent sites (manually, feeds, or limited crawling)

“Add to cart” sends users to the vendor site (or opens vendor checkout)

You earn affiliate commission (where available)

✅ Minimal operations, no order routing, no returns/payout headaches
❌ Not a single checkout; less “owned” commerce experience

Option B — True marketplace with vendor onboarding (still reasonable)
You do need vendor consent, but you make it painless:

Vendor provides a feed (CSV/Google Sheet/API) OR connects their Shopify

Your store imports the catalog and routes orders back to them

This is where Shopify Collective (for Shopify vendors) + an order-routing hub (for non-Shopify vendors) becomes the “minimum headache” architecture.

Option C — Build your own inventory/order aggregator (highest headache)
You create integrations per vendor (feeds, APIs)

You create order splitting + vendor notifications + tracking ingestion

You manage payment splits / vendor payouts

This is doable, but it’s the opposite of “minimum headache.”

Practical recommendation you can tell the client
If she wants shoppable inventory inside her Shopify with “place order on my site”:

Require opt-in via one of two vendor paths:

Shopify vendors: join via Collective invitation / signup link 

Non-Shopify vendors: provide a simple product feed + accept orders via a standardized process

If she insists on no vendor opt-in, the honest answer is:

you can only do an index + link-out model, not true “checkout on our site,” without creating legal/ops chaos.

If you tell me which of these she’ll accept:

single checkout is mandatory vs link-outs acceptable, and

how many vendors are already on Shopify,

…I’ll lay out the cleanest implementation path (vendor onboarding contract + catalog structure + how you keep it searchable/sortable on Shopify without killing yourself operationally).

You said:
Ok assuming we have the shopify site URL but not the contact info for a few hundred shopify sellers: how do we automate the discovery of their contact info? We cannot afford the time to look up each one individually.
ChatGPT said:

Thought for 1m 37s
You can’t reliably “discover” contact info that a merchant hasn’t made public. What you can automate (legitimately) is: extract whatever business contact + social links they already publish, at scale, from the handful of Shopify pages that are very often present.

Here’s the lowest-headache approach I’d use.

1) First choice: use Shopify Collective’s built-in Discovery (when possible)
If you’re eligible for Shopify Collective as a retailer, Collective has a Discovery feature to find suppliers and it explicitly exposes “key supplier details… and contact information more easily” (Shopify changelog). 

How to leverage this with only store URLs:

For each store URL, programmatically fetch the homepage title/brand name.

Use that name to search in Apps → Collective → Discovery. 

When it matches, you’ll often get the contact/social details right there. 

This avoids scraping and tends to be cleaner if the supplier participates in Collective.

2) The practical automation: crawl the “standard Shopify pages” and extract public contact info
Shopify stores commonly publish contact details in:

a contact page (often .../pages/contact) (you’ll see Shopify’s own example thank-you URL format using /pages/contact?...). 

policy pages that have standardized URLs Shopify documents (privacy/terms/refund/shipping). 

So your automation pipeline is:

Step A — For each store domain, try these URLs (in order)
/pages/contact and /contact 

/policies/privacy-policy

/policies/terms-of-service

/policies/refund-policy

/policies/shipping-policy 

Step B — Parse the HTML for:
mailto: links

visible emails (regex)

phone patterns

“sameAs” social links in JSON-LD (application/ld+json) (Instagram is often the fastest path to reach them)

the actual contact page URL if it’s linked in the footer/nav

Step C — Output a CSV with:
store domain

emails found (if any)

phone (if any)

contact page found (URL)

policy pages found (Y/N)

instagram/facebook/tiktok links

status (ok / blocked / none found)

Guardrails (important)
Only collect what’s publicly displayed on their site.

Rate-limit hard (don’t hammer sites) and respect robots.txt where possible.

Expect a chunk to be “no public email” (many merchants use forms only).

3) A ready-to-run script (public-info extraction only)
Create a file discover_contacts.py and run it against a text file of URLs.

urls.txt
https://example1.com
https://example2.com
...
discover_contacts.py
import re, csv, time
from urllib.parse import urljoin, urlparse

import requests
from bs4 import BeautifulSoup

EMAIL_RE = re.compile(r"[A-Z0-9._%+-]+@[A-Z0-9.-]+\.[A-Z]{2,}", re.I)
PHONE_RE = re.compile(r"(\+?\d[\d\-\(\)\s]{7,}\d)")

CANDIDATE_PATHS = [
    "/pages/contact",
    "/contact",
    "/policies/privacy-policy",
    "/policies/terms-of-service",
    "/policies/refund-policy",
    "/policies/shipping-policy",
]

def norm_base(url: str) -> str:
    if not url.startswith(("http://", "https://")):
        url = "https://" + url
    parsed = urlparse(url)
    return f"{parsed.scheme}://{parsed.netloc}"

def fetch(session: requests.Session, url: str) -> str | None:
    try:
        r = session.get(url, timeout=15, allow_redirects=True)
        if r.status_code >= 400:
            return None
        return r.text
    except requests.RequestException:
        return None

def extract_links_and_jsonld(soup: BeautifulSoup):
    links = [a.get("href") for a in soup.select("a[href]") if a.get("href")]
    jsonlds = []
    for tag in soup.select('script[type="application/ld+json"]'):
        if tag.string:
            jsonlds.append(tag.string)
    return links, jsonlds

def find_social(links):
    socials = {"instagram": "", "facebook": "", "tiktok": "", "x": ""}
    for href in links:
        h = href.lower()
        if "instagram.com" in h and not socials["instagram"]:
            socials["instagram"] = href
        elif "facebook.com" in h and not socials["facebook"]:
            socials["facebook"] = href
        elif "tiktok.com" in h and not socials["tiktok"]:
            socials["tiktok"] = href
        elif ("twitter.com" in h or "x.com" in h) and not socials["x"]:
            socials["x"] = href
    return socials

def parse_page(html: str):
    soup = BeautifulSoup(html, "html.parser")
    text = soup.get_text(" ", strip=True)

    emails = set(m.group(0) for m in EMAIL_RE.finditer(html))
    # Sometimes emails only appear in visible text:
    emails |= set(m.group(0) for m in EMAIL_RE.finditer(text))

    phones = set(m.group(0) for m in PHONE_RE.finditer(text))

    links, jsonlds = extract_links_and_jsonld(soup)
    socials = find_social(links)

    # If there’s a contact link somewhere, capture it
    contact_link = ""
    for href in links:
        if href and ("contact" in href.lower()):
            contact_link = href
            break

    return {
        "emails": ";".join(sorted(emails)),
        "phones": ";".join(sorted(phones)),
        "contact_link": contact_link,
        **socials
    }

def main():
    session = requests.Session()
    session.headers.update({
        "User-Agent": "Mozilla/5.0 (compatible; vendor-discovery/1.0; +contact@example.com)"
    })

    with open("urls.txt", "r", encoding="utf-8") as f:
        seeds = [line.strip() for line in f if line.strip()]

    rows = []
    for i, seed in enumerate(seeds, 1):
        base = norm_base(seed)
        row = {
            "domain": urlparse(base).netloc,
            "base": base,
            "status": "",
            "found_on": "",
            "emails": "",
            "phones": "",
            "contact_link": "",
            "instagram": "",
            "facebook": "",
            "tiktok": "",
            "x": ""
        }

        # Try homepage first (footer often has contact)
        html = fetch(session, base)
        if html:
            parsed = parse_page(html)
            for k in parsed:
                row[k] = parsed[k] or row[k]
            if row["emails"] or row["phones"] or row["instagram"] or row["contact_link"]:
                row["found_on"] = base

        # Then try common Shopify paths
        for path in CANDIDATE_PATHS:
            if row["emails"] and row["phones"]:
                break
            url = urljoin(base, path)
            html = fetch(session, url)
            if not html:
                continue
            parsed = parse_page(html)
            for k in parsed:
                row[k] = parsed[k] or row[k]
            if parsed["emails"] or parsed["phones"]:
                row["found_on"] = url
                break

        row["status"] = "ok" if (row["emails"] or row["phones"] or row["instagram"] or row["contact_link"]) else "none_found"
        rows.append(row)

        # polite rate-limit
        time.sleep(1.0)

        if i % 25 == 0:
            print(f"Processed {i}/{len(seeds)}...")

    with open("vendor_contacts.csv", "w", newline="", encoding="utf-8") as out:
        w = csv.DictWriter(out, fieldnames=rows[0].keys())
        w.writeheader()
        w.writerows(rows)

    print("Wrote vendor_contacts.csv")

if __name__ == "__main__":
    main()
This script:

only checks a few very common pages (including Shopify’s standardized policy URLs). 

grabs emails/phones/social links if they’re public

produces vendor_contacts.csv

4) When emails aren’t public (very common): use socials + a single onboarding link
When a store has no published email, your best automation isn’t “find email anyway” — it’s:

pull Instagram / TikTok / Facebook links (often present in footer or JSON-LD)

send a short DM asking them to opt into your supplier program

include one onboarding link (ex: “apply / connect your store”)

If you use Collective, Shopify explicitly supports inviting suppliers, but it still needs an email for an invite flow. 

So socials become your “no-email” channel.

5) Reality check on success rate
With a few hundred Shopify stores, expect something like:

40–70%: some public contact method (email or form or social)

10–30%: only a contact form (no email)

10–20%: minimal info + aggressive bot protection

That’s why “public extraction + social fallback + Collective Discovery where possible” is the clean combo. 

If you want, paste 5–10 example store URLs (just domains, no private info) and I’ll tell you which paths tend to work best for that cohort (some niches lean harder on policy pages; some always have email in the footer).

Here’s how I’d “play it” so you come across as confident, structured, and genuinely collaborative—while quietly protecting you from scope creep.

The strategy
On your reply, you want to do 4 things:

Mirror her modular framing (you’re aligned with how she wants to work).

Define your lane in a way that feels helpful, not defensive.

Ask a small set of high-leverage questions that will instantly reveal scope, dependencies, and timeline risk.

Propose a clean next step: a call agenda + an immediate “Phase 1 blueprint” deliverable you can produce quickly after the call (paid or as part of kickoff).

You don’t need to solve everything by email. Your goal is to set the tone: “I’m calm, I’m organized, I’ll make this real, and I won’t let it become chaos.”

Why this works
