What to do on the call (your “calm operator” script)
Use this structure; it keeps you from getting dragged into vague vision talk.

1) Start: clarify roles in plain language (2 minutes)
“Here’s what I can own end-to-end: platform setup, theme build, component implementation, responsiveness, performance basics, SEO/analytics setup, and an editing workflow your team can run with.”

Then ask:

“What are you expecting your design partner to deliver to me, and when?”

2) Force a Phase 1 definition (10–15 minutes)
For each site, ask:

“What are the must-have pages for launch?”

“What is the must-have functionality for launch?”

“What specifically is Phase 2?”

If she struggles, propose a default:

C+B Phase 1: home + about + classes/resources + subscribe/membership gating + one template page type for new content + email capture + analytics.

Lillier Phase 1: core catalog structure + collection pages + product pages + cart/checkout + search + basic merchandising + analytics.

3) Confirm content readiness (5 minutes)
“What content is locked today?”

“What content is missing and who is producing it?”

“What is the minimum viable photography for launch?”

4) Confirm technical constraints (5 minutes)
“Do you need external hosting for shared CSS/JS or should we keep everything inside platform tools?”

“Any required integrations (email, SMS, payment, shipping, subscriptions tool, CRM)?”

5) Close with a concrete deliverable (2 minutes)
End with:
“After this call, I’ll write a Phase 1 build blueprint: site map, templates, component list, required integrations, and a dependency timeline. That becomes our shared source of truth.”

That blueprint is how you protect yourself.

One line you should use if she hints at the mega marketplace idea

No debate, no deep dive—just contain it.

You said:
How WILL I keep it client-editable? Because I do NOT WANT to be bogged down in manually adjusting visual boxes on a grid, creating by mouse on squarespace for instance. That's fine for the client, but for me - nah.
ChatGPT said:

Thought for 1m 2s
Totally fair. The trick with Squarespace is: you don’t “design” pages by hand. You build a system (global styles + reusable sections + template pages) so you mostly work in code, and the client does the mouse stuff later.

Here’s the cleanest way to do that on Squarespace 7.1 without getting stuck nudging boxes.

The core approach: system-first, template-driven
1) Prefer Classic Editor sections for most content
Squarespace 7.1 lets you use the classic editor instead of Fluid Engine, and you can also add blank classic editor sections. 

This is your best defense against “manual grid fiddling.”

Rule of thumb:

Use Classic Editor sections for 80–90% of the site (content-forward pages, resources, classes, etc.).

Use Fluid Engine only where you truly need it (a single hero layout, a special collage, etc.), and only after you’ve locked your layout patterns.

2) Make the look-and-feel come from global CSS, not per-page tweaking
You set typography, spacing rhythm, buttons, cards, forms, and image treatments once via:

Site Styles (baseline)

Custom CSS / Code Injection (your real “theme layer”)

That way, the client can keep adding normal Squarespace blocks, and they’ll automatically look “on brand.”

3) Build a “Section Library” and reuse it everywhere
Squarespace supports Saved Sections (notably via Circle accounts) so you can save a section once and reuse it across pages. 

This is the closest thing Squarespace has to “components.”

Your workflow becomes:

Build 12–20 canonical sections once (hero, two-column text+image, card grid, quote band, CTA band, FAQ accordion, etc.).

Save them.

Assemble pages by inserting saved sections, not by designing from scratch each time.

4) Build “Template Pages” the client can duplicate
Create hidden pages like:

TEMPLATE – Standard Page

TEMPLATE – Resource Page

TEMPLATE – Gated Resource

TEMPLATE – Join/Subscribe
Then the client edits by duplicating and swapping content.

This gives you the “custom code mindset” even on a no-code CMS: you’re shipping a page system, not one-off pages.

How you avoid manual edits during development
Option A (best, least manual): host your CSS/JS and inject once
Put your compiled cb.css and cb.js on a CDN (Cloudflare Pages / Netlify / S3). Then in Squarespace, you paste one link in Code Injection and you’re done.

You keep iterating in your repo.

Deployment updates the assets.

Squarespace instantly reflects changes without you re-pasting CSS constantly.

This aligns with Squarespace’s supported approach of using code injection + code blocks for custom code. 

Option B: paste CSS into Custom CSS (more manual, still manageable)
If the client doesn’t want external hosting:

You paste the compiled CSS into Design → Custom CSS occasionally.

Still no per-page grid nudging, because the sections/templates carry structure.

What stays client-editable (without you handholding)
Client edits:
Text, images, ordering of sections

Adding a new resource page by duplicating a template

Adding a new page by inserting saved sections

Updating “gated content” by moving pages into the member area / gating rules (Phase 1 is “light membership” anyway)

You control (in code):
Typography scale, spacing system, buttons, cards, nav behavior

Mobile rules, breakpoints, “editorial rhythm”

Any JS behaviors (accordion, sticky header, subtle interactions)

The biggest “gotcha”: Code Blocks are not the answer for everything
Squarespace Code Blocks are great for small, contained custom widgets. 

But don’t build the whole page in HTML inside Code Blocks, because then the client can’t easily edit it later.

Use Code Blocks for things like:

a special “AI tool embed” placeholder (Phase 1 “no heavy custom logic”)

a custom CTA component if Squarespace blocks can’t match

tiny enhancements (not full layouts)

The exact operating rules I’d use so you don’t get trapped
No per-page styling. Everything is global styles + reusable sections.

No “pixel pushing.” Classic sections + consistent spacing utilities only.

Every page must be buildable from templates. If it can’t, it’s not Phase 1.

Client edit workflow is: duplicate template → replace content → publish.

You only touch Squarespace editor to assemble sections, not to design from scratch.

Quick question that decides the workflow
Are you (or the client) a Squarespace Circle member? If yes, saved sections become a superpower (true reusable components). 

If not, you can still do it with “template pages + duplicate,” just slightly less elegant.

If you tell me whether the existing C+B site is 7.1 and whether you can use Classic Editor sections for most pages, I’ll outline a concrete “Section Library” (the exact 15–20 sections to build first) + the exact template pages to create so Phase 1 stays fast and painless.

You said:
Are you saying I can make a custom code sections / blocks /page templates, and then save them as discrete, editable chunks for the client to reuse and reskin easily?
ChatGPT said:
Yes, but with an important caveat: in Squarespace you can save and reuse sections/layouts, not true “custom-coded components” in the way you’d do in React. You can still get 90% of what you want if you treat:

Squarespace blocks/sections = editable content containers

your CSS/JS = the design system + behavior layer

a small amount of HTML in Code Blocks = special widgets only

What you can save and reuse
Saved Sections (reusable chunks you insert on any page)

These are editable after you insert them.

Best for: hero layouts, CTAs, 2-col sections, card grids, FAQs, etc.

Depending on the account/setup, this is often done via Squarespace’s “Saved Sections” feature (many accounts have it; Circle makes it easier/standard).

Template pages you duplicate

You create a page that contains a “canonical” arrangement of sections, then duplicate it whenever you need a new page of that type.

Best for: “Resource page,” “Program page,” “About page,” “Gated resource page,” etc.

Those two together are essentially “page templates,” Squarespace-style.

What you can’t do (not cleanly)
You can’t create a fully custom-coded block type that becomes a first-class Squarespace block with its own UI fields (like a real component with props).

If you put a whole section’s layout in a Code Block as raw HTML, it can look great, but it stops being truly client-editable (they’re editing HTML, not content fields).

The best practical pattern (client-editable + you don’t pixel-push)
1) Build reusable sections using native blocks
You create a section using:

headings, text blocks, buttons, images, summaries/lists, etc.

Then your global CSS makes those blocks look like your “component system.”

Example: You style all Squarespace buttons to match your button component, all summary blocks to match your card grid, etc.

Result: client edits text/images like normal, but it stays on-brand automatically.

2) Add “utility classes” that let you reskin sections quickly
Squarespace lets you add a section “ID” (and in many cases you can add classes via Code Injection patterns). Then your CSS can target:

#section-hero

#section-cta

#section-grid

So you can have variants like:

hero: left-aligned vs centered

card grid: 2-up vs 3-up

CTA: light vs dark

without editing layout each time.

3) Use Code Blocks only for special non-standard bits
If something can’t be built with native blocks (rare), you can use a Code Block and keep it small and isolated.

Examples:

an “AI tool” placeholder widget (Phase 1)

a custom signup embed if needed

a special “collection filter” UI (not common on Squarespace)

How the client “reuses and reskins easily”
Here’s what their workflow looks like:

Add a new page by duplicating a template page.

Insert a Saved Section like “Hero A” or “CTA B.”

Edit text/images in normal block editors.

Choose a section variant by picking from a small “variant menu” you provide (usually: duplicate a variant section, or switch section ID).

Meanwhile, you retain the power:

if you improve the global CSS, the entire site updates.

The Shopify side works similarly, but even better
On Shopify, you can create true reusable components:

Sections + Blocks with editable fields

Theme editor controls

Snippets for shared UI

So your “build once” mindset is more natural there. Squarespace is “design system + saved layout” rather than “true component library.”

A concrete example of a “reusable section” that stays client-editable
You build a “Card Grid” section using a Squarespace Summary Block (client adds items) and you style it:

Summary Block becomes .cnb-card-grid

Each summary item becomes a card

Images auto-crop consistently

Hover effects and spacing are global

Client edits:

add/remove cards

reorder

change titles/descriptions

swap images

You never touch the grid again.

If you tell me which Squarespace version you’re on (7.1 yes/no) and whether the site currently uses Fluid Engine heavily, I’ll propose an exact “Section Library” (like 15 sections) and how to implement each one using native blocks + CSS so the client gets a component-like experience without you living in the editor.

You said:
Ok and what's the situation in shopify with regards to this?
ChatGPT said:

Thought for 43s
Shopify is much closer to what you’re hoping Squarespace can be. With Online Store 2.0, you can absolutely build custom, reusable, client-editable “components” as:

Sections (reusable modules)

Blocks (sub-components inside a section)

JSON templates (page “recipes” that assemble sections)

Section groups (reusable header/footer and other layout-controlled groups)

Merchants then edit everything in the Theme Editor without touching code. 

What “client-editable” looks like in Shopify (the good news)
1) Sections and blocks are first-class “components”
You write a sections/whatever.liquid file and define a JSON schema inside it. The schema creates the editable UI fields the client sees (text, images, selects, toggles), and you can also define blocks they can add/reorder inside the section. 

2) Sections can be added to any page via JSON templates
In OS 2.0, templates are JSON files (for product, collection, page, etc.) that list which sections appear on that template. Merchants can add/remove/reorder sections right in the Theme Editor. 

3) You can make “global reusable chunks” with section groups
Section groups are JSON data files that store a list of sections and app blocks to render, and their settings. They’re used to make parts like header/footer customizable without code edits. 

4) You can make it scalable with dynamic sources (metafields/metaobjects)
Clients can fill structured fields (metafields/metaobjects) and connect them to section settings using dynamic sources, so “adding a new page/product type” doesn’t require layout rebuilds. 

The dev workflow that keeps you out of mouse-land
You build a section library once, then “compose” templates.

Theme file anatomy (simplified)
sections/ = your reusable components (hero, grid, editorial block, etc.)

snippets/ = tiny partials you reuse inside sections (button, icon, card)

templates/*.json = which sections appear on each page type

assets/ = CSS/JS (your design system output)

Shopify even documents that merchants can customize via settings at the theme/section/block level through JSON. 

Concrete example: a reusable “Hero” section that the client can reskin
Create sections/hero.liquid:

<section class="c-hero c-hero--{{ section.settings.variant }}">
  <div class="c-hero__inner">
    {% if section.settings.kicker != blank %}
      <p class="c-hero__kicker">{{ section.settings.kicker }}</p>
    {% endif %}

    <h1 class="c-hero__title">{{ section.settings.title }}</h1>

    {% if section.settings.body != blank %}
      <div class="c-hero__body">{{ section.settings.body }}</div>
    {% endif %}

    {% if section.settings.cta_label != blank and section.settings.cta_link != blank %}
      <a class="c-btn" href="{{ section.settings.cta_link }}">{{ section.settings.cta_label }}</a>
    {% endif %}
  </div>
</section>

{% schema %}
{
  "name": "Hero",
  "settings": [
    { "type": "text", "id": "kicker", "label": "Kicker" },
    { "type": "text", "id": "title", "label": "Title", "default": "Headline" },
    { "type": "richtext", "id": "body", "label": "Body" },
    { "type": "text", "id": "cta_label", "label": "Button label" },
    { "type": "url", "id": "cta_link", "label": "Button link" },
    {
      "type": "select",
      "id": "variant",
      "label": "Style variant",
      "default": "standard",
      "options": [
        { "value": "standard", "label": "Standard" },
        { "value": "minimal", "label": "Minimal" },
        { "value": "inset", "label": "Inset" }
      ]
    }
  ],
  "presets": [{ "name": "Hero" }]
}
{% endschema %}
Now the client can:

Add this section to many pages

Swap the variant from a dropdown

Edit title/body/CTA in the UI

Reorder sections visually

That’s exactly what Shopify designed sections/settings for. 

Page templates: “save a layout recipe” (Shopify-style)
Create templates/page.about.json (example):

{
  "sections": {
    "main-hero": { "type": "hero", "settings": { "variant": "minimal" } },
    "main-richtext": { "type": "rich-text", "settings": {} },
    "main-cta": { "type": "cta", "settings": {} }
  },
  "order": ["main-hero", "main-richtext", "main-cta"]
}
That’s how you give them “templates” they can reuse. OS 2.0 is explicitly built around JSON templates and dynamic sections. 

Limits and guardrails (so you don’t overbuild)
Shopify OS 2.0 pages rendered via JSON templates/section groups have practical limits (Shopify documents caps like 25 sections per template/group and up to 50 blocks per section). 

This is fine for a clean, editorial build (and it keeps clients from turning pages into junk drawers).

So, yes: Shopify can do what you wanted Squarespace to do
If your goal is:

“I want to code reusable building blocks, then let the client assemble/edit pages without me.”

Shopify OS 2.0 is basically that. Sections/blocks/settings are the whole game. 

If you tell me what theme you’re starting from (Dawn or something else), I’ll propose a tight Phase 1 “section library” for Lillier (like 10–12 sections max) and exactly which JSON templates to create so the client can launch fast and extend later without calling you for every new page.

Thought for 32s
What she means by “content-forward” for C+B
In practice, “content-forward” almost always means:

Reading and viewing come first. The site is designed around stories, guidance, resources, and classes rather than flashy interactions or heavy UI.

Typography and spacing do the heavy lifting. Strong type scale, comfortable line length, generous whitespace, clear hierarchy.

Photography is treated like editorial. Fewer images, higher impact, consistent crop/treatment, lots of breathing room.

Minimal “UI chrome.” Fewer badges, widgets, carousels, popups, and gimmicks. Navigation is simple and calm.

Pages are easy to extend. New resource pages look great just by using the standard block patterns, without bespoke layout work each time.

“Light membership” fits the vibe. Gated content exists, but the site should not feel like a SaaS app with dashboards.

What to expect from this as the developer: you’ll be judged more on rhythm, clarity, and restraint than on fancy features.

What to expect from a “design system / component and system spec”
A good spec from her design partners will usually include most of the following. You should ask for it in a form you can actually build from.

1) Design tokens (the “system backbone”)
You want a concrete set of decisions, not vibes:

Typefaces, sizes, weights, line-heights

Spacing scale (ex: 4/8/12/16/24/32/48)

Colors (backgrounds, text, borders, accents) + states (hover/focus/disabled)

Breakpoints and container widths

Your contribution if missing: create a token file yourself (even a simple JSON) and run it through both platforms as CSS variables. If partners give “visual direction” but not numbers, you turn it into numbers.

You said:
Is 7.1 or fluid engine better for our squarespace needs?
ChatGPT said:

Thought for 2m 17s
They’re not really alternatives.

Squarespace 7.1 = the site version / platform.

Fluid Engine = one of the editing experiences for sections inside 7.1 (grid-based drag/resize/overlap). In 7.1, a “block section” can be Fluid Engine or Classic Editor. 

So the real decisions for C+B are:

Is the existing C+B site on 7.0 or 7.1?

If it’s 7.1: which sections should be Fluid Engine vs Classic Editor?

My recommendation for your needs (content-forward + client-editable + you hate grid fiddling)
If C+B is already on Squarespace 7.1
Go hybrid:

Use Classic Editor sections for most of the site (editorial/content pages, resource/library pages, most CTAs). You can still add new blank Classic Editor sections in 7.1. 

Use Fluid Engine sparingly only where you truly need layout “art direction” (a hero, a special collage, maybe 1–2 signature sections).

Why:

Fluid Engine is a grid system and tends to pull you into layout-by-mouse. 

Classic Editor sections keep you in a more structured, “content-first” flow, and they’re harder for clients to accidentally wreck later.

If you upgrade a Classic section to Fluid Engine and save, you can’t revert that section back. Squarespace explicitly notes it’s not reversible after saving. 

Also: if you do convert sections to Fluid Engine, Squarespace warns the layout can shift / misalign because the two systems aren’t equivalent. 

If C+B is currently on Squarespace 7.0
For a March 1 Phase 1, I’d usually avoid upgrading to 7.1 right now unless there’s a clear blocker, because:

The 7.0 → 7.1 update can remove/alter template-specific features (headers, banners, nav behaviors, etc.), and Squarespace documents a bunch of differences. 

The update is optional but irreversible once published. 

Squarespace even recommends duplicating the 7.0 site before updating so you can reference it. 

Good news: “member sites / gated content” is supported across both versions (Squarespace documentation includes 7.0-specific notes and setup steps), so membership alone usually isn’t a reason to rush an upgrade. 

What this means in plain terms for your workflow
7.1 is the long-term direction, but Fluid Engine should not become your default if you want to avoid hand-layout work.

Your best practical setup is:

Classic sections for the content system

Fluid Engine only for a couple “signature” sections

Global CSS doing the heavy lifting so the client can edit content without “designing.”

One quick check you can do before the call
Ask (or check yourself in the editor):

When editing a section, if you see “Add Block” in the top-right, it’s Fluid Engine; if you see insert “+” points between blocks, it’s Classic Editor. Squarespace describes this distinction. 

If you tell me whether C+B is 7.0 or 7.1, I’ll give you a concrete “Phase 1 policy” like:

“Only 2 Fluid Engine sections allowed site-wide”

“Everything else built from Classic sections + reusable patterns”
so you don’t get dragged into layout drudgery.

Here’s a clean, defensible way to justify $6,000 for Phase 1 and $400 / $700 / $1,000 monthly afterward, without sounding like you’re “selling.” The framing is: two platforms, reusable systems, clean handoff, and guardrails that prevent chaos.

1) Justifying the $6,000 Phase 1 fixed fee
The simple logic (what she’s actually buying)
You’re not charging for “pages.” You’re charging for:

Two platform builds (Squarespace + Shopify)

A shared system (typography, spacing rhythm, components/sections) applied consistently

A build that is easy for her to extend without re-hiring you for every new page

A Phase 1 blueprint + scope control, plus QA + launch support

That’s what turns “ambitious vision” into something real, on schedule.

The “time math” you can cite (without overexplaining)
A $6,000 fixed Phase 1 fee usually implies roughly 40–60 hours depending on your effective rate and how clean the inputs are (design/copy/page lists already locked helps). That’s realistic for:

system setup + section library

two platforms

QA + launch

You don’t need to state your hourly rate unless she asks, but you can say:

“This is priced as a fixed Phase 1 execution scope, roughly equivalent to ~X weeks of focused build time, with a clear blueprint and handoff.”

What makes $6,000 “fair” inside a $12,500 total budget
It leaves room for her design partners + photography while still valuing the person doing full implementation.

It matches her “modular + supported” intent: you take the build lane and don’t compete with design spend.

It’s not a bargain-basement number for two platforms, but it’s also not consuming the entire budget.

What you should attach to the $6,000 (to protect both of you)
Make the justification stronger by tying it to deliverables and boundaries:

Included in $6,000 Phase 1

Phase 1 execution plan (page types + section library + milestones)

C+B Squarespace Phase 1 build on staging, then launch swap

QA + launch support window

Explicitly not included (Phase 2 / separate scope)

Marketplace/multi-vendor mechanics

Large-scale content migration or “unlimited content uploading”

Net-new features beyond the Phase 1 blueprint

This makes the $6k feel like a “solid, elegant foundation,” not an open-ended promise.

2) Justifying the monthly maintenance tiers ($400 / $700 / $1,000)
Frame these as ongoing site partner options that match how she described wanting to operate: someone who can keep things tidy and evolving while she handles broader fires.

What you’re really selling here
Response time + continuity + proactive upkeep

A predictable way to handle “small but constant” tasks: edits, tweaks, app conflicts, subscription settings, theme adjustments, seasonal updates, buglets.

You’re not selling “time.” You’re selling “we won’t lose momentum after launch.”

Make each tier about outcomes (and include hours as a guideline)

$400/mo — Lite
Best for: stability + small updates

Up to 1–2 hours/month

Minor content updates (text/image swaps)

Basic troubleshooting and quick fixes

Best for: active upkeep + small improvements

Up to 3–4 hours/month

Shopify: collection merchandising tweaks, template adjustments

Squarespace: membership gating tweaks, resource organization support

Why it’s justified: this is the “real partner mode” for a live brand that’s evolving.

$1,000/mo — Plus
Best for: ongoing growth + faster cadence

Up to 5–6 hours/month

Everything in Standard

Priority turnaround

More proactive improvements: new page builds from templates, A/B-ish refinements (light), ongoing polish cycles

Coordination with other partners if needed (design/photo) to implement updates smoothly

Retainer covers maintenance + small iterative work.

That keeps your relationship healthy and prevents resentment.

ChatGPT said:
Her reply is good news: she’s accepting scope + budget, and she’s proposing a very normal tweak to the payment milestones.

What’s an SOW?
SOW = Statement of Work. It’s the document that spells out:

what you’re delivering (scope/deliverables)

what’s not included

timeline/milestones

payment schedule + invoicing terms

responsibilities/dependencies (design spec, content, access)

revision limits / change-request process

launch + post-launch support terms

Think of it as the “operating manual” for the project so nobody has to rely on memory.

Is 30/40/30 reasonable?
Yes — as long as “core pages built and ready for review” is clearly defined so you’re not doing 80% of the work before the 40% payment triggers.

You’ll want to tighten that milestone to something measurable, like:

both sites have global styles applied

navigation + header/footer are in place

all agreed Phase 1 core templates are built in staging/dev

gated content mechanism is configured (C+B)

Shopify core templates are functional (home/collection/product/content)

content can be placeholder if final photography/copy isn’t ready

That way the 40% is tied to build completion, not final polish.

Anything else you should know/do right now
Before you let her send the agreement/SOW, make sure these are explicitly in it (quick list):

Definition of “core pages built” (what triggers the 40%)

Dependencies: design system handoff, photography, copy, access

Out-of-scope items explicitly excluded (AI tool, marketplace)

Revision / iteration expectations (e.g., number of review rounds per milestone)

Launch definition: what counts as “launch” (go-live on primary domain) and what happens if dependencies delay it

Post-launch support: your $400/$700/$1,000 tiers as optional add-on, and what’s included/excluded

Staging approach: you’ll work in a staging/duplicate environment (especially for Squarespace) until go-live

Payment terms: net 7/14, late fee policy (optional), and that you invoice at each milestone

A concise reply you can send her
(No em-dashes.)

You said:
now she wants to schedule a kick-off. what's a kick-off?
ChatGPT said:
A kickoff is the first official working meeting after the agreement is signed. Its purpose is to get everyone aligned and remove blockers so you can start building immediately.

Think of it as: “We’re live, here’s the plan, here are the owners, here are the deadlines, here’s what I need first.”

What you do in a kickoff (for this project)
1) Confirm Phase 1 scope in plain terms

Re-state what’s in and what’s out (no AI tool, no marketplace)

Confirm the Phase 1 page list and the “core templates” you’ll build

2) Lock the inputs + dependencies

Design partners: when you get the component/system spec (even v1)

Content/copy: what’s final vs placeholder

Photography: what’s arriving when (Lillier product shots especially)

3) Get access and environments set

Squarespace admin access (and agreement to duplicate for staging)

Shopify collaborator access and theme decision (Dawn-based or other)

Domain/DNS access if needed for launch

Any email/form destinations and tools

4) Decide architecture and editing workflow

How she (or her team) will add pages later

Membership gating rules for C+B

Lillier collection/category architecture

5) Agree on review cadence

When she reviews builds (weekly 20–30 min is typical)

Where feedback goes (one doc, Notion, email thread, etc.)

6) End with next steps

You leave with a checklist and dates, she leaves knowing what she owes you and when

What to ask her to bring to kickoff
Core page lists (even if rough)

Any WIP design system or component spec

Content folder links (copy + photos)

Product/collection architecture for Lillier (top-level categories)

What you should bring
Your milestone timeline to March 1

Your “section library” list (10–15 sections)

A short dependency checklist

A proposal for the weekly review rhythm

If you want, I can draft a tight kickoff agenda you can paste into an email, plus the exact questions to ask so you leave the meeting with everything you need.

C) Lillier (Shopify) store architecture + launch essentials
“How many products at launch, roughly?”

“Are there variants (size, color) or mostly single-SKU items?”

Feedback channel: ____

You said:
since it's not a marketplace build, how do you think commission actually operates in this case? so i can be prepared for that part.
You said:
Looks like c + b squarespace is using 7.1
ChatGPT said:
If it’s not a marketplace build (no vendor portals, no split payouts, no routing orders to sellers automatically), “commission-based” usually means the commission exists in the business + ops layer, not in the checkout experience.

Here are the most common ways that plays out on a “standard Shopify store launch,” and what it would mean for you technically.

1) Margin model (most common, simplest)
How it works: Lillier “takes commission” by setting the retail price above cost (wholesale pricing). The “commission” is effectively gross margin.

What the customer sees: totally normal store.

What ops does: track vendor COGS and pay vendors manually from reports.

Tech impact (Phase 1): basically none. Just make sure product data can store vendor + cost info (even if only in admin notes/metafields).

What to ask today:

“Are you buying wholesale / setting retail price above cost, and tracking margin as commission?”

2) Consignment / payout after purchase (still not marketplace, but needs discipline)
How it works: Customer buys from Lillier. Lillier later pays each vendor their share. There’s still a single checkout, but behind the scenes, order lines get attributed to vendors and payouts happen manually or via bookkeeping tools.

What the customer sees: normal store.

Tech impact (Phase 1): light-to-medium:

Add a Vendor field (Shopify has “vendor” built-in, plus you can add metafields).

Make sure order exports/reports can group by vendor (for payout).

You might add a simple internal workflow doc; maybe later an app.

What to ask today:

“Is the commission model consignment, meaning you’ll pay vendors after a sale? If yes, do you want vendor attribution at the product level on day one?”

3) Affiliate / referral (link-out checkout on the vendor’s site)
How it works: Lillier lists products, but “Buy” links out to the vendor’s own checkout using affiliate tracking.

What the customer sees: they leave Lillier to purchase elsewhere.

Tech impact (Phase 1): this is not a standard Shopify store experience anymore. It conflicts with “single checkout” unless used for a tiny subset.

What to ask today:

Client shared a google drive older with me where I can see one photo in the outermost dir, but all inner dirs appear empty. am i missing some permission, or what?
ChatGPT said:

Thought for 1m 8s
Yeah — that pattern (you can see the folder names, but they look empty) is usually one of these Drive quirks:

Most likely causes
1) You have access to the parent folder, but not the contents inside
This happens when files/subfolders were shared with different rules (or were moved in from somewhere else and didn’t inherit permissions the way you’d expect). Drive often shows the folder as “empty” instead of telling you “no access.”

Quick test: open one of the “empty” folders and look at the top-right:

if you see a Request access button or a banner, it’s permissions.

if no banner, still could be restricted contents.

Fix (client side):
Have her open the parent folder → Share → add your email explicitly (not just link) → ensure it applies to everything inside.

2) Those inner folders are shortcuts to other folders you don’t have access to
Shortcuts can appear inside a shared folder, but you may not have permission to the shortcut target.

Quick test: right-click one “empty” folder → View details
If it says Type: Shortcut, that’s the explanation.

Fix: she needs to share the actual target folder with you (or replace shortcuts with real folders/files inside the shared folder).

3) It’s a Shared Drive / restricted container
If she’s using a Workspace Shared Drive with tight policies, sharing a folder to an external collaborator can show structure but block contents depending on org settings.

Quick test: in the folder header, does it say it’s in a Shared drive (not “My Drive”)?

Fix: she may need to:

add you as a member of the Shared Drive (Viewer/Commenter is fine), or

move the photo library into “My Drive” and share from there.

4) The folders are genuinely empty (placeholders)
Sometimes they’ve created structure but haven’t moved the files in yet. The one image in the root is a clue this could be the case.

Quick test: use Drive search within the folder for jpg or png.
If zero results, it may truly be empty.

What I’d do in 2 minutes (your side)
Open one “empty” folder → refresh → try List view (sometimes grid doesn’t render items instantly).

Right-click → View details → check if it’s a shortcut.

In that folder, search: type:image or jpg.

Check if you see any “limited access” indicators.

A simple message to send her (fast + non-accusatory)
“Thanks for sharing the photo library. I can see the folder structure, but the subfolders are showing as empty on my end (I only see one image in the root). This usually happens when the inner folders/files don’t inherit permissions or some of the folders are shortcuts to locations I don’t have access to. Could you either (1) re-share the top folder with my email ensuring it applies to everything inside, or (2) confirm whether those subfolders are shortcuts / in a Shared Drive?”

If you tell me whether the folder lives in My Drive or a Shared drive, and whether “View details” shows Shortcut, I’ll tell you exactly which fix is the right one.

You said:
how to apply share permission with all subfolders, when sending permission to a certain email?
ChatGPT said:
Here’s the clean way to do it in Google Drive, plus what to do if it still doesn’t cascade.

The normal way (works if inheritance is allowed)
If it’s in My Drive
Right-click the top-level folder → Share

In Add people and groups, enter the email

Choose role (Viewer/Commenter/Editor)

Click the gear icon (settings) if needed:

(Optional) disable “Editors can change permissions…” etc.

Click Send

This should give that email access to everything inside the folder that inherits permissions.

If it doesn’t apply to everything (common causes + fixes)
A) Some subfolders/files have unique permissions
Those items won’t inherit from the parent.

Fix (client side):

Open one of the “empty” subfolders

Right-click → Share

If it shows “Restricted” or different people, it has unique perms

Add your email there too (or reset sharing by removing restrictions)

There isn’t a perfect “force propagate to all descendants” button in consumer Drive UI. The practical fix is: make sure items aren’t uniquely restricted, or use an admin/workspace tool if they have one.

B) The subfolders are Shortcuts
Sharing the parent folder doesn’t grant access to the shortcut target.

Fix: Share the actual target folder (or move the real folders/files into the shared parent, not shortcuts).

C) It’s inside a Shared Drive
Sharing behaves differently and may be governed by Workspace policy.

Fix options:

Add you as a member of the Shared Drive, or

Move/copy the asset folders into My Drive and share from there.

Best “quick verification” step for them
After sharing the top folder, have them:

Open one subfolder

Right-click → Share

Confirm your email appears under “People with access”

If your email shows there but you still see empty, it’s usually shortcuts or a Shared Drive restriction.

If you want a one-line instruction you can paste to the client
“Please share the top-level PHOTO LIBRARY folder directly to my email (not link-only), then open one of the inner folders and confirm my email appears under ‘People with access.’ If the inner folders are shortcuts or in a Shared Drive, you’ll need to share the shortcut targets / add me to the Shared Drive as well.”

If you want, tell me whether that photo library folder is in My Drive or a Shared drive, and I’ll give the exact simplest fix for that case.

You said:
Let's focus our attention ow on how to plan to build the two sites most efficiently. We want to use our time wisely, economically. What in your professional opinion is the best approach here? ChatGPT said:

Thought for a second

Thought for 18s
The most efficient way to build these two different platforms without doing everything twice is not “one shared codebase” in the traditional sense (Squarespace + Shopify won’t let you truly share templates/components cleanly), but a shared design-system layer + shared content patterns, then a thin platform-specific implementation layer.

That gives you maximum reuse while staying realistic about each platform’s constraints.

Below is the approach I’d use, with comparisons so the reasoning is clear, then a step-by-step execution plan.

Compare and contrast: 3 ways to do this
Option 1: Build both sites separately inside each platform
Pros

Fast start, low tooling overhead.

Cons

You duplicate decisions constantly (spacing/type/buttons/cards/nav behavior).

Rework multiplies when design changes.

Harder to keep the “shared DNA” consistent long-term.

Verdict: Fine for tiny sites; inefficient for what you’re doing here (membership/login/gating + AI overlay on C+B, curated taxonomy + brands system on Lillier).

Option 2: Try to force a “single codebase” across both platforms (true shared templates/components)
Pros

In theory, one source of truth.

Cons

In practice, Shopify themes (Liquid/JSON templates) and Squarespace 7.1 (sections/blocks + limited code injection) don’t share a common rendering model.

You’ll waste time building an adapter layer that’s more work than the project.

Verdict: Not worth it.

Option 3: The best approach: shared “DNA kit” + platform adapters
Build a Shared DNA Kit once:

design tokens (type scale, spacing scale, radii, shadows, button styles)

reusable section patterns (hero, editorial text block, card grids, CTA rows, accordions)

interaction patterns (modal behavior, nav patterns, form styling)

image rules (aspect ratios per module; crop guidance)

Then implement that kit in each platform using the platform’s native strengths:

Shopify: theme code (sections/snippets), collections, brand templates.

Squarespace 7.1: saved sections + page templates, minimal code injection, and only custom JS where required (ex: AI overlay).

Development Plan for Cupcakes+Broccoli (Squarespace 7.1) & Lillier (Shopify)
Introduction and Goals
We are building two complementary websites – Cupcakes + Broccoli (an educational community site on Squarespace 7.1) and Lillier (an e-commerce marketplace on Shopify). Both sites will share a unified visual design system and brand feel, while leveraging each platform’s strengths. The primary goals are to deliver mobile-first experiences with clear structure, built for easy owner editing and extension. Development will prioritize code-centric workflows for flexibility and scalability, avoiding over-reliance on drag-and-drop, while still enabling a non-technical client to manage content and reuse components via the UI after launch. All pages and features in Phase 1 will be built with an eye toward long-term maintainability, allowing new content and sections to be added without structural overhaul. Key Objectives:
Shared Design DNA: Implement the design partners’ component and style specifications on both sites, ensuring consistent typography, color palettes, and UI components across platforms. Both sites should have a cohesive look (layout, buttons, forms, etc.) so users sense a unified brand experience.
Flexible, Code-Based Development: Use developer-friendly workflows – custom code, templating, version control – to speed up development and avoid the constraints of purely visual editors. However, ensure that all site content (text, images, links) is managed through CMS features (Squarespace’s editor and Shopify’s theme/panels) rather than hardcoded in code, so the client can easily edit without touching code.
Reusable & Editable Components: Build pages from modular sections/blocks that can be reused and adjusted. For Squarespace, utilize 7.1’s Saved Sections feature to create a library of branded sections that can be dropped into any page
. For Shopify, leverage Online Store 2.0’s sections and blocks – self-contained components with customizable settings – to allow drag-and-drop page updates
. This will let the client extend pages or create new ones by mixing existing sections (e.g. hero banners, CTAs, product grids) without custom code.
Client-Friendly CMS Structure: Structure content types and editing workflows so a non-technical editor can safely update the site. This means using the built-in CMS capabilities of each platform (pages, collections, blogs, etc.) rather than custom code for content. We will avoid “hardcoding” copy or images into templates. Instead, use dynamic fields or editable areas:
In Squarespace, all text and images will be in editable content blocks or page settings (not in Code Injection except when absolutely necessary for design).
In Shopify, use theme schema settings and content entries so that, for example, homepage text, banner images, or product details come from theme editor inputs, collection descriptions, or pages – not fixed in Liquid templates. This ensures the client can change labels, imagery, and links through Shopify’s interface.
Balance Phase 1 and Future Needs: Deliver the required Phase 1 pages and functionality on time, but build the foundation to accommodate future growth. That means:
Using scalable architecture (e.g. setting up navigation, category structures, and membership logic that can handle new pages or features later without refactoring).
Possibly including placeholders or initial support for Phase 2 features (for instance, structuring the Shopify theme so adding a “Fashion” or “Gifts” category later is straightforward, even though these are out-of-scope in Phase 1).
Documenting and organizing code and assets clearly, so future developers (or the client, with guidance) can extend the sites.
With these goals in mind, below is a detailed breakdown comparing the development approach on Squarespace vs Shopify, followed by strategies specific to each site, and finally an implementation sequence with an actionable Phase 1 checklist.
Platform Workflow & Architecture Comparison: Squarespace 7.1 vs Shopify
Building on Squarespace 7.1 and Shopify will require different approaches, given the platforms’ inherent differences. We will leverage each platform in the way that best aligns with our goals of flexibility and client-friendly editing:
Squarespace 7.1 (Cupcakes + Broccoli)
Dev Workflow: Squarespace 7.1 does not support the old Developer Mode for full template code access (unlike Squarespace 7.0) – we cannot build from scratch by uploading templates
. Instead, development occurs within Squarespace’s framework:
We will use the Squarespace 7.1 section editor to assemble pages from pre-built sections and content blocks. Squarespace pages are composed of sections that stack vertically, each containing content blocks (text, images, buttons, etc.). We will choose section layouts that closely match the design, then fine-tune styling.
For custom styling or functionality beyond Squarespace’s style settings, we will add Custom CSS/JS via the Squarespace Code Injection feature (available on Business plan and above). Note: We’ll use custom code cautiously – while it gives flexibility, Squarespace warns that code injections can conflict with the platform’s own code and aren’t officially supported
. We’ll keep any injected code lean, test it thoroughly on different devices (to ensure it remains responsive and doesn’t break Squarespace’s mobile optimizations
), and document it for future maintenance.
Reusable Sections: A major Squarespace advantage for our needs is the Saved Sections feature (Squarespace 7.1) which lets us save a designed section and reuse it on other pages
. We will create a library of common sections (e.g. a newsletter signup block, a testimonial or quote block, a CTA banner) per the design system. This approach essentially builds a mini design system within Squarespace – the saved sections act as “branded building blocks” that ensure consistency and save time when creating new pages
. The client can add a saved section to any page with a couple of clicks instead of recreating layouts each time.
Content Management: All textual content and images will be managed through Squarespace’s CMS interface:
Static pages (About, Work With Amanda, Blind Dinners, etc.) will have their copy placed in text blocks that the client can edit inline.
Repeated content (like list of stories, or resources) will use Squarespace collections if applicable. For example, the “Stories” section can be a Blog page, so each story is a blog post – allowing Amanda to easily publish new stories through Squarespace’s blog editor. We will style the blog list and posts template to match the site design.
We will avoid any situation where the client would need to edit the site by touching code. (If a design element truly requires custom code to implement, we’ll try to wrap it in a user-friendly way – e.g. using Code Block with instructions, or at least isolating CSS that might need editing).
Design & Templating Constraints: Because we can’t fully code new page templates in 7.1, we will choose the base theme or initial layout that is closest to our needs and customize from there. Squarespace’s built-in style editor will be used for global styles (fonts, colors, spacing) as much as possible. We anticipate using custom CSS for fine-grain control (for example, if we need to perfectly match the design spec’s spacing or if certain elements like buttons need custom state styling beyond the style editor capabilities).
Membership Integration: Squarespace offers Member Areas (now referred to as “Member Sites”) as a way to gate content behind a sign-up/paywall
. We will utilize this for Cupcakes+Broccoli’s membership features. In practice, we’ll create a Member Area within the Squarespace site, which will contain pages only accessible to members (and choose free vs paid access as needed). Out-of-the-box, Squarespace will handle account creation, login, and paywalling of those pages – a user who isn’t logged in will see a sign-up prompt for the membership plan. We will configure at least two membership tiers:
A free tier (or public access) for basic content.
A premium paid tier for full content (as hinted by the copy: “Premium members receive unlimited access to deeper responses…” etc. and a CTA to compare membership options).
Using Squarespace’s system ensures only members with the appropriate plan can view protected pages
. This satisfies the requirement for “light membership / gated content for agreed pages/resources”. We will design the member pages to look like part of the site (avoiding a generic “dashboard” feel – e.g., by using the same header and styles on member pages).
Login/Signup UI: We will include a persistent Login button in the header (as noted in the nav spec) that triggers the Squarespace login overlay or directs to the login page. After login, members should seamlessly access the content; we’ll test that the transition doesn’t feel like leaving the site’s aesthetic.
Scalability & Maintenance: Since our ability to extend via code is limited, we will rely on Squarespace’s structured approach for scalability. For example, if new pages are needed, the client can duplicate an existing page (or insert saved sections) to preserve style consistency. Squarespace 7.1 allows duplicating entire pages and sections easily
. We will provide guidance on this to the client post-launch. The overall structure (navigation, page hierarchy) can be managed via the Squarespace Pages panel, which the client can handle for adding a new top-level page or rearranging navigation.
Risks/Bottlenecks (Squarespace): The main risk is hitting a wall with Squarespace’s limitations. If the design calls for a highly custom layout or interactive component not supported by Squarespace blocks, implementing it may require creative workarounds (custom code injection or embedding external widgets). We must also be mindful that custom code is not guaranteed to be future-proof on Squarespace
 – Squarespace updates could potentially break unsupported customizations. To mitigate this:
We will keep custom JavaScript/CSS modular and avoid affecting core features. For example, the AI Concierge overlay on the C+B homepage will likely be implemented via a custom code block or third-party script. We’ll implement it such that it loads separately (e.g., an embedded script or iframe for the AI chatbot) to avoid interfering with site content. We will ensure closing the AI modal returns the user to the same scroll position (per spec).
If any design element truly cannot be achieved within Squarespace’s framework, we will communicate with the design team to find an acceptable alternative that stays within platform bounds.
Performance on Squarespace can degrade if too many large images or heavy scripts are injected; we’ll optimize images (use Squarespace’s built-in responsive image features and provide appropriately sized images) and only load scripts when needed.
Shopify (Online Store 2.0) – Lillier
Dev Workflow: Shopify provides a full-featured development environment for themes, which aligns well with our code-based workflow preference. We will likely start with Shopify’s reference theme (e.g. Dawn, which is a lean, Shopify 2.0-compliant theme) as a base, and then build out the custom Lillier theme from there. Key workflow elements:
Use the Shopify CLI and Theme Kit/GitHub integration to develop locally with version control. This allows efficient coding in a local editor and syncing changes to a development theme on the Shopify store. It also provides a safety net (we can roll back if needed)
. We will have a Git repository for the theme code to track changes and facilitate collaboration.
Theme Architecture (Online Store 2.0): Shopify’s Online Store 2.0 introduced JSON-based templates and “sections everywhere,” enabling a modular, flexible theme structure
. We will utilize this fully:
Each page template (homepage, product page, collection page, etc.) will include sections that can be rearranged or customized via the Shopify Theme Editor. Sections and blocks are modular components that give merchants (the client) the opportunity to customize and extend their theme*
.* We will design most of Lillier’s content areas as sections (with configurable settings) so the client can easily modify content or layout without code.
For example, the homepage will consist of multiple custom sections: Hero banner, Category grid, Brand spotlight, “Lillier Difference” features, and a CTA/footer section. The client can reorder or toggle these sections in the theme editor, or even reuse them on other pages if needed.
We will create Liquid snippets for any repetitive code fragments (like a product card design used in multiple sections) to keep code DRY (Don’t Repeat Yourself) and maintainable.
Reusable & Extensible Components: Because Shopify doesn’t have an exact analog of Squarespace’s Saved Sections (Shopify sections themselves are inherently reusable in templates), we’ll ensure each custom section is built to be flexible:
Sections will use schema definitions to expose settings like text fields, image pickers, selectors, etc., so the client can change content easily. For instance, a “Category Highlights” section might have a setting for selecting which collections to feature and text to display. The schema also allows default content presets, so when we add the section it comes pre-configured with sample content that the client can then edit.
We will design these sections to be generalized enough for reuse. If a section might be useful on multiple pages (e.g., a generic two-column text + image section), we’ll code it in a way that it can be added anywhere via the theme editor. Shopify 2.0 sections can indeed be included on any page type, and merchants can add/remove sections per page as needed
.
Content Management (Shopify CMS): Shopify is primarily e-commerce, but it also includes a rudimentary CMS (Pages, Blog, and now Metaobjects). We plan to use:
Collections for organizing products, which doubles as our category pages. Per the Lillier taxonomy, we will set up top-level collections for each primary category (Home, Beauty, Pantry, Pet). Products will be tagged or otherwise assigned so that each product belongs to one primary category (curation is intentional; each brand’s products mostly fall under one category).
Subcategories: We will implement subcategories as either additional collections or as product tags/metafields used for filtering, not as separate navigation entries. For example, within the “Home” category, subcategories like Kitchen, Bath, Cleaning, Living, etc. will be filters on the Home collection page rather than distinct pages. Shopify’s built-in filtering (OS 2.0) can filter a collection by product tags or metafields. We will configure filters (e.g. a filter by “Subcategory”) so that on a category page, a user can narrow products to a subcategory like “Kitchen” or “Bath”. This aligns with the requirement that subcategories remain in the background for Phase 1 (enhancing browsing but not cluttering the main nav).
Brands: We need to present brands in a dedicated section of the site (in nav and pages). To implement “Brands”:
We will likely create a collection (or page) for each Brand. One approach: treat each brand as a Shopify Collection, where collection title = brand name, collection description = brand story/description, and collection image = brand hero image. We can manually assign each brand’s products to its collection (or use an automated rule like “Product Vendor = BrandName”). This way, each brand has a “page” (the collection page) listing only that brand’s Phase 1 products, and we can render the brand story at top. We will style these brand pages to feel editorial (rich story content) rather than a plain product catalog.
The Brands index page (accessed via the “Brands” top nav) will list all brands. We will build a custom page template for this, possibly with a section that automatically pulls in all brand collections to display their logo or name with a link. (If using the collection approach, we can get a list of all Brand collections by using a common tag or by listing all collections in a certain manual menu). Alternatively, we might use Shopify’s new Metaobjects to create a structured list of brands (with fields for name, description, logo) and use an iteration in Liquid to output them. But for Phase 1, a simpler manual approach (hardcoding the list or using collections) is acceptable given a known list of brands, and it will still be easy for a developer to add a new brand entry when needed.
We will include filtering on brand pages by primary category if feasible. For example, on a brand page that has products from multiple categories, we could allow filtering the products list by those categories. However, since each brand is assigned one primary category in Phase 1, this filter may not be very relevant initially (most brands’ products all fall under the same category). We will plan the page structure such that if in future a brand spans categories, adding a filter or indicator (e.g. grouping products by category) is possible.
Pages & Blog: Other static content like the About page will be a Page in Shopify (allowing the client to edit content via a rich text editor). The About page can still use a custom Liquid template or sections for layout (for example, to insert a full-width image or custom design elements), but the main narrative text will be in the Page content for easy editing. We will similarly handle any other informational pages.
Editorial content / blog: The taxonomy explicitly puts editorial “Stories” out of scope for Phase 1. So initially, Lillier will not have a blog section. However, the homepage’s “Brand Spotlight” and “Explore More Stories” hint at a storytelling component. In Phase 1, we will fulfill this by perhaps linking “Explore More Stories” to the Brands page or a static page highlighting that this feature is coming. We’ll note this as a future enhancement (perhaps Phase 2 might introduce a blog or journal where monthly brand stories are posted). Our Phase 1 theme will be built to allow adding a blog later without major rework (Shopify has a built-in Blog feature).
Global Elements & Settings: We will utilize Shopify’s Theme Settings (in config/settings_schema.json) to centralize global style options and reusable content:
E.g., define color scheme variables, font choices (if custom fonts are needed, we’ll include them via CSS @font-face or use Shopify’s Google Fonts integration).
Set up a global announcement bar text (the copy “Shop Black. Every day. All in one cart.” can be a configurable text in the theme settings so the client can update it).
Enable customer accounts (optional at checkout) to allow returning customers, though no special member content on Lillier – it’s standard e-commerce accounts.
We’ll incorporate the provided utility texts: Cart helper text (“You can shop multiple Black-owned businesses with one checkout”) and Shipping note (“Items may ship separately from each brand”). These will be placed appropriately – e.g., on the Cart page or mini-cart UI, we’ll display those notes. We can implement these as static text or preferably editable text in theme settings so the client can tweak wording if needed in the future.
E-Commerce Setup: Beyond theme code, some configuration is needed:
We will configure shipping such that if products ship from different vendors, the order can still be placed in one checkout. Likely, we will set up a single location for inventory for simplicity (if Lillier acts as the merchant of record for all sales). We’ll use the note in the UI to explain that items may ship separately. (If down the line each brand ships directly, we might use Shopify’s multi-origin shipping feature – but that may complicate shipping rate calculations. For Phase 1, perhaps all items ship at a flat rate or free shipping threshold and then Lillier coordinates with brands manually.)
Set up tax, payment gateway, etc., but these are more operational and likely handled by the Lillier team; still, we must verify that our theme properly surfaces any necessary messaging (like showing tax inclusive/exclusive info, etc., if needed).
Collections and product organization will be carefully curated because it’s a curated marketplace with limited SKUs per brand. We’ll implement those collections and tag products accordingly, and ensure that the theme’s product listing components can handle small numbers elegantly (e.g., not assume infinite scroll or huge catalogs).
Scalability & Future-Proofing (Shopify): The Shopify site is being built with growth in mind:
Our category/collection structure can accommodate new categories (e.g., adding “Fashion” or “Gifts” later). We will avoid hardcoding category names in multiple places. Instead, use data-driven approaches where possible. For example, the homepage “Shop by Category” section can pull categories from a manual list or navigation, which we can update via Shopify admin when new ones are added, rather than requiring code changes.
Similarly, if more brands are onboarded, adding a new brand collection and including it in the Brands page should be a straightforward content update. We might document the steps for the client: “To add a brand: create a collection, tag products, add it to Brands page (via x method)”.
Because we are using Online Store 2.0, the theme can be updated in sections, and new sections can be added later by development without rebuilding the whole theme. We will keep the code organized and commented, making future edits or new features (like adding an editorial blog or gift cards page) easier.
We will follow Shopify’s best practices for theme development (ensuring accessibility, performance, and using Shopify’s built-in features rather than reinventing the wheel). For instance, we’ll use the Shopify Polaris icons or SVGs for consistency in UI (like cart, menu icons) and ensure text is accessible.
Risks/Bottlenecks (Shopify): Building a custom Shopify theme is time-intensive. Potential bottlenecks and our mitigation:
Complex Filtering or Features: If advanced filtering or a custom functionality (like product bundling or subscriptions) was needed, we might rely on Shopify apps. In Phase 1, it appears we can manage with native features (standard filtering, etc.), avoiding third-party app dependencies that could bloat the site. We’ll confirm if any app is required for things like email capture popups or reviews – if yes, integrate them carefully and ensure they don’t conflict with our code.
Data volume and performance: A marketplace could eventually have many products. In Phase 1, SKU counts are limited, so performance will be fine. But we’ll still code for efficiency: use lazy loading for images, limit liquid loops on front-end if product count grows, and test for any sluggishness.
Coordination with Design: If the design partners have very specific interactive elements (e.g., animations or complex hover effects), implementing those in Shopify might require additional libraries or scripts. We’ll weigh the value of each fancy effect vs. added complexity. Simpler is better for maintainability, especially with a client who will be managing content – we don’t want a fragile theme. We’ll highlight any such elements early and decide if they’re must-have or can be simplified.
Shopify-specific constraints: Certain layout decisions might be constrained by Shopify’s structure. For example, the checkout page on Shopify cannot be fully customized (only light branding) because it’s controlled by Shopify. That’s fine for us; we just ensure the logo and color scheme carry into checkout for consistency.
Timeline management: To avoid rushing at the end, we plan to develop the Shopify site in parallel with content integration. We will use placeholder content to build out the theme, then swap in final copy and images once ready. The risk is if final copy or imagery deviates significantly (size or length) from placeholders, it could require style adjustments. We will mitigate by using realistic sample data (e.g., create a few dummy products, use actual text from the copy docs during development where possible).
Cupcakes + Broccoli (Squarespace 7.1) – Detailed Implementation Strategy
For Cupcakes+Broccoli (C+B), the focus is on content (educational resources, stories, community info) with a membership layer for premium content. We will implement a content-forward site with an emphasis on clarity and ease of reading, reflecting the platform’s mission. Below is the plan per aspect: Information Architecture & Page Structure: According to the project documents, C+B’s site map and pages include:
Home – Overview of the platform, key offerings, and CTAs into other sections.
About – Background on C+B and Amanda (founder’s story, mission).
Work With Amanda – Detailing consulting/advisory services (likely what the copy doc called “Services Page”).
Learn – The resource library overview (“Practical learning you can use” section, likely leading to resources).
Stories – A blog-like section for founder stories and experiences.
Blind Dinners – Info page about the invitation-based gatherings.
Membership – Explanation of membership options and benefits, with sign-up CTA.
Login – Not a page per se, but a link that triggers member login (provided by Squarespace’s system).
We will confirm the final page list in Phase 1 and ensure all those types are accounted for. The top navigation will mirror the above structure (with “Login” as a button). There may also be footer navigation for utility links (privacy policy, etc.), which we will add as needed. Homepage: The C+B homepage sets the tone. From copy provided:
Hero section with a powerful headline (“Black ownership is Black survival, freedom and wealth.”) and subhead, and primary CTA “Join Membership”. We will implement this as a bold hero section with text and a prominent call-to-action button. In Squarespace, we might use a built-in cover or banner section, or a combination of text blocks on a background. The CTA will link to the Membership page.
Secondary CTA “Learn More About C+B” goes to About page.
Next sections (“What This Is”, “Built Around Experience”) explain the platform’s approach. We’ll create a rich text section for these, possibly using a two-column layout (text and maybe an illustrative image if provided). Inline link “Learn how C+B works” will go to About page.
AI Concierge teaser: There’s a section describing the AI assistant and showing example questions. We plan to implement an interactive trigger (a button “Ask a question” that opens the AI interface as a modal overlay). The modal can be achieved with a Code Block injecting a custom script (possibly integrating something like an AI chat widget). The key is it should appear as an overlay on the homepage without navigating away. This may require custom HTML/JS – we’ll isolate it so it doesn’t conflict with other elements.
“Learn” teaser section on homepage with a CTA to Resources.
“Work With Amanda” teaser with CTA to that page.
“Blind Dinners” teaser with CTA.
“Membership” teaser with CTA(s) “Join Membership” and “Compare Options”.
Closing section with a resonant line (“Ownership is a practice”) and final membership CTA.
We will likely break the homepage into several sections matching the above content blocks. Using Squarespace’s section editor, we can create vertically stacked sections, each with appropriate background/color styling to differentiate. For example:
Hero: full-bleed background (maybe a color or image if provided) with centered text and buttons.
Content explanation sections: maybe a simple centered text section for “What This Is” and “Ask What You Are Stuck On” (AI) – possibly with an image or icon for the AI concierge to draw attention.
Teaser grid: We have a sequence of offerings (Learn, Work with Amanda, Blind Dinners, Membership). We might present these in individual sections or as a 2x2 grid of blocks with an image and text for each. Squarespace allows creating content layouts with multiple columns; if design calls for side-by-side teasers, we can either use a pre-made layout or build it via a Code Block with HTML/CSS for precise control.
Final CTA: a strong closing section with a background color that contrasts the top, repeating the membership invitation.
We will make each of these sections reusable where it makes sense. For instance, the closing “Explore Membership” CTA might also be useful to include at the end of other pages (About or Learn). We can save that section in Squarespace and insert it on multiple pages to ensure consistency
. Membership (Gated Content) Implementation: A critical part of C+B is the membership model:
We will enable Squarespace Member Areas and set up a member area (perhaps call it “Community” or “Members”). Within this Member Area, we will add any pages that should be gated. Based on content, likely gated content could include:
Certain resource library pages (perhaps premium tools or downloads).
Possibly the “Community” page (if it contains forum or community content for members).
“Premium members” perks like office hours info might be on a members-only page.
We’ll clarify with the client which pages/resources are for members only. For example, the Membership page itself (which describes options) would be public, but if there’s a “Member Home” or exclusive content library, that would be inside the member area.
Pricing Plans: Using Squarespace’s interface, we’ll create at least one paid membership plan (the Premium tier). If there is a free membership (e.g., for signing up to get limited access or just to collect emails), we can configure a $0 free plan as well. Squarespace allows multiple pricing plans per member area (free or recurring fee)
. The site’s Membership page will then have “Join” links that direct the user to purchase the appropriate plan.
We will style the paywall and login screens with the site’s branding. Squarespace lets you add a logo to the login page for member areas and some basic style adjustments. We will ensure that when a non-member hits a gated page, the experience of prompting them to sign up looks coherent (likely we’ll use the Membership page as the sales pitch and direct them from the paywall to that page to choose a plan).
Membership Page: This page will likely outline the benefits of membership and compare tiers. We’ll implement a section that can compare the Free vs Premium features (if needed, an anchor link to a comparison table within the page). Even if exact pricing or tier details are not in the copy docs, we will structure the page to accommodate two tiers and highlight the key difference (like premium gets unlimited AI, office hours, etc., while free is more limited content).
After purchase, members get access to the gated pages automatically (Squarespace manages that once pages are under the Member Area). We will test this flow thoroughly: sign up as a member, ensure we can log in/out, and see the correct content.
Other Key Pages:
About: Will describe the founder-led nature of C+B and what the platform is (content from the copy: “This is a founder-led practice...” and Amanda’s bio summary). We will format this with a combination of text and perhaps a photo of Amanda (if provided). The design might call for pull quotes or stylized text; we can do that with Squarespace’s text editor or small custom CSS for drop caps, etc. There’s an inline link “Learn how Membership works” that goes to Membership page. We ensure all such cross-links are set up.
Work With Amanda: This is a services page for consulting. The copy (from “Services Page” in docs) emphasizes execution, clear goals, etc.. We will lay this out likely with a hero section (“Support When We Need It” as headline) and then a textual description. If there are any specific service offerings or a call-to-action (like “Contact us to inquire about consulting”), we will include a contact form or button accordingly. (Squarespace has a Form Block we can use to capture inquiries.)
Learn (Resources) Page: The copy tagline is “Learn With Us – practical education designed to be used”. We suspect this page is an entry to the resource library, describing what kind of resources are available (tools, frameworks, etc.) and inviting users to explore. The CTA is “Explore Resources” which might lead either to a listing of resources or directly to membership sign-up if resources are gated. We need to clarify if the resources themselves are publicly listed. Possibly, the Learn page might list categories of resources (like Tools, Frameworks, Stories) but then trying to access might prompt login for some.
One approach: Use a Summary Block or a custom index page listing some resource items, so content can be easily updated. For example, if they have a category of resources (maybe a Blog named “Resources” or a Gallery of resources), we can auto-display latest entries. Given limited info, we’ll likely just build Learn as a static overview for Phase 1.
Stories: We will implement Stories as a blog on Squarespace. This allows the client to easily add new story entries over time, fulfilling the need for ongoing content.
We will set up a Blog Page titled “Stories” and use the provided content for the landing page hero (“Real Stories. Real Momentum.” and intro). The blog listing will show posts (title, excerpt, maybe an image if each story has one). We can tweak the layout via Squarespace’s blog settings or CSS to match the style (e.g., maybe a minimalist list or cards).
We will create a sample story post (or a few) using the provided example content about “decisions, uncertainty, momentum”, so that the section isn’t empty. The CTA “Start Reading” will simply scroll to or highlight the stories list below the hero.
If needed for design, we can use tag filters or categories within the blog to organize stories (not specified, but maybe in future they might categorize stories).
Blind Dinners: A static page describing what the Blind Dinners are (intimate gatherings for Black founders, not networking, with trust and shared understanding). We will format this clearly, possibly including imagery of such dinners if available. There might be an inquiry form if one can request an invite or sign up interest.
Login/Account: We will double-check the user flow for login. With Member Areas, Squarespace typically provides a login screen and a default “Account Management” page for members (where they can update their profile or payment method). We will ensure that link is accessible (maybe via the Login button or as a link in the footer “My Account” for logged-in users). The client (Amanda) should be able to manage members (Squarespace provides a list of members and their plans in the backend).
Design System on Squarespace: To maintain the visual consistency:
We’ll apply global style settings (fonts, colors) in the Squarespace Design panel. If the design spec calls for custom fonts not in Squarespace’s library, we can embed them via CSS. However, we try to use built-in font options for easier maintenance (Squarespace supports Google Fonts and some Adobe fonts).
We will define a consistent heading hierarchy (h1, h2, etc.) across the site, matching the design. For example, hero headlines might all be an H1 style – we ensure those are uniform (size, weight) on both home and other pages.
Buttons and links: style primary and secondary buttons according to the design system and use them consistently (e.g., the same “Join Membership” style button appears multiple times). We’ll set this in the style editor if possible so all Buttons blocks have that style.
Spacing and alignment: We’ll use CSS if needed to fine-tune margins/padding of sections to create a harmonious rhythm, so that when stacking saved sections we don’t get inconsistent spacing.
Testing on Squarespace: We will make sure to test the site on various devices (Squarespace’s responsive engine is generally good, but custom code like our AI modal or any custom HTML sections will need manual testing for responsiveness). We’ll also test the membership sign-up and login from a user perspective: e.g., what happens after sign-up (Squarespace can redirect to a certain page – we might have it redirect new members to a welcome page or the “Community” page). In summary, for Cupcakes+Broccoli we aim to deliver a content-rich site that the client can update like a blog/website, with minimal technical hurdles, and a smoothly integrated membership system for premium content access.
Lillier (Shopify) – Detailed Implementation Strategy
Lillier is a curated e-commerce marketplace, so our strategy centers on building a robust Shopify theme that supports an easy shopping experience across multiple categories and brands, while also conveying the brand’s story and mission (making it easy to “shop Black” daily). Navigation & Structure: From the taxonomy:
Main Menu: Home, Shop, Brands, About. (No other top-level items in Phase 1.)
The Shop menu will include the primary shopping categories (Home, Beauty, Pantry, Pet) as sub-menu items. We will configure this in Shopify’s Navigation settings: a top-level “Shop” item that is either non-clickable or leads to a general “All Products” or “Shop” landing if needed, and four child links (Home, Beauty, Pantry, Pet collections). The decision on whether “Shop” itself has a landing page: We might create a “Shop” collection list page that visually presents the categories (essentially duplicating what the homepage category section does). However, since the homepage already has “Shop by Category,” we might simply make “Shop” unclickable and rely on the dropdown. We’ll verify with the client which behavior is preferred for usability.
Brands menu leads to the brands index page listing all brand profiles.
About leads to the about page.
We’ll also include typical e-commerce utilities: a cart icon, and an account icon for login/register (Shopify can handle customer accounts).
Footer: We’ll set up a footer with links like Contact, FAQ (if any), Terms and Privacy, and social media links as needed. These can be configured via a separate footer menu for easy updating.
Homepage: The Lillier homepage content from the copy document:
Hero section: Headline “Own Your Joy”, subhead “Shop Black. Every day. All in one cart.”, and body text about Lillier being one-stop for high-quality products from Black-owned businesses. We will design a compelling hero, likely with a background image or graphic that evokes joy/ownership (if brand assets include a hero image). The CTAs in the hero: “Shop New Arrivals”, “Explore Brands”, and “Featured Collections”. These might be implemented as three prominent buttons or links. We can also consider using a slideshow if there are multiple hero images (not indicated, but we’ll stick to a static hero unless provided otherwise).
“Shop New Arrivals” will link to a collection of new products. We will create a “New Arrivals” automatic collection (e.g., tag products as “New” or use date added) and use that.
“Explore Brands” jumps to the Brands index.
“Featured Collections” – possibly scrolls down the homepage to the next section, which is “Shop by Category”, or it could link to a page listing all collections. The copy is a bit ambiguous; however, immediately after, the content lists categories with a headline “Shop by Category”. So it’s likely that by “Featured Collections” they mean those top categories.
Shop by Category section: Headline “Shop by Category”. The intro copy spans page 1-2 of the PDF and lists categories:
Household Essentials,
Beauty and Personal Care,
Food and Beverage,
Home and Décor,
Fashion and Accessories,
Gifts and Stationery.
This list includes 6 categories, which is more than the 4 primary categories for Phase 1. The taxonomy document explicitly says “Gifts” is out-of-scope for Phase 1 and doesn’t mention “Fashion” either. Possibly, the design copy was written in a forward-looking way. For Phase 1 delivery, we have two options:
Include all 6 categories on the homepage for completeness of the design system, even if some (Fashion, Gifts) initially have no products. They could link to placeholder collections or simply not be clickable. Or,
Limit the homepage categories to the 4 we know are launching (Home, Beauty, Pantry, Pet) to avoid confusion, and adjust the copy if needed.
We will clarify with the team; but given “Household Essentials” sounds like “Home”, “Food & Beverage” = “Pantry”, etc., likely they intended these to map:
