Household Essentials (could be subset of Home category),
Home & Décor (this also overlaps with Home),
Beauty & Personal Care (Beauty),
Food & Beverage (Pantry),
Fashion & Accessories (future),
Gifts & Stationery (future).
For Phase 1, we’ll implement the four main categories. We can present four or six cards on the homepage depending on final decision. Our code will be such that categories are data-driven. We could, for example, base this section on a manual list of collections defined in theme settings – so the client can add “Fashion” later easily by adding one more item to that list.
Each category card will have an image and category title. We will curate or design images for each (or use product images that represent the category). The category titles will link to the respective collection pages. Also, there is a CTA “Browse All Collections” at the end of that section. This might link to a page that shows all category collections (or all products). If we include all categories on the homepage, this might not be needed; but we could have it link to a “Shop” landing page listing the categories again in case someone skips down.
Brand Spotlight section: Headline “Behind Every Brand, a Story”. This section highlights that each month Lillier features a brand story. CTA “Explore More Stories” – since editorial is phase 2, for now this CTA can link to the Brands page (which is effectively stories about each brand). Or we might create a dedicated “Stories” page on Lillier that is an editorial blog placeholder, but given out-of-scope, better to point to Brands or About for now. Implementation: We could create a “Brand Spotlight” section in the theme that allows the client to feature one brand. For example, a section with:
An image or logo of the featured brand,
A short excerpt (the brand’s intro or current story teaser),
A link to read more (brand’s page).
They can update this monthly by selecting a different brand and updating text. We’ll build it to be easily editable (maybe drop-down of brand collections to choose one, if possible via a product or collection picker in the section settings, and a text area for the excerpt). If we can’t get dynamic data easily (Liquid can fetch a collection’s description), we might just have them fill in the fields manually.
The Lillier Difference section: Headline “More Than a Marketplace”. Body copy explains Lillier’s broader mission beyond checkout. It then lists supporting points:
One cart checkout across multiple brands,
Thousands of verified Black-owned businesses,
Everyday essentials alongside special finds,
Built for scale and sustainability.
We will design this section as a visually appealing list of unique selling points (USPs). Possibly use icons or simple graphics for each point (cart icon, checklist icon, etc.). We can style it as a grid of small highlights or as a column of bullet points with checkmarks. The text can be baked into the theme or made editable (since these points could evolve, making them editable is wise). CTA “Learn More About Lillier” will link to the About page for those who want a deeper explanation.
Closing Section: Headline “Every Cart Is a Contribution” and body copy about how every order helps a Black-owned business grow. CTA “Shop Now”. This is the inspirational closing that drives users to start shopping. We will implement this likely as a full-width banner or overlay text on an image (perhaps an image of joyful customers or entrepreneurs, if available). The “Shop Now” button will link to either the Shop page or simply scroll up to categories; but likely to the Shop (Home category or all products). Summarizing homepage sections:
Hero (Joy theme, CTAs)
Categories grid
Brand Spotlight
“Difference” mission highlights
Closing CTA
We will ensure the homepage is responsive: on mobile, grids will stack vertically (e.g., category cards stack 2 per row or single column, depending on design; text is readable without zoom). We will use CSS media queries or the inherent responsive design of Dawn as a base to adjust layouts.
Category (Collection) Pages: Each of the four main categories (Home, Beauty, Pantry, Pet) will have a collection page template.
We’ll likely have a banner image and description at the top of each collection page to introduce the category (for SEO and UX). The taxonomy doc provides a “Purpose” and “Includes” for each category; we can incorporate some of that language into the collection description text. For example, the “Home” collection description might say it includes kitchen, bath, cleaning, decor items, etc., giving context. We can put that in the collection’s admin description and have the theme display it nicely.
Product Filtering: We will enable filters such as by subcategory (e.g., under Home, filter by Kitchen, Bath, Cleaning, etc.). Shopify 2.0 allows use of product tags or metafields as filter options. We could tag products with their subcategory (like a product in Home category might have tag “Kitchen” or “Bath”) and then set up filter by tag group. Alternatively, we create subcategory collections, but that adds complexity in navigation. Using filters is more elegant:
We’ll ensure each product has a metafield or tag for subcategory according to the taxonomy list etc.
In the theme’s collection template, we activate the filter sidebar (Shopify’s native filter, configured in admin to allow filtering by product tags or a custom metafield for subcategory).
We’ll test that filtering UX. If needed, we can use a third-party filter app, but likely unnecessary for the small scale.
The product grid on collection pages will show product cards with image, title, price, and perhaps the brand name (since that’s important in this context: maybe show “Brand X – Product Name”). We can customize the product card snippet to include vendor name.
If category pages need a certain sort (e.g., by default sort by featured, which the client can control manually by arranging products in collection), we ensure that’s set.
We will also consider pagination or infinite scroll. Given limited SKUs, we might not need pagination at launch, but we can leave the default pagination or a “Load more” button if provided by theme.
Brand Pages: As discussed, each brand will be a collection or page:
If using collections for brands:
We will create a separate collection template for brand collections so that it can display the brand story content nicely. For example, at the top of the page, show the brand’s logo or a banner, a text block for the story, then the product grid of that brand’s products.
The product grid on a brand page might not need filtering (since likely fewer products; but if a brand does have multiple categories, we could filter by category here as well – taxonomy suggests this is an option).
If we need more flexibility for design (like wanting to layout text and images in a richer way than a collection description allows), we could instead use a Page for brand profile and then embed a section that displays products (by a specific collection or dynamic by vendor). However, this would require making a unique page template per brand to know which products to pull – doable, but if number of brands is significant, that’s unwieldy. Using collections is simpler because Shopify automatically ties products to it.
We’ll ensure brand collection pages aren’t visible in main “Shop” filtering or others (they’ll mostly be accessed via Brands page or product links).
Brands Index page: We will create a Page (Brands) with a custom section that lists all brands with a link to each brand page. We might display brand logos or just names alphabetically. For Phase 1, the number of brands might be moderate, so a simple grid of logos with brand names is effective.
If the brand logos or images are not all ready or if visual consistency is an issue, we might list by name initially.
This page can also reiterate Lillier’s ethos, possibly with an intro paragraph (e.g., “Meet the brands” and a note that each brand is independently owned, etc.).
We’ll also consider SEO for brands: ensure each brand page has proper meta tags (Shopify will use collection name for title, etc., which is fine; we can further customize in the theme if needed).
Product Pages: Each product page will follow a template but we must adapt it to highlight the brand:
At the top, product title, price, variant options (if any), add-to-cart button – standard.
We will show the brand name (vendor) near the title or as a subtitle (e.g., “by [Brand Name]”). That brand name should link to the brand page so users can explore the brand’s story and other products.
Product media: images or videos. We’ll implement a gallery/carousel that is mobile-friendly (likely Dawn’s default gallery or a lightbox).
Product description: We have to write or insert whatever descriptions are given by the brands. Possibly, if there’s a consistent format (like listing ingredients for beauty, etc.), ensure styling.
We might also include a section on the product page template for “About the Brand” – a short blurb about the brand to reinforce the story, with a link to read more on the brand page. This can be done via the vendor info:
If brand info is short, we could utilize Shopify’s vendor field plus a static object: e.g., maintain a dictionary of vendor => short description in the theme, but that’s not client-editable.
Better, we could leverage Shopify’s Metafields: define a metafield on the Product or Vendor for “Brand short bio” and fill it for each. However, vendor metafields aren’t first-class in Shopify as of now (Shopify doesn’t have vendor-specific resource to attach metafield to each vendor easily). Alternatively, use a Metaobject (create a “Brand” metaobject with fields including name, bio, etc., link products to it via a metafield). This is advanced, and given phase 1 time, might be overkill. Instead, we can hardcode a solution for now: just ensure the brand pages carry that info, and the product page simply links to the brand page for full story.
So likely: product page shows “Brand: [Name]” as a link, and perhaps not duplicate the story.
Other sections on product page: We might include “Related products” or “More from [Brand]” to encourage browsing. Shopify can show more products from same collection or same vendor. We can include a “You may also like” section showing a few products either from the same category or brand.
We will ensure the product page template is sectioned as well, so we can easily add or remove elements (like if later we want to add reviews, we can drop in Shopify’s review app widget as a section).
About Page: The About content from the copy:
Headline “Own Your Joy” (same as homepage) – likely to use again for consistency.
Subhead “Making it easy to support Black-owned businesses every day.”.
Story text explaining why Lillier exists (problem statement of supporting Black businesses conveniently), how Lillier solves it, and the significance of every purchase.
A section “Why Lillier Exists” in the copy emphasizes the barriers Black-owned businesses face and Lillier’s solution.
“Our Name” story about the founder’s grandmother Lillier Madison and the philosophy of joy and ownership.
Closing line “Joy Is Something You Own” and CTA “Shop the Collection”.
We will break the About page into meaningful sections:
Possibly a hero banner with the headline and subhead.
A narrative section for “Our Story / Why Lillier Exists” – maybe an image of the founder or something alongside text describing the mission.
A smaller section for “Our Name” story, which adds a personal touch (we might style this as a pull-out quote or a distinct blockquote style to highlight the grandmother’s quote “You got to make your own self happy.”).
End with a CTA to shop (to drive users back to buying).
Since About is mostly static content, we can either hardcode it in the page template with the copy or, more flexibly, have it as a regular Page in Shopify with a rich text content that the client can edit. Perhaps we’ll use a hybrid: create the About page in admin and fill it with the main text, then use a custom about template that automatically inserts the “Our Name” callout and maybe an image in a predefined way for design consistency. Other Pages/Features:
Search: Shopify has a search page. We will style the search results page minimally (likely just ensure it uses our typography).
404 page: We should style the 404 to fit branding, possibly with a friendly message.
Account pages (login/register): Shopify allows some customization via language and some minor CSS. We will at least update the text to match tone (maybe a message on login like “Welcome back” or so). But this is minor.
Checkout: We can’t deeply customize checkout on basic Shopify, but we will upload the logo and set brand color for the checkout accents so it feels on-brand. Also include the shipping note again in checkout info if possible (Shopify allows adding a message on the cart or an info page before checkout; we could include “remember items ship separately” in the cart page as earlier).
Analytics and SEO: We will ensure that both sites have baseline SEO set:
For Squarespace, we’ll fill in SEO descriptions for each page (Squarespace has SEO settings per page) and connect Google Analytics (per the deliverables).
For Shopify, we’ll set up Google Analytics or Shopify’s analytics, Facebook Pixel, etc., as provided by the client. We’ll also generate sitemap, and ensure things like alt text on images are added. The theme will be coded with good HTML semantics (proper headings, etc.) for SEO.
Accessibility: We’ll follow accessibility best practices, especially on Shopify where we control code: alt text for images, proper aria labels for icons, keyboard navigability for menus, sufficient color contrast for text (we’ll verify the design colors against contrast guidelines and adjust if needed to be AA compliant).
Unified Brand Feel Across Sites: Finally, we will verify that when a user transitions from Cupcakes+Broccoli to Lillier (if that’s expected, since they are separate but related brands), the experience is consistent. Shared visual cues will include:
Color scheme: The same or complementary brand colors on both (e.g., if the primary color is a certain green or orange, both use it for buttons and links).
Typography: Using the same font families on both sites for headings and body, if licensing allows. If not, choose very similar styles.
Graphic elements: If the design uses certain shapes, iconography, or illustration styles, incorporate those on both.
Tone of content: Both sites’ copy already has a cohesive voice. We will ensure UI text (like button labels, error messages) align in tone (e.g., friendly and clear).
We might also cross-link the sites subtly: e.g., Lillier’s about page or footer might mention the connection to Cupcakes+Broccoli (if appropriate). Not a requirement, but something to consider if the brand wants a sense of ecosystem.
Before launch, we’ll do a final UI polish pass on both sites side by side to ensure things like the treatment of CTAs or form fields feel like part of the same family.
With the strategies defined for each site, we can now outline a build sequencing plan to implement Phase 1 efficiently, and provide an action-oriented checklist.
Build & Implementation Plan (Phased Sequence)
To execute this project efficiently, we will proceed in stages, some in parallel, to meet the target timeline (the outline mentioned a target around March 1 for Phase 1). Below is the sequencing plan with key milestones (M1–M4 from the outline) and tasks:
Milestone 1: Project Kickoff & Planning (M1) – Confirm all pages and requirements, gather assets.
Milestone 2: System & Core Templates implemented (M2) – Both sites have design system and main page structures in place (without final content).
Milestone 3: Content Insertion & Polish (M3) – All final copy and media placed, membership and products configured, sites nearly ready.
Milestone 4: QA, Launch Prep & Launch (M4) – Testing complete, revisions done, sites live.
We will utilize a staging approach: for Squarespace, we’ll likely build on the live site but not make the new pages public until launch (using “Not linked” section or a duplicate site if needed for safe staging). For Shopify, we will develop on an unpublished theme or development store, then publish at launch time.
Phase 1 Action Checklist
Below is an extensive, task-oriented checklist of actions for Phase 1, broken down by area:
Project Setup & Access
 Access Credentials: Obtain admin access to the existing Squarespace site (Cupcakes+Broccoli) and the Shopify account (Lillier). Ensure the Squarespace site is on a plan that supports Member Areas and custom code injection, and the Shopify store is on a plan that supports the needed features (likely Basic or higher for Shopify 2.0 and use of plugins if any).
 Local Dev Environment (Shopify): Install Shopify CLI and authenticate to the Lillier dev store. Pull down the base theme (e.g., Dawn) into a local repository. Set up Git for version control.
 Design Assets: Collect all design materials from design partners – style guide (colors, fonts), logos, icons, any UI kit or component specs, and image assets (photos for hero banners, product images from brands, etc.). If any asset is missing, mark a placeholder and request it.
 Content Confirmation: Gather final or near-final copy for both sites (the provided copy docs). Clarify any ambiguous navigation or category issues (e.g., confirm which categories to show on Lillier homepage, confirm if any C+B content is members-only beyond obvious cases). Make a final list of all pages to be built for each site.
 Kickoff Meeting: (If not already done) Meet with client/designers to walk through the plan, ensuring alignment on scope. Review any potential platform constraints (for example, ensure they understand Squarespace limitations and are okay with them). Confirm the membership offering details (free vs paid tiers, pricing, etc.) needed for C+B.
 Create Backup/Clone (Squarespace): Since we’ll be modifying an existing Squarespace site, create a backup or clone. If possible, enable Developer Mode in a test environment (though 7.1 doesn’t allow Developer Mode, as an alternative we can duplicate pages or spin up a trial site for experimentation). At minimum, export the site content or ensure we can revert changes if something goes awry.
Global Design System Implementation
 Squarespace Style Setup: In the Squarespace site’s Design panel, set the global fonts and colors according to the brand guide. Configure font families and sizes for each heading level and body text. Set site background and text colors, button styles (so all Buttons share the correct styling by default). If required, add custom CSS for any styles that aren’t configurable (e.g., if custom font via @font-face, load it in Code Injection with CSS, or if letter-spacing on headings needs to match exactly).
 Shopify Theme Base & Settings: In the theme code, define the color scheme and typography. Likely update theme.scss/CSS with brand colors, create variables. Add the brand font (if a Google Font, include via Shopify settings; if custom, add to assets and import in CSS). Configure settings_schema.json with theme settings for colors (primary, secondary, background, text, etc.) and typography scale if needed, so the client can tweak in editor if ever necessary.
 Shared Assets: Add logos to both sites. In Squarespace, upload the logo image and set it in the header. In Shopify, add logo in header section settings. Ensure retina versions if needed. Also, add any favicon or social sharing images to both.
 Verify Mobile Styles: Early on, test a sample page in both environments on a small screen to ensure fonts aren’t too large or colors have enough contrast. Adjust base sizes if needed for readability on mobile.
Structural Setup
 Squarespace Pages & Navigation: Create the page structure in Squarespace:
Home, About, Work With Amanda, Learn, Stories (Blog), Blind Dinners, Membership pages. Use the exact titles and order as per nav spec. Put them under Main Navigation in the correct order. Add the external “Login” link in the nav (Squarespace may allow a special login link if Member Areas is on; if not, we’ll use a URL like /member-login or whatever Squarespace uses for login).
For now, keep these new pages disabled or not linked (or behind an under-construction page) so the public doesn’t see them until launch. We might keep the current site intact and build new pages concurrently.
Set up the Stories as a Blog Page type. Configure the URL slug (e.g., /stories). Add a couple of example posts to populate it.
If any page (like “Work With Amanda”) should be a different type (maybe a Cover page?), decide and create accordingly. But likely all can be standard pages except Stories which is a blog.
 Squarespace Member Area: Enable Member Areas in Squarespace settings
. Create a new Member Area (via Pages panel, click “+” next to Member Areas)
. Name it appropriately (e.g., “Members” or “Community”). Choose a starter layout if prompted (we’ll likely end up customizing anyway). Under that Member Area section, create any pages that are specifically for members-only content (e.g., maybe a “Premium Resources” page, or “Office Hours Info” page, etc., based on what needs gating).
If all/most content is intended to be free except a few premium pages, we’ll only put those premium ones in the member area. For example, perhaps general “Learn” pages are public but actual resource downloads or premium AI access page is members only.
Configure pricing plans for the Member Area: Set up one free plan (if needed) and one paid plan (“Premium”), with details like price, billing interval (likely monthly or yearly), description of benefits. This also sets up the checkout for membership.
Copy the URL or slug for the login page that Squarespace provides (for linking our Login button).
 Shopify Collections & Products: In Shopify admin, create the collections for:
Home, Beauty, Pantry, Pet (top categories). For each, add a description (using content from taxonomy for SEO/context) and a representative image if available.
If including “Fashion” and “Gifts” for future, we can create those collections now but maybe hide them from navigation until needed.
Create a “New Arrivals” collection (automatic, sort by created date or tag “New”).
Create brand collections for each brand that will be in Phase 1. Use brand name as title, add a short description or placeholder (will fill in later with actual brand story), and maybe a logo image if provided.
Add a handful of sample products to the store to begin theme development. At least one product per category and a couple per brand. Use placeholder images (or real ones if available early), a dummy price, assign to appropriate collections (both its category and its brand collection). Set the vendor field as the brand name (important for linking products to brand).
If any product has variants (like size or color), set up one example to test how the theme handles it.
We may not have all actual product data until later; but this step is critical so we can see product grids and design accordingly.
 Shopify Navigation: Go to Navigation in Shopify admin:
Main menu: Add “Home” (link to home page), “Shop” (we might add as an item that is just a placeholder or link to “all products” or first category), “Brands” (link to Brands page, which we’ll create as a Page), “About” (link to about page).
For “Shop” item, add submenu items: Home, Beauty, Pantry, Pet (each linking to the corresponding collection). If possible, make “Shop” itself unclickable by setting as # or just not linking anywhere (Shopify doesn’t allow empty link easily, but can use # for now).
Footer menu: Add standard links like FAQ (if we plan one), Contact, Terms, etc. Since not mentioned, perhaps at least add Privacy and Terms pages (we can generate boilerplate for those or leave placeholders).
 Brands Page (Shopify): Create a Page in Shopify called “Brands”. We will not put content in it yet; instead, we’ll assign a custom template to it later that will list the brands. But having it in place allows linking the nav.
Core Template Development
 Squarespace Homepage Layout: Start building the C+B homepage in Squarespace:
Insert a new page (if not already from template) for Home. Use section editor:
Add a section for Hero. Configure background (color or image), add heading, subheading, buttons as per content. Adjust style (font sizes, spacing) either in section settings or override with CSS if needed.
Add subsequent sections: “What This Is” (with headline and body text), “AI Concierge” (with example prompts). Possibly combine these two in one section with two columns or keep separate for each idea – depends on design. Use text blocks for copy and style inline elements like the example questions (maybe italic or bullet list).
Add “Learn” teaser section (headline + body + CTA button).
Add “Work With Amanda” teaser (similar structure).
Add “Blind Dinners” teaser.
Add “Membership” teaser (with two CTAs as given).
Add final “Closing” section (centered text and CTA).
For each section, use Squarespace’s options to match design (like background colors or images, padding, alignment). Where needed, save the section as a reusable one (e.g., the final CTA might be saved as “Closing CTA section” if we’ll reuse on other pages).
Insert any relevant images (if design has illustrations or photos in between text, use Image Blocks or background images in sections).
Do not publish any obviously incomplete content. If the homepage is replacing an existing one, we may keep it unlinked until launch and use a temporary homepage.
Keep notes of any limitations encountered (e.g., if cannot put two buttons side by side, perhaps use a button block and a text link as secondary, or custom HTML).
 Squarespace Other Pages: Build out structure for key pages:
About: Create a section with the main narrative (from copy). Possibly include an image of Amanda. Format the text nicely (maybe pull quotes for “They come for clarity.” line, etc., if in design).
Work With Amanda: Section with hero (headline from copy) and text. Possibly break into bullets or sub-sections if the content is long (the copy reads as a few paragraphs).
Learn: Section with hero (if any) and intro text. We might list resource categories or simply have a CTA to the resources library. If the plan is to eventually list resources, maybe include a summary block of latest “Stories” or some placeholder.
Blind Dinners: Section with descriptive text (maybe photo of a dinner table for ambience).
Membership: This page might have multiple sections: one to pitch overall membership value (similar to what’s on home), one to outline tier differences (perhaps a comparison table or a two-column list of Free vs Premium benefits). Use toggles or accordion if a lot of info per tier. Ensure there is a strong CTA here as well (Join now button which triggers membership signup).
Stories (Blog) List: Style the blog list. Choose between Squarespace’s summary block vs default blog page layout. We can use the blog page’s built-in layout options (grid or list, with or without images). Configure it to look clean. Possibly show the full text of latest story or just excerpts. Use the copy for Stories page intro as a guiding text at top.
Blog Post template: Ensure the styling for headings, body text, and images in a story is on-brand (we might set a custom style for the post title to use the same style as other page titles).
Note: For now, fill each page with the WIP copy. Actual final content might tweak but this gives us a base to test design and spacing.
 Squarespace Custom Code Elements: Implement any needed custom code:
Add Code Injection in header or footer for site-wide scripts: e.g., integrate Google Analytics tracking code (if account info provided).
Write a script for the AI Concierge modal:
Perhaps embed a third-party chat widget if one is chosen. If none exists yet, create a dummy modal (a hidden div with some text input and close button) just to demonstrate the flow. Use a Code Block on homepage to include a trigger button and a script that toggles the modal’s visibility. Ensure CSS covers full-screen overlay and proper z-index.
We’ll refine this when the actual AI backend is ready, but at least structure is in place.
Custom CSS: as we build pages, accumulate any extra CSS needed into the Custom CSS panel. Organize it by page or component (comment sections clearly, e.g., /* Homepage Hero styles */). For instance, if we need to adjust the spacing after each section or style the membership comparison table, do it here.
 Shopify Theme Coding: In parallel, start coding the Shopify theme structure:
Header: Edit the header section (sections/header.liquid). Implement the logo placement, navigation include (Shopify’s main-menu), and icons for cart/account. If a mega-menu or dropdown is needed for the Shop menu, implement dropdown markup (Dawn might have a ready approach for nested menus). Ensure “Brands” and “About” appear. Possibly include a favicon or small tagline in the header if design calls for it (not likely).
Footer: Edit sections/footer.liquid. Add necessary menus (Shopify often has menu schema for footer links). Include social icons if any. Include the tagline or a short about blurb if desired.
Homepage (index.json): Construct the JSON template with the sections:
Hero section: Create sections/hero-banner.liquid (or adapt Dawn’s existing hero if available). Add schema fields for heading, subheading, text, background image, and up to 3 CTA buttons (with link and label). We will bind these to the copy provided. For now, set default text as per the WIP content. Make sure it’s flexible (if they want to change headline later).
Category grid section: Create a custom sections/home-categories.liquid. This section will display a set of collections. In schema, we can use a collection list setting (Shopify allows repeating blocks in schema of type collection). For example, allow up to 6 collection blocks, each with an image (we can default to collection image) and title (collection title). If we want to override titles (like use “Household Essentials” instead of the collection’s actual name “Home”), we could allow custom text – but likely better to keep them in sync with collection names for consistency. For now, we use collection titles.
In the template, loop through the collection blocks and output an image box with the collection’s image and title, linking to the collection URL.
Default state: add the 4 main collections and potentially 2 future ones as disabled blocks or commented out.
Include the section heading “Shop by Category” and intro text from copy in the section’s static content or schema.
Put a configurable “Browse All Collections” link at bottom (schema text field for link label and a link selector).
Brand Spotlight section: sections/brand-spotlight.liquid. Schema: maybe allow selecting a specific Brand collection (or a specific product or metaobject if that’s easier – collection is fine if each brand is a collection). Also fields for spotlight title, description, and link text. Implementation: display the brand’s image or a featured product image, the provided text (or we could dynamically pull the collection’s description as the story snippet), and a link to that brand’s page.
We’ll set default to whatever brand is planned first (if known). If not, keep generic.
Lillier Difference section: sections/lillier-difference.liquid. This can be mostly static content from the copy since it’s describing the platform’s benefits. Schema fields: maybe just text for each bullet point in case they want to tweak phrasing. We can also code the points directly from copy to save time, but giving editability is nice. We’ll include the heading “More Than a Marketplace” and the bullet list (structured as nice icons or checkmarks). Possibly each point can be a schema list of text items (makes editing easier if they want to add or remove points in future).
Closing CTA section: Perhaps reuse a generic section or create sections/home-closing.liquid. Schema: heading text, body text, button link. Default values from copy (“Every Cart Is a Contribution” etc.).
Once these custom sections are created, include them in templates/index.json in the desired order with preset content. Shopify’s CLI might allow us to generate these easily. We will then open the theme editor preview to verify layout.
Collection Template: Create a templates/collection.json (Shopify 2.0 uses JSON for default). We’ll likely use one template for all top-level categories and a variant for brand collections if needed:
In collection.json, include the section main-collection-banner (we might code a section that shows the collection image and description nicely).
Include the default product-grid section (Dawn provides a section for the product grid and filtering). We might utilize that or write our own if customizing the grid. Possibly easier is to use Dawn’s main-collection-product-grid which comes with built-in filter and sort controls, then style via CSS as needed to match design. We’ll test that.
Save this as the default template for all collections.
If brand pages need a different layout (like story at top plus products), consider creating an alternate template collection.brand.json. That template could use a different section which prints the collection description in a bigger way (for brand story) and maybe hides filters (since filtering by category on a single brand could be a nice-to-have but not crucial day1).
Assign brand collections to the brand template if we do this.
Product Template: Edit templates/product.json. Use Dawn’s default sections (product info, product recommendations, etc.) as baseline:
Ensure the product page shows the vendor. In Dawn’s main-product.liquid, add the vendor name linked to the brand page (we can construct link to /collections/<vendor-handle> if using collections for brand, or /pages/<brand-page> if not).
Check Dawn’s section for product recommendation; it typically shows related items (from same collection). That might suffice for “You may also like”. If needed, adjust logic to show more from same brand or category.
Include a section or block for product description that splits into tabs if needed (some theme do that for long descriptions or additional info). Probably not needed unless product info is large.
Add a static note on product page if desired, like “Shipping: Items from this brand may ship separately.” But since that’s repeated for all, maybe skip to avoid clutter.
Brands Page Template: We will create a custom liquid template for the Brands index:
templates/page.brands.liquid (with corresponding page.brands.json if using JSON template). In it, we include a section (or just hardcode within the template) that iterates over all brand collections.
One approach: If all brand collections share a tag like “Brand” or are all in a manual “Brands” collection list, we can fetch those. Shopify’s Liquid can’t directly query all collections except by type of rules. Alternatively, we maintain a menu “Brands” with all brand links and iterate through that menu in Liquid to display each brand. Actually, using a menu might be simplest: We can ask the client to maintain the Brands list via Navigation. However, duplicating data (collections and menu) is not ideal but acceptable.
To keep it simple: We may use the all_products global to get vendor names (but that requires some trick and is not straightforward in Liquid loops).
Given time, we might hardcode a list for now and note to improve later. But since they likely won’t add brands frequently without a developer initially, a hardcoded list (or a list in the page content) is okay for Phase 1. We will however strongly prefer a dynamic solution:
Perhaps create a collection of collections via the custom list-collections.liquid that Dawn has for “all collections” and filter that by those that are brand collections. If we name all brand collections with a prefix like “Brand: X”, maybe we can filter by name. Or put them all under a smart collection category which isn’t possible in Shopify.
Possibly easiest: Use JavaScript to fetch collection listings via Shopify’s Storefront API or sections. But that’s too heavy.
For now, plan: manually code brand entries for each known brand in page.brands.liquid – each entry showing brand image, name, maybe one-liner, and link. Leave instructions that to add a new brand, one must edit this template (or we’ll update it as developer).
Alternatively, store brand info in a JSON file or metafield. But that requires code to parse if not integrated.
We’ll decide on the least brittle approach given the time.
About Page: Create templates/page.about.json or similar. We can use the theme editor to craft the about page with existing sections:
Possibly reuse the Hero section for a banner at top (“Own Your Joy” again).
Use a rich text section for the story content (Shopify has a section type for custom content or we can create one).
Use an image-with-text section to display the anecdote about Lillier Madison (maybe an image of her if available, with the quote text overlay or caption).
Conclude with a CTA section (perhaps reuse the closing CTA style urging “Shop the Collection”).
If easier, we might not need a custom template file; we can just use the default page template and configure sections via the theme editor for the About page, since in OS 2.0 each page can have its own section layout. That may be sufficient: open “About” in theme editor and build it from the sections we created (hero, text, etc.). This keeps content easily editable by merchant as well (text would be set in those sections or in the page’s content).
 Liquid & Schema QA: Go through each Liquid file and JSON to fix any syntax errors. Use theme check or similar tools. Then preview on a development theme:
Load the Shopify dev site preview. Check that homepage renders all sections correctly with our placeholder content. Tweak spacing, responsive behavior (shrink browser or use Chrome dev tools for mobile width).
Click through a category page: see products, test filters (enable filtering for that collection in admin by selecting filter options). Adjust filter styles if needed (Shopify might render a basic sidebar; ensure it fits brand – if needed, apply CSS to style checkboxes or dropdowns).
Open a product page: verify vendor link goes to brand collection and works. Check image gallery and add-to-cart. We’ll use Shopify’s preview without actual purchase since store likely password-protected until launch.
Check the Brands page: ensure all brand links work (point to brand collections). If any brand has no products yet, ensure page still loads (maybe hide if empty).
Check About page layout in preview, adjust if something is off.
Client-Side Features & Integrations
 Membership Purchase Flow Test (Squarespace): In Squarespace, do a dry run: click Join on Membership page, see the sign-up flow. (We might need to actually simulate a purchase – we can create a 100% discount code for ourselves if setting a paid plan, or set a $0 price for testing, because we may not want to actually charge a card in dev. Or use Squarespace’s member area preview mode if available). Check that after “purchase” we can access a member-only page. Check the email notification Squarespace sends to new members (so we can customize it if needed with branding).
 E-commerce Settings (Shopify): Configure basic shop settings:
Set up payment gateway in test mode (or at least enable Bogus gateway) to test checkout if needed.
Configure shipping rates (e.g., flat rate or free shipping threshold) just to simulate the flow. (We’ll eventually get final shipping setup from client).
Add shop policies (we can paste provided or placeholder text into Shopify’s legal settings so that the footer links to Terms/Privacy pages are populated).
If the client has any apps to install (for email marketing popup, reviews, etc.), integrate them now and ensure they don’t visually break the theme. For example, if using an email capture app, set it up and test that the popup shows and can be closed, etc.
 Analytics and SEO Integration:
Squarespace: Insert Google Analytics Tracking ID or Google Tag Manager snippet via Code Injection (if client provided). Enable built-in SEO settings like generating sitemap, setting site title format, etc. Fill page descriptions for SEO using the copy (maybe take first sentence of each page).
Shopify: Add Google Analytics UA or GA4 ID in preferences, Facebook Pixel if given. Check that theme has the {{ content_for_header }} in <head> and {{ content_for_footer }} before </body> – Dawn does – to ensure all tracking scripts (and app scripts) load. Set store meta title and meta description in Preferences. For each page/collection/product, make sure the SEO meta fields are filled reasonably (Shopify auto-generates from content, we can adjust important ones like homepage and about).
 Performance Considerations: Optimize any large images (ensure hero images are compressed, use correct dimensions). Shopify will auto generate different sizes for img URL, and Squarespace does similar, but we should still use appropriately sized images for banners. Remove any unused sections or code before launch to avoid bloat.
 Cross-Site Consistency Check: Compare elements on both sites:
Do buttons have the same shape and color? Adjust CSS if one is off.
Are headings in similar style and tone? E.g., if one site uses all-caps for H2, the other should too if that’s brand style.
Check that both have favicon set (perhaps the same favicon if they’re sister sites).
If any content overlaps (not much except maybe explaining mission), ensure wording is aligned or at least not contradictory.
Content Population & Review
 Import Final Copy: Once the client provides final edited copy (if the WIP docs update), replace any placeholder text on both sites with final text. This includes product descriptions for actual products on Lillier, the final list of bullet points or CTA texts if they changed, etc. Double-check for typos or inconsistencies during this process.
 Add Imagery: Upload final images:
For Squarespace: add any illustrations or photos on pages (e.g., maybe an image of Amanda on About, or decorative images on Home). Use high-quality but optimized images. Set appropriate focal points in Squarespace so responsive cropping looks good.
For Shopify: upload product photos for all launch products (client will need to provide these). Ensure each product has at least one good image, ideally more. Also upload brand logos or lifestyle images for brand pages if provided. Add them to the brand collections or wherever needed in theme (the Brand spotlight might have a specific image to use).
Hero banners: ensure we have a compelling hero image for Lillier (if they want one behind “Own Your Joy”). If none provided, we might choose a color background or a pattern to keep it clean.
 Configure Products (Final): Enter real product data in Shopify:
Add all products slated for launch, with proper titles, descriptions, pricing, SKU, stock, vendor, collections, tags for subcategories, etc. This might be done via CSV if many – the client might do it, but as developers, we might assist or at least spot-check one per category.
Ensure each product is assigned to exactly one of the main category collections (to enforce the taxonomy rule).
Set up collection sort orders or manually merchandise the category pages if required (e.g., maybe feature certain products at top).
 Membership Content (Squarespace): Fill in any gated content pages:
If there are premium resource articles or downloads ready, add them to the member area pages. For example, if “premium resources library” has some PDFs or templates, upload those and link them.
If office hours schedule is to be displayed for premium, put that info on the appropriate page.
Essentially, ensure that when a premium member logs in, there is actually valuable content to see, not just a blank page. We might create a welcome page for members: “Welcome, here’s how to get started” etc.
 Internal Linking: Go through both sites and link everything:
On C+B, ensure every CTA button or link points to the correct page or section (use anchor links for Membership page comparison section if needed).
On Lillier, link homepage CTAs to their targets (New Arrivals collection, etc.). Link any “Learn More” to About, “Explore Brands” to Brands page.
In any text where there’s a reference to another section (e.g., in About, “Every purchase is a vote for ownership” might not need a link, but if any text says “See our brands” make it a link).
Check that no link is broken (especially, if we changed any page slugs for readability, update nav and links accordingly).
 Client Review Session: Walk the client (and design partners) through the nearly finished sites:
Gather feedback on visual fidelity to design. Tweak styles if colors or spacing are slightly off.
If client finds editing something confusing in the CMS, adjust to simplify (for example, if editing a certain text requires digging into theme code, perhaps add a schema field to expose it).
Confirm the membership sign-up pricing and text are correct (maybe legal needs adding that it's recurring billing, etc.).
Incorporate any content changes they request (they might reword some section after seeing it live).
Ensure legal compliance: e.g., if there’s a membership, have Terms of Service accessible; if collecting emails, have a privacy policy linked.
Quality Assurance (QA) & Testing
 Cross-Browser Testing: View both sites on latest versions of Chrome, Firefox, Safari, and Edge. Also test on iOS Safari and an Android Chrome. Check for any layout breaking or font issues. Fix any browser-specific problems (like flexbox issues or anything).
 Responsive Testing (Detailed): Use dev tools or devices to test at various breakpoints:
For C+B: ensure navigation collapses to a mobile menu nicely (Squarespace handles this automatically with its templates). Check that long headings or CTAs aren’t cut off on small screens. Verify the AI modal is usable on mobile (e.g., maybe it needs a full-screen overlay style on mobile).
For Lillier: test the homepage sections on mobile (category grid might turn into a swipe carousel or stack; ensure text remains readable). Test the collection filter on mobile – Shopify will likely show a “Filter” button that toggles a sidebar or dropdown; verify that works and is styled properly. Test the product page on mobile (image gallery swipe, add-to-cart usability).
If any sections look awkward on small screens, add responsive CSS rules to adjust (like maybe fewer columns, smaller text, hide certain decorative images on mobile, etc.).
 Performance Check: Run a Google Lighthouse audit or Shopify’s theme analyzer:
Aim for good scores on performance. If any large JS or image slows it down, consider lazy-loading it. For instance, ensure that any Youtube/Vimeo embeds (if any) are deferred or use a preview image.
For Shopify, check liquid rendering speed (if we used heavy loops, but with small product count it should be fine). Also consider enabling Shopify’s built-in image lazy loading (Dawn does that).
On Squarespace, not much we can do beyond optimizing images and not adding too many third-party scripts. The AI script might be one – ensure it loads when needed (maybe on clicking the button) rather than on page load if possible.
 Functional Testing:
Forms: Submit any contact or newsletter forms to ensure emails get sent or stored (Squarespace forms to email or storage, Shopify newsletter form to customer list).
Membership purchase: In Squarespace, do a final end-to-end test: create a test account, purchase membership (maybe with a 100% off code or a test gateway if available). Ensure the content unlocks. Then cancel that membership (to test if cancellation messaging is clear).
E-commerce flow: In Shopify, simulate a purchase:
Add a couple of products (from different brands) to cart. Verify cart page shows both, and the messaging like “multiple brands one checkout” is visible if we put it there.
Proceed to checkout (maybe use a test payment or just go to the payment screen and then abort if live gateway). Ensure shipping options show properly for multiple items, and any additional info (like “ships separately” could be reiterated on checkout page as static text if needed).
If possible, place an order in test mode to ensure order confirmation and emails work. Then check in admin that order split by location if multiple, etc. (If multi-origin shipping is configured, test that shipping rates appear correctly. If not using that, ensure just one shipping entry).
Search and Navigation: Use the search box to find a product or brand, see that results are reasonable. Click through the navigation links on both sites to ensure all go to the right place.
Account login (Shopify): Create a customer account via the storefront (if we enabled accounts optional). Log out, log in, see that nothing odd happens (the account area will be basic but at least it works).
Edge Cases: Try weird scenarios:
Very long product title or very long brand name – does it break layout? (We can truncate or CSS wrap as needed.)
No products in a category – does the page handle “No products” elegantly? We might add a message like “Coming soon” on empty collections.
Member tries to access without logging in – ensure the Squarespace paywall appears as expected.
Rapidly resizing screen or using an iPad – check if design still looks good on intermediate tablet sizes.
 Fix Bugs: For any issues found in testing, fix them. This could involve adjusting CSS, tweaking section schema, or even a quick script fix. Re-test those fixes.
 Content Proofreading: Read through all text on the live site with fresh eyes or have a colleague do it. Ensure formatting is correct (no weird line breaks, lists display properly, special characters show correctly, etc.). Check that capitalization is consistent (e.g., some headings in copy might be in Title Case, make sure style doesn’t accidentally uppercase everything unless intended).
Launch Preparation
 Squarespace Launch Prep: If we built new pages hidden, now is time to go live:
Double-check that the site’s domain is correctly connected and no “Under Construction” remains. (If we want to coordinate launch timing, Squarespace doesn’t have a staging push, so we might either manually swap homepage content at a low-traffic time or use a “Site Availability” password and then remove it at launch moment.)
Connect domain and SSL: ensure the custom domain (if different from squarespace.com placeholder) is set up with proper DNS, and SSL is on.
Remove any test content/users: e.g., remove our test member account (so it doesn’t count toward anything or appear in lists).
Ensure member area is set to “Active” and payment gateway for membership (Squarespace will use Stripe likely) is configured if needed for the paid tier.
Finalize any SEO/social settings: set the social share image for homepage etc., so when the site is shared it looks nice.
 Shopify Launch Prep:
Make sure the store status is switched from password-protected to public when ready. (Don’t forget to disable the storefront password on launch day so people can access the site.)
If the store was on a temporary domain (myshopify.com), set up the custom domain (e.g., lillier.com) in Shopify and switch primary domain, confirm it’s working.
Ensure all payment and tax settings are correctly input and go through a live transaction with a real card (perhaps a $1 product or a test item) to ensure payment flows to the correct account.
Uninstall or turn off any dev/test apps or code. E.g., remove the theme preview bar, disable “theme watching” from CLI.
Switch to the newly developed theme as the live theme. (We will have been previewing it; now we publish it.)
Quickly smoke-test the live theme after publish (just in case something behaves differently on live).

For squarespace 7.1 = how do we code custom sections that we can save as discrete chunks, to later be reused and edited within the UI visually? Alternatively, if this is NOT possible at all in 7.1, then how can we use a different version of squarespace where this is possible?
ChatGPT said:

Thought for 3m 50s
The blunt truth about Squarespace 7.1 “custom sections”
In Squarespace 7.1, you cannot build a truly custom, code-defined “section type” that shows up in the editor with nice editable fields the way Shopify sections do. Squarespace supports client-side custom code via Code Blocks / Code Injection / Custom CSS, but that code does not become a first-class, reusable “component with visual controls.” 

What you can do (and what I recommend for this project): build a reusable Section Library using Saved Sections, where the layout + styling rules come from your global CSS/system and the content stays in normal Squarespace blocks so the client can edit visually. 

Best approach in Squarespace 7.1: “Saved Sections + System CSS + Client-editable blocks”
Why this matches your goals
You only do the “drag/drop” work once per section pattern, then reuse forever via Saved Sections.

The client edits copy/images/buttons in the normal UI, without touching code.

You still get a code-forward workflow because the “design system” lives in global CSS + light JS, not in endless per-page manual tweaks. 

Key constraint to design around
Saved Sections are snapshots. If you later improve the section template, it won’t automatically update every place it’s been used—you’d update instances manually (or re-insert). 

So: use Saved Sections for layout patterns, and use centralized content mechanisms (Collections/Summary blocks) for anything you’ll want to update globally.

Step-by-step: how to build “discrete chunks” that are reusable and UI-editable
1) Create a “Section Library” page (your internal component catalog)
Create a page in Not Linked called something like “Section Library”.

On that page, build your 10–15 core patterns (hero, editorial split, CTA, card grid, story list, etc.).

This page becomes your single source of truth for structure.

(“Not Linked” is perfect for internal build pages you don’t want in nav.) 

2) Build sections using native blocks as the editable surface
For each section pattern:

Use Text / Image / Button / Gallery blocks for everything the client should edit.

Avoid Code Blocks unless you truly need a micro-interaction that Squarespace can’t do.

This is the critical trick: content stays native, so client edits are painless.

3) Save each section as a Saved Section
Squarespace 7.1 supports saving a section to reuse elsewhere. 

After you’ve built a section, use the Save Section flow (the “heart/save” action in the section controls). 

Name them like a real component system:

C+B / Hero – Editorial

C+B / CTA – Join Community

C+B / Grid – 3 Cards

etc.

4) Create pages by assembling from your Saved Sections catalog
This is where you stop doing repetitive layout work:

New page = add page → insert saved sections → adjust copy/images.

For you, it becomes “composition,” not “designing.”

5) Put the “design system” in global styles, not per-section fiddling
Use:

Site Styles for global typography/colors

Custom CSS for tokens (spacing scale, button rules, card rules)

Code Injection only when you need behavior (tiny JS) 

This is how you stay “programmer-first” while still building in Squarespace.

Make it more reusable: give sections stable hooks (without brittle random IDs)
Squarespace sections have internal data-section-id values, but those are not friendly to maintain across reuse. A better option in 7.1 now is built-in section Anchor Links: you can assign an anchor slug per section in the editor. 

Practical use:

Assign slugs like cnb-hero-editorial, cnb-cta-join, etc.

You can then write CSS/JS that targets those anchors (how exactly it appears in DOM can vary, but the anchor mechanism is official and stable for linking). 

This gives you a repeatable “component naming” habit that doesn’t rely on hunting random IDs.

Where you shouldn’t use Saved Sections (and what to do instead)
Because Saved Sections don’t sync globally 
, anything that must update everywhere should be built using centralized content features, for example:

Stories as a blog/collection feed + a Summary block on the Home page

Any “Featured” grids that should auto-populate from a collection

This is how you avoid updating the same content in 6 places.

If you insist on “real coded components with editor controls”: the 7.0/Developer Platform reality
Squarespace’s Developer Platform (template-level code access) is Version 7.0 only. 

But:

You cannot downgrade an existing 7.1 site to 7.0. 

And Squarespace itself recommends using 7.1 for new builds because new features focus there. 

So the only way to use 7.0 dev mode would be:

Start an entirely new 7.0 site on a 7.0 template, rebuild, migrate content, move domain.
For this project (existing C+B already on 7.1 + “light membership”), that’s almost certainly the wrong tradeoff.

My recommendation for C+B (7.1)
Stay on Squarespace 7.1 and do this:

Build a Section Library page (Not Linked)

Create 10–15 Saved Sections that represent the whole site’s building blocks

Put your “system” into global CSS tokens + rules

Use collections/summary blocks for anything that needs global updating

Document for the client: “To add a page: create page → insert sections A/B/C → swap images/text.”

If you want, paste a screenshot of the current C+B editor view (or tell me whether it’s Fluid Engine everywhere), and I’ll propose a concrete Phase 1 Section Library list tailored to your actual pages (Home/Learn/Services/Community/Stories) and the “content-forward + light membership” goals.

ChatGPT said:
Yep — makes complete sense. The winning pattern in Squarespace 7.1 for a “programmer-first, client-editable” build is:

Let Squarespace store the content (text/images/buttons) in normal blocks, but make the site look and behave like your custom system via global CSS (and small JS).
You touch the visual editor only to (1) place blocks in roughly the right order, and (2) save sections for reuse.

Below is a step-by-step plan that gets you maximum styling in code and minimum UI work.

The core strategy
What lives in code (you)
Type scale, colors, spacing system, grid rules

Button styles, form styles, nav styles, card styles

“Section components” via CSS classes + predictable structure

Responsive behavior (mobile-first)

Small enhancements (accordion, tab switching, sticky CTA, etc.) via JS

What lives in the Squarespace UI (client)
Edit headline text, body text

Swap images

Change button labels + links

Add a new page by inserting prebuilt “saved sections” and editing content

Step 0 — Choose the minimum-work editing mode
Use Fluid Engine, but treat it like a dumb container.

You’re not “designing” with it.

You’re only dropping in blocks and letting your CSS do the layout.

In each section, you’ll set blocks to full-width (or a simple consistent width) and stop touching pixel placement.

If the site currently has classic editor sections mixed in, that’s fine — the method still works.

Step 1 — Establish a “Design System” in one place (global code)
You want one place to control everything:

Put this in ONE of these (pick one and stick with it)
Design → Custom CSS (best for CSS-only system)

Settings → Advanced → Code Injection → HEADER (best if you want to load one external CSS file + JS file)

My recommendation: Code Injection with external files, so you can version-control your system.

Structure
Create a tiny “theme” repo locally:

/cnb-theme
  /dist
    cnb.css
    cnb.js
  /src
    tokens.css
    base.css
    components.css
    sections.css
    utilities.css
    cnb.js
Then paste cnb.css into Squarespace (or host it and link it).

Goal: Every visual decision is controlled here.

Step 2 — Use CSS tokens like a real design system
In tokens.css, define everything:

Colors

Font sizes

Spacing scale

Border radius

Shadow

Container widths

Breakpoints

Example (conceptually):

--space-1…--space-8

--font-size-1…--font-size-6

--radius-1…--radius-3

--container-max

Now every component uses tokens, not random values.

Step 3 — Force a consistent layout without “dragging”
This is the key part.

A) Decide your global layout pattern
For example:

All page content sits inside a centered container max-width

Sections have consistent vertical padding

Grids snap at breakpoints (1 col mobile, 2 col tablet, 3 col desktop)

B) Apply it globally with CSS targeting Squarespace’s structure
Squarespace wraps blocks/sections with consistent classes in 7.1. You’ll write CSS that:

Sets consistent paddings on sections

Normalizes typography inside text blocks

Normalizes images and buttons

Controls gaps between blocks

You’re essentially “skinning” Squarespace.

Step 4 — Build “components” as Saved Sections, but styled by CSS classes
Since Squarespace won’t give you custom section types, you create them like this:

Your workflow for each reusable component section
Add a new section in the page

Add only the blocks the client should edit (text, image, buttons)

Add a single Code Block at the top of the section containing only a tiny marker (not styling)

Your CSS uses that marker to style the entire section as a component

Save the section as a Saved Section

Why a Code Block marker?
Because Squarespace doesn’t give you “add a class to section” reliably in the UI.

So you create your own “component tag” inside the section. Example marker text:

<div class="cnb-section cnb-hero"></div>

Then your CSS can target .cnb-hero and apply layout rules to the rest of the section content.

UI work remains minimal: insert blocks; no visual tweaking.

Step 5 — Create a component library page (your assembly line)
Make a Not-Linked page called “Section Library” and build:

10–15 Saved Sections that cover Phase 1, like:

Hero (image + headline + subcopy + CTA)

Editorial split (image left/text right)

Card grid (3 cards)

Quote/testimonial

“Dinner Party” feature strip

CTA banner

FAQ accordion (text blocks)

Simple image gallery strip

“Story list” section (auto pulling from blog if used)

Once this exists, building new pages is:

Insert Saved Sections

Replace text/images

Done

Step 6 — Make client edits dead-simple
Your client should only do these actions:

Click text → edit

Click image → replace

Click button → change label/link

Insert a Saved Section → repeat

So you must avoid patterns that require:

moving blocks around

resizing columns

adjusting padding/margins in UI

All spacing comes from your CSS system.

Step 7 — Use collections for anything that should update everywhere
If “Stories” is a blog/collection, then your homepage “featured stories” should be a Summary/collection display so:

client adds a story once

it shows up everywhere automatically

This avoids duplicate edits.

Step 8 — Minimal JS only for behaviors Squarespace can’t do
Examples:

Accordion toggles

Tabs

Sticky mobile CTA

“Reveal on scroll” (optional)

Navbar behavior

Keep it lightweight and maintainable.

The bare minimum UI work, in concrete terms
For each page/section, you will do ONLY:

Add section

Add the editable blocks (text/image/button)

Paste a tiny marker element in a Code Block (one-liner)

Save section

No dragging things into place. No “nudging” columns.

When you add a Code Block in your Squarespace 7.1 editor, do you have access to:

Code Block that renders HTML, and

Code Injection (header/footer) on the plan you’re on?

If yes, perfect — we’ll do the marker-based component system above.

If no (rare), we’ll switch to a “structure-only blocks + global selectors” approach that targets sections based on their block order, but it’s more brittle.

You said:
What is this inn our repo:     to:contentReference[oaicite:3]{index=3}js ?
ChatGPT said:
That string is not real JavaScript and it’s almost certainly an accidental artifact from an AI/citation system, not something you (or Squarespace/Shopify) should ever ship.

What it is
to:contentReference[oaicite:3]{index=3}js

oaicite / contentReference are internal citation markers some AI tools insert when they’re trying to attach sources.

If it’s showing up in your repo, it usually means:

someone copy/pasted text from an AI chat response that included hidden citation markup, or

a formatter/MD-to-HTML step accidentally preserved it as literal text.

What to do
Search the repo for oaicite and contentReference.

If you’re in the repo root:

Windows PowerShell:

Select-String -Path .\* -Pattern "oaicite|contentReference" -Recurse
macOS/Linux:

grep -RIn "oaicite\|contentReference" .
Delete it wherever it appears. It has no valid purpose in production code.

If it’s inside a JS file and causing errors, it might be breaking a line like:

to:contentReference[oaicite:3]{index=3}js
Just remove that entire token/line.

If it appears inside a template / markdown / CMS content
Remove it from the source content and re-run your build.

If you’re using any “AI export” or “copy with citations” workflow, turn off citations or copy as plain text.

Quick sanity check
If you paste the 3–10 lines above and below it (and tell me which file it’s in), I’ll tell you exactly what to remove and what the line was probably supposed to be.

(But bottom line: it’s junk and safe to delete.)
