Cupcakes + Broccoli Squarespace 7.1 Implementation Plan (Code-First Approach)
Overview and Goals
Cupcakes + Broccoli’s website will be built on Squarespace 7.1 with a code-first approach to achieve a custom design while keeping the Squarespace visual editor usage minimal. The goal is to create a mobile-first site that shares design DNA with its Shopify counterpart (Lillier) and is easy for the client to edit basic content (text/images) without touching code. Key objectives include:
Use GitHub for code management: All HTML snippets, CSS, and JS will be stored and version-controlled on GitHub. The Squarespace site will pull these in via code injection (global header/footer and special pages) rather than relying on drag-and-drop design.
Minimal Squarespace sections: We will use Squarespace sections as lightweight editable shells or hooks – essentially placeholders where the client can later update text or swap images via the Squarespace editor. Complex layouts and styling will be handled by custom code, not by the Squarespace UI.
Content-forward, reusable sections: The design will prioritize content (text, imagery) and use a library of section templates that can be reused across pages. This ensures consistency and easy extension (the client can duplicate a section for a new page and just edit content).
Lightweight content gating: Implement a simple custom solution to gate member-only content (e.g. certain “Stories” or resources) without using the full Squarespace Member Areas feature. This avoids a heavy SaaS-style member portal while still restricting access to premium content.
AI concierge modal (Phase 1): Include a non-functional placeholder for the Cupcakes + Broccoli AI Concierge – likely an overlay/modal launched from the home page – to be styled and positioned per design, but just a static dummy for now.
No live dynamic data (Phase 1): All interactive or dynamic-looking elements (e.g. the AI concierge Q&A, membership status) will be implemented with static front-end code or dummy content. No live API calls or backend integration are planned in this phase, which keeps complexity low.
This plan is a step-by-step developer-ready guide balancing flexibility and maintainability with ease of handoff. In each step, we reference project documents for page structure and goals to ensure alignment with the intended architecture.
1. GitHub Repository Setup and File Structure
Set up a dedicated GitHub repository to manage all custom code for the Squarespace site. Organize the repository for clarity and long-term maintenance:
CSS: Create a css/ directory for styles. Use logical separation such as:
css/main.css – Global site styles (typography, layout, header/footer styling, common section styles).
css/pages/… – Optional sub-files for page-specific CSS if needed (e.g. home.css for the home page hero and special elements, about.css, etc.). Keep these small; prefer combining into main.css if possible to reduce file requests.
css/special.css – Styles for Squarespace system pages (lock screen, etc.) if they need distinct CSS tweaks (could also be included in main.css with scoped selectors).
JS: Create a js/ directory for scripts. Examples:
js/main.js – Core JavaScript to be loaded on all pages (e.g. navigation enhancements, footer behaviors, any site-wide interactions).
js/ai-concierge.js – Specific script for the AI concierge modal behavior (Phase 1 placeholder). This can be included in main.js or separate if you prefer to toggle it on only the home page.
Keep scripts modular and well-commented for clarity. No external API calls should be present – any “dynamic” behavior will be simulated or left as a stub for future implementation.
HTML: If there are significant chunks of custom HTML (for example, a complex component that will be injected via code block or script), you can store them in the repo as .html snippet files for versioning. For instance, html/ai-modal.html could contain the markup for the AI concierge modal. In practice, this HTML might be directly included via code injection or a Code Block in Squarespace, but having it in GitHub ensures changes are tracked.
Assets: An assets/ directory can hold any custom images, SVGs, or font files if needed. However, for content images that the client will swap, plan to use Squarespace’s built-in image management (so the client can replace via the editor). Only store design assets that are truly static (e.g. an SVG icon used in styling) in the code repo.
README: Document the project structure and any build/deploy process. While Squarespace doesn’t have a build pipeline, note in the README how to update the Squarespace site with the latest code (e.g. “copy main.css content into Squarespace header injection” or if using external hosting, how to point to the latest version). This will help during handoff.
Rationale: A clear file structure makes it easy to find and update parts of the code. Using GitHub for version control means any changes to styling or scripts can be tracked and rolled back if needed, supporting the goal of maintainability. It also allows collaboration or review before pushing updates to the live site.
2. Squarespace Configuration for Code Injection
Next, prepare the Squarespace 7.1 site to receive custom code. Squarespace 7.1 does not support Developer Mode (no full template code access), so we will leverage Code Injection for global modifications
. Ensure the site is on a plan that supports code injection (Business or higher, as required by Squarespace)
. Key steps:
Global Header Injection: In Squarespace’s Settings → Advanced → Code Injection, add a <style> link or tag for the CSS, and any <script> tags needed in the header. For maintainability, link to external files on GitHub or another host if possible. For example, you could host main.css and main.js on GitHub Pages or another CDN and include:
<link rel="stylesheet" href="https://YOUR_CDN_URL/css/main.css">
and similar for the script:
<script src="https://YOUR_CDN_URL/js/main.js" defer></script>
This way, updates just require pushing to GitHub (which updates the CDN) without manually copy-pasting code. If an external host is not feasible, you can paste the raw CSS inside <style>…</style> tags and JS inside <script>…</script> tags in the injection panel
. Keep the code organized and commented even in this panel for clarity.
Global Footer Injection: Use the footer code injection for any scripts that should load at the end of the body. In our case, since we’ll defer most JS (using the defer attribute or placing in footer), you can include the main JS here if not already linked in header. The footer is also a good place for any inlined script that needs to execute after the DOM is ready. Make sure to wrap any inline JS in <script> tags
.
Per-Page Injection (if needed): Squarespace allows page-specific code injection (in page settings). We will keep most code global, but if a certain script or style should only run on a specific page (e.g. home page hero special effect), you can add a small snippet in that page’s “Page Header Code Injection” field
. We will avoid heavy use of this to keep things consolidated, but it’s an option for page-specific tweaks. (Note: Squarespace recommends not injecting large HTML in page header injection, and transparency settings can affect it
, so we’ll primarily use it only for small scripts or to set body classes if needed.)
Special Pages Injection: Squarespace provides separate code injection fields for system pages like the Lock Page, Order Confirmation, and Order Status pages
. Use these for code that should run only on those pages. For example, if we are using a password-protected page as a lightweight gating solution, we can style the Lock Page (password entry screen) via its injection field with custom HTML/CSS (e.g., a branded message or custom styling so it doesn’t look like a generic Squarespace lock page). Similarly, if any commerce elements (order confirmed or status) are used, we can inject styles to ensure they match the site design. For Phase 1 of Cupcakes + Broccoli, full commerce might not be used on Squarespace (since Lillier/Shopify handles store), but this is ready if needed.
Maintainability tips: Keep the injected code as lean as possible. Leverage external files for bulk code. Also, document in the GitHub repo or a shared document which code is injected where. For example, note that “main.css linked in header injection” and “special CSS for lock page is in Lock Page code injection panel (with corresponding code in repo).” This helps future developers or the client’s team know where to look if something needs changing.
3. Content Structure with Minimal Squarespace Sections
With the site ready for code, set up the pages and sections in Squarespace to serve as content hooks. We will create the main pages as per the site architecture (from the navigation and taxonomy documents): Top-Level Pages (Squarespace):
Home – Landing page with multiple content sections (hero, “What This Is” intro, AI Concierge intro, Learn, Work With Amanda, Blind Dinners, Membership teaser, closing section) as outlined in the content doc.
About – About the platform and Amanda Johnson’s story (text-heavy with perhaps images).
Work With Amanda – Page describing consulting/advisory services.
Learn – Resource library landing page (likely will contain links or listings for tools, frameworks, etc.).
Stories – Blog or articles section (likely implemented as a Blog collection in Squarespace for easy publishing of stories from founders, etc.).
Blind Dinners – Info page about Blind Dinners (mostly static content and perhaps a way to express interest).
Membership – Sales page for membership, with tier details and a call-to-action to join (the content doc even provides copy for Core vs Premium tiers).
Login – This may not be an actual page but a login URL. If using Squarespace’s Member Area or a custom login, this would be handled differently (possibly the login button triggers a Squarespace login popup or directs to a lock page). We’ll include a persistent login button in the header as specified.
For each page, follow these steps to create minimalistic sections:
Use Blank Sections: In the Squarespace page editor, add blank sections for each distinct content block. Avoid using pre-designed layouts; we just need plain containers that we’ll style via CSS. For example, on the Home page, add a blank section for the hero, another for the “What This Is” content, another for the AI concierge teaser, etc., corresponding to each content group in the home page outline. Do the same for other pages (e.g., About might be one or two sections, Stories index might use a blog section or summary block).
Insert minimal content blocks: Within each section, add only the basic elements needed for content that the client will edit:
For text content (headings, paragraphs), use Text Blocks. Type in the placeholder or actual text from the copy document. Do not worry about formatting (we’ll handle typography and positioning with CSS). If needed, break into multiple text blocks for semantic separation (e.g., one text block for a heading, another for a paragraph, so the client can edit them separately). You can also use the HTML editor within a text block to add span tags or classes if needed, but prefer to keep it simple for the client – rather add distinguishing classes via JavaScript after load if needed.
For images, use Image Blocks. This allows the client to replace the image via the Squarespace interface later. For instance, if the design calls for a hero background image or an inline image on a page, place an image block in that section. We can set it to a placeholder image (to be replaced with final photography). Through CSS, you might reposition or resize it, but using the block ensures the client can swap the image in the same spot.
For any list of items (like a grid of resources or stories), you have options: you can either use native Squarespace summary blocks or collection blocks (like a Blog for Stories), or simply hard-code the structure. Since we prefer code-first, we might use a Code Block for something like a custom HTML structure for a grid. However, remember the client can’t easily edit content inside a raw HTML Code Block without some technical know-how. So a compromise: if stories are a blog, let Squarespace handle the blog index and then style it via CSS. If resources under “Learn” are static, maybe each resource can be a small piece of content the client can edit (or a list we manage as static HTML but maybe not ideal). We’ll choose the approach per section:
Stories: Implement as a blog collection for ease of adding new stories. We will style the blog index page (and blog post pages) with CSS to fit the design. Member-only stories can be flagged (via a Category or Tag like “Members”) so we can visually indicate or restrict them (more on gating later).
Learn: Could be a regular page listing resource links. Possibly use a Summary Block to pull excerpts of “resource” posts, or manually list them in a text block with links. This depends on how these resources are stored – if they are also blog posts or pages. For Phase 1, a simple page with sections for each type of content (tools, frameworks, etc.) might suffice, with each item perhaps linking to a PDF or blog post. Again, use minimal text blocks for titles and descriptions; style them later.
Membership tiers: On the Membership page, rather than using a Squarespace pricing block, we can manually layout the two tiers using text and maybe an image or icon, to have full control with CSS. For example, two column text blocks or a small HTML in a Code Block. If the client needs to tweak the pricing or description, having them as normal text is best. We’ll just ensure our CSS can target those elements to apply the proper design (like making the price large, etc.).
Assign identifiers for styling: Squarespace doesn’t let you directly add IDs to sections in the UI, but each section gets a unique data-section-id and an optional name if you label the section in the editor (you can label a section for your own reference). We can use these auto-generated IDs or HTML structure to target sections. Alternatively, add a Code Block at the top of a section with a simple wrapped <div id="home-hero-section"></div> around the content to serve as a hook, but this is usually not necessary. We can mostly rely on nth-of-type or section indexes if the structure is consistent. For clarity, we might note the section IDs by inspecting the published site and use them in our CSS comments (e.g. /* Home hero section: section #block-yui-1234... */). This way, if Squarespace regenerates an ID (rare unless section is recreated), we can update it.
Ensure sections remain editable: Avoid adding complex code that the client might accidentally break in the visual editor. For instance, do not encourage the client to edit anything inside a Code Block – keep those for structures that won’t require client changes. All user-editable content (text paragraphs, headings, images) should be in normal blocks. That way, the client can use Squarespace’s in-line editor to change copy or replace an image without messing up HTML structure. The custom code will style these elements externally.
By the end of this step, we’ll have all pages set up in Squarespace with the correct sections and basic content in place (using the copy from the provided documents). The site will look very plain at this point, but it establishes the “hooks” we need: each piece of content exists and can be targeted via CSS/JS. Example – Home Page Structure:
Hero Section: Blank section with a Text Block for the headline (“Black ownership is Black survival…”), a Text Block for subhead, another for body copy, and perhaps two Button Blocks or linked Text for the primary CTA (“Join Membership”) and secondary CTA (“Learn More”). We might also include an Image Block if there’s a decorative image or background (or set a background image for the section via Squarespace’s section background setting, which the client can also change if needed).
“What This Is” Section: Blank section with Text Blocks for the headline (“Built Around Experience”) and body text. The inline link “Learn how Cupcakes + Broccoli works → About page” can be a part of that text block (the Squarespace editor allows hyperlinking text). We will style it to look like a nice link or button via CSS.
AI Concierge Teaser Section: Blank section with Text Blocks for the headline (“Ask What You Are Actually Stuck On”) and body explaining the AI concierge, plus perhaps a list of example prompts. We’ll also put a button or link “Ask a question” which will later trigger the modal. This button could be a standard Squarespace Button Block or a text link styled as a button – either way we will intercept its click with JS to open the modal.
Learn Section (Home page): Text for headline “Practical Learning You Can Use” and body, plus a CTA link “Explore Resources → Learn page”.
Work With Amanda Section (Home): Text for headline and body and CTA.
Blind Dinners Section (Home): Text for headline and body and CTA.
Membership Section (Home): Text for headline and body, and CTA buttons for “Join Membership” and “Compare Membership Options”.
Closing Section: Text for closing statement (“Ownership Is a Practice”) and final CTA link.
Each of these sections on the home page corresponds to content from the provided documents, ensuring we cover the intended messaging. Other pages will be structured in a similar breakdown. For instance, the About page might have sections for “This Is a Founder-Led Practice” (intro about Cupcakes+Broccoli) and “Who Amanda Is” (Amanda’s bio), broken into editable text blocks.
4. Injecting Custom CSS for Styling
With content in place, we implement the design through CSS. All custom styles will live in our main.css (and any supplementary CSS files) that we prepared in GitHub. We will inject this CSS site-wide via the <style> link or tag in the header (as set up in step 2). Key guidelines for CSS:
Global Styles and Reset: First, include any CSS reset/normalize to ensure consistency across browsers (you can use a mild reset or just zero-out margins on sections, etc.). Then define global typography styles (fonts, base font-size, line-heights) and color variables (Squarespace allows adding custom CSS freely, so you can use CSS variables or just hardcode values from the design spec). This ensures the site’s text and basic elements use the design’s font (likely the design partners specified a font). You can add the font via an @import or <link> if it’s a Google Font, or via the Squarespace font picker if it’s easier (though code-first suggests we might directly include it in CSS).
Section and Layout Styling: For each section (hero, about, etc.), write CSS rules to achieve the desired layout:
Use the Squarespace section structure to target elements. For example, Squarespace sections typically have a structure like <section data-section-id="..."><div class="section-content"><div class="row"><div class="col">.... You can target by the data-section-id or by using nth-of-type if the order is fixed. E.g., if the Home page hero is the first section on that page, section:nth-of-type(1) h1 { ... } could style the hero headline. A more robust approach is to use a custom identifier: if the hero’s heading text is unique, you could target it via its content or add a span with a class in the text. Alternatively, once the site is published, inspect and copy the section[data-section-id] for the hero and use that in the CSS (e.g. section[data-section-id="5f4c..."] { /* hero styles */ }). Document which ID corresponds to which section for clarity.
Positioning and Spacing: Use CSS Grid or Flexbox as needed to reposition elements. For instance, if you want text and image side by side, you could inject an extra wrapper via a Code Block or directly use CSS to make the section’s column into two columns. Many Squarespace blank sections by default stack content vertically; you can override by selecting the section in the editor and choosing a layout (like two columns) for that section if it helps, then Squarespace will handle the basic column HTML. But if we want full custom control, we might keep everything in one column and use our CSS to split content into columns or absolutely position an image. Use media queries (or flex-wrap) to ensure it’s mobile-friendly (design is mobile-first, so perhaps design expects single column on mobile, two columns on desktop, etc.).
Typography and Colors: Apply styles to headings, paragraphs, and links to match the design language. From the content, we see some phrases like “These stories are not performance. They are perspective.” which might be displayed in a special stylistic way. Ensure block quotes or emphasized text have appropriate styling. Use the brand colors for backgrounds or text as needed.
Buttons and CTAs: Style the CTA links and buttons consistently. The “Join Membership” or “Ask a question” should have a distinct button style. If using actual Button Blocks from Squarespace, override their classes (Squarespace buttons have classes like .sqs-block-button-element). If using text links, style those anchors to look like buttons (with padding, background, border radius, etc.). Make sure the login button in the header is styled as per design – perhaps a special highlight style to be obvious (the content doc notes a persistent login button, likely as a styled element in the nav).
Navigation & Header: Use CSS to customize the header navigation. We likely use the default Squarespace header for functionality (logo, menu, etc.) but we can override styling to match the design. For example, if we need the “Login” to appear as a button on the right, and other links normal, we can target that link specifically (maybe by its text or position) and style it differently (add a background, etc.). If the template does not support a persistent top banner, consider enabling the fixed header option in Squarespace (if available) or use CSS position: fixed on the header. Ensure the mobile menu styles are adjusted if needed (Squarespace will generate a mobile menu; we can style the hamburger icon or menu background to fit the brand).
Footer: Style the footer similarly. If using Squarespace’s default footer (with maybe some text or social links), we can keep it simple but match colors/spacing. Or if a custom footer is needed, we could treat it like other sections: minimal content in editor, then style via CSS.
Responsive (Mobile-First): Ensure the CSS is written mobile-first (as the goal is mobile-friendly). That means designing for small screens by default, then using @media (min-width: X) for larger screens to enhance layout. E.g., on mobile the hero section text might be centered and stacked, while on desktop it could be split with an image. Test across common breakpoints. Squarespace 7.1 is responsive out-of-the-box, but since we override a lot, we must explicitly handle responsiveness. For example, if we manually create columns using CSS grid, add a media query to stack them on narrow screens.
Testing CSS in Squarespace: As you develop the CSS, you can paste it into Site Styles → Custom CSS editor temporarily for live preview (Squarespace has a Custom CSS panel which shows changes in real-time). This is handy for quick iteration. Once satisfied, move the CSS to the code injection for final (the CSS editor and code injection can both be used, but it’s best to consolidate usage to one method to avoid confusion). Remember, Squarespace’s custom CSS editor has a 128k character limit
 – our CSS should stay well under this, but it’s a consideration if the design were very large. We can always link external CSS to bypass that limit entirely.
Throughout styling, refer back to the design specs (component/system guidelines from the design partner) to maintain consistency. The code-forward approach allows us to implement those specs precisely. For instance, if the design system defines a specific card style for “Stories” excerpts, we will implement that in CSS rather than relying on a native Squarespace block style. Maintainability: Keep the CSS organized with comments indicating sections/pages. For example:
/* Home – Hero Section */
section[data-section-id="..."] h1.hero-title { ... } 
This helps future developers (or a tech-savvy client contact) quickly find where to adjust styles for a given section. Use understandable class names and avoid overly specific selectors so minor content changes (like adding an extra text block) don’t break the layout. Also, document any Squarespace quirks – e.g., “using !important to override a Squarespace default here” or “targeting this button by nth-child because Squarespace doesn’t give an ID”.
5. Injecting Custom JavaScript for Interactivity
Next, implement JavaScript to handle any interactive elements or enhancements, keeping in mind that everything remains static/offline in Phase 1. Place the JavaScript in main.js (or split into multiple files as planned) and load it via code injection (preferably in the footer or deferred)
. Key scripts to include:
AI Concierge Modal (Placeholder): The home page has an “Ask a question” CTA that should trigger the AI concierge modal. Implement a modal dialog in HTML/CSS and use JS to control its visibility:
Add the modal’s HTML structure either directly on the page (e.g., in a Code Block placed on the Home page but hidden by default) or inject it via JavaScript on page load. For example, your main.js can create a div with id="ai-concierge-modal" containing some placeholder content (maybe a mock chat interface or simply a message “AI Concierge coming soon…”). Insert this into the DOM (e.g., append to body).
Style the modal via CSS (in main.css) to be full-screen overlay, centered container, etc., matching whatever design is intended. The content can be minimal now – perhaps just a title, a paragraph explaining this will be an AI assistant, and a close button.
JS functionality: Add an event listener to the “Ask a question” button/link so that on click, it sets the modal to display: block (or adds an active class). Also, attach a listener to the modal’s close button (an “X” or “Close” text) to hide the modal and restore the page state. The content doc notes that the user should not navigate away and closing returns to same scroll position – since we’re using an overlay, we inherently stay on the page; just ensure document.body overflow is handled (maybe add overflow: hidden to body when modal opens to prevent background scroll).
Since this is a placeholder, we won’t implement actual AI chat logic. You might include a dummy input box and a disabled “Submit” button just for looks, but clearly mark in code that functionality is pending future development.
Navigation Behavior: If the design calls for any custom navigation behavior (for example, a header that changes on scroll, or a mobile menu customization), use JS to add those classes. For instance, a common enhancement is making the header shrink or add a background on scroll. We can detect scroll and toggle a class like .scrolled on the header. Another example: the login button might open a dropdown or login form – if we go that route, JS would handle showing a login form. However, given we want to avoid heavy custom member systems in Phase 1, the login link likely just goes to a standard Squarespace login page or member area prompt. So navigation JS might be minimal.
Content Gating Logic (if any in JS): For the lightweight gating, we might use JS to hide/show certain content based on a condition (like presence of a cookie or password). If we decide not to use Squarespace’s built-in page password for member content, an approach could be:
Have member-only articles (stories) still visible in the listing but when a user clicks, the page content is replaced by a prompt to login if they are not authorized. We could implement this by checking a cookie (for example, set a cookie when a member logs in via some custom form) and if not present, replace the article content with a message or redirect to membership page. This requires writing a script on the story pages. However, this is somewhat advanced and might be overkill for Phase 1. Alternatively, simpler: mark member-only stories with a label and just prevent access via Squarespace’s own password or leave them published but with a note. We will revisit gating in the next section – but any JS needed for it would be included here (e.g., reading localStorage or toggling content).
Miscellaneous UI enhancements: If there are any other dynamic elements – for instance, a carousel or lightbox – try to use Squarespace’s built-in features first or a lightweight approach. We want to avoid heavy libraries for now (performance and simplicity). If a carousel is needed and Squarespace doesn’t provide one in 7.1 by default, consider a minimal vanilla JS snippet or a well-tested lightweight library (added via script). But only include if absolutely necessary, given the “no live data” constraint, probably we can avoid slideshows for now (or use static images in a grid instead of a fancy slider).
Performance considerations: Because our JS is injected and runs after the page loads, be mindful of performance
. Keep the scripts efficient and initialize only what’s needed. Use defer on script tags (or place scripts at bottom) so they don’t block page render. Also, guard against errors – wrap custom code in try/catch or conditionals especially if manipulating DOM elements that might not exist (for example, check if the “Ask a question” button is on the page before attaching the event, so it doesn’t error out on other pages). This ensures one page’s functionality doesn’t break the whole site’s script execution.
All scripts should be thoroughly tested on the Squarespace site (both desktop and mobile) to ensure they work with Squarespace’s environment. For instance, test that the AI modal overlay appears over the Squarespace header and that the Squarespace responsive menu still works when our code is present. Also verify that our code doesn’t conflict with any Squarespace built-ins (naming collisions, etc.). Since we can’t modify Squarespace’s core JS, we have to work alongside it. Maintainability: Document any third-party code or unusual techniques in the repo. For example, if we use a known workaround to detect Squarespace login status via JS, comment it with a source link. Keep functions modular. In the GitHub README or a wiki, list the main interactive features and how to adjust them (e.g., “To change the behavior of the AI modal, see js/ai-concierge.js…”). This will help future developers and make handoff smoother.
6. Lightweight Content Gating Strategy
One of the project requirements is to implement light membership / gated content without using the full Squarespace Member Areas system. In practice, this means certain pages or resources (for example, some “Stories” or in-depth resources in the Learn library) should only be accessible to members, but we want to avoid the overhead and design of Squarespace’s membership portal. We will adopt a simple gating approach in Phase 1:
Public vs. Members-Only Content Identification: We will identify which content is members-only based on the project docs. The “Stories” section explicitly notes: “Some stories are shared publicly. Others are reserved for members. Membership makes space for deeper reflection, trust, and care.”. This suggests that within the blog, certain posts will be premium. Similarly, perhaps some resources in “Learn” are for members only. We can use a convention like tagging those items with a label “Members” (for stories, use a Category or Tag in the blog named “Members-Only”). On the front-end, we can visually indicate locked content by a small padlock icon or “Members Only” text on those items in the list, via CSS content or a conditional (Squarespace’s summary block might allow a little HTML in excerpts, or we overlay via CSS).
Gating Implementation Options:
Password-Protected Pages: The simplest non-member-area gate is to use Squarespace’s built-in password protection on pages or collections. For instance, we could put all member-only stories in a separate blog collection that is password-protected (so only those with the password can see it). Or lock the individual story pages with a password. The downside is one universal password that must be shared with members, but for Phase 1 this might be acceptable (especially if membership is small or manually managed). If we go this route, we will customize the Lock Page to have appropriate messaging. Using the Lock Page code injection, we can add a message like “This content is for Cupcakes+Broccoli members. Enter the member password to continue.” and style it with the site’s branding (so the experience feels integrated, not a default lock screen)
. Once the user enters the password, Squarespace will remember it for that session.
Lightweight Account System: If individual accounts are needed (e.g., each member has their own login), we could still use Squarespace Member Areas but keep it minimal. That would involve setting up a Member Area for, say, “Member Resources” and assigning certain pages (or the blog) to that area. Squarespace would handle login, account creation, and password reset (the doc hints that “Password reset [is] handled through standard Squarespace flow” which implies they considered using Squarespace’s account system). We would then strip away any unnecessary parts of the Member Area UI through CSS (hiding any Squarespace-generated “profile” or visuals, to avoid a dashboard feel). The member-only pages would then automatically require login. This is more robust than a single password, but also more setup (and possibly an extra cost on Squarespace’s plan). Given the directive to avoid a full member area, we might hold off on this unless the client explicitly wants individual accounts in Phase 1.
Custom Code Gating: As mentioned under JS, a purely custom approach could involve an external authentication (like a simple form that sets a cookie). For example, we could create a custom login page (or modal) where a member enters an email and a predetermined password, and if correct, we set a cookie member=true. Our JS would then check for member=true cookie and reveal member content. However, this approach has security considerations (we’d be effectively hardcoding a password in client-side code, not truly secure) and is not recommended for anything beyond a trivial gating. We likely won’t do this for real membership – it’s mentioned just for completeness. We prefer using Squarespace’s built-in mechanisms for security and simplicity.
For Phase 1, the most practical approach is a single shared password for member content, as an interim solution. We can provide this password to paid members manually. All member-only pages (or blog posts) would be placed under a locked section. Implementation steps:
Mark all premium Stories as “locked”. In Squarespace, you can assign a password to a blog page (the entire blog or individual posts if needed by putting them in a locked folder). We might create two blog pages: “Stories” (public) and “Member Stories” (locked), and on the main Stories index page, show public stories normally and for members, perhaps instruct them to log in to see more. Alternatively, a single blog with mixed content: public posts accessible normally, member posts when clicked would prompt for password. Squarespace doesn’t support per-post password easily (aside from putting each in its own page), so dual-blog approach might be cleaner.
Use the Lock Page code injection to style the password prompt. We’ll include a friendly message and maybe a link like “Not a member? [Join here]” on that lock screen, which we can do via simple HTML injection (Squarespace’s lock page can be styled with custom code as noted)
.
The membership page will explain the tiers and have a Join CTA that likely leads to a payment. If we are not using Squarespace’s Member Areas purchase flow, we might direct “Join Membership” to the Shopify site (Lillier) to handle the checkout for membership purchase. Or possibly to a simple checkout (Squarespace Commerce does allow selling a subscription product – we could utilize that just to take payment and then email the person the password). The exact method is outside this site’s scope but should be clarified. Regardless, for our site implementation, the join button can link to whatever sign-up method is decided (could be an external form or a Shopify product page).
We will clarify these assumptions with the client, but ensure our code can accommodate changes. If later they switch to a true Member Area, our structure (sections and content) remains the same; only the gating method changes.
Note: Because this gating is “light,” we must set expectations that it’s not foolproof security – determined individuals might guess the password or share it. Given the “no dashboard” requirement, this trade-off is likely acceptable initially. The focus is on giving genuine members a reasonably easy way to access content while keeping casual visitors out, without deploying a full software solution. We’ll document in the handoff notes how to change the password or move to a more robust solution in the future if membership grows (e.g., enabling Member Areas or integrating a third-party service).
7. Handoff for Content Editing
To ensure long-term maintainability and easy handoff to a non-technical client, we will prepare documentation and configure the site such that the client can perform common updates without breaking the code:
Editable Content Guide: Provide a brief guide (could be part of the README or a separate doc) listing which content can be edited through Squarespace and how. For example: “Homepage hero text – edit via Squarespace page editor: navigate to Home page, first section, edit the Text Block text. Do not remove the text block or section.” And “Homepage hero background – replace the image by editing the section’s background image or the Image Block in that section.” By mapping content in this way, the client knows what’s safe to change. This ties back to our minimal section approach, which ensures that most text and images are indeed Squarespace-editable.
Reusable Sections & New Pages: Explain that if the client wants to add a new page or section later, they should duplicate existing sections to maintain design consistency. Squarespace 7.1 allows saving sections or duplicating them on pages. For instance, if they need another page similar to About, they can duplicate the About page as a starting point. Our code is mostly global, so it will style new pages/sections as long as the structure (classes or section types) is recognized. If they create something brand new, it might not automatically be styled – hence duplicating is safer. We can even save a couple of blank “template sections” in the site (not visible on any page) that contain the basic structure and classes for, say, a two-column text/image section, which they can copy when needed.
Training: During handoff, schedule a walkthrough with the client (or provide a video) to show how to perform simple edits: editing text in place, swapping an image (Squarespace makes that as simple as clicking the image block and uploading a new image), and adding a new story to the blog. Emphasize that they should avoid deleting certain blocks that our CSS expects (for example, if a section is designed assuming one heading and one paragraph block, deleting one might affect styling).
Safe Mode & Recovery: Inform the client about Squarespace’s Safe Mode for custom code. If a future code change causes issues, Squarespace can disable code injection via safe mode to let an admin fix it
. While we as developers will handle code updates via GitHub, it’s useful for the client to know in case they see something broken – they can alert a developer rather than trying to fix code themselves. Also, because code is in GitHub, we have an audit trail of changes and can roll back if needed.
By clearly delineating what the client can do without touching code, we fulfill the goal of owner editing and extension. The client can evolve content and even add pages within the boundaries of the styles we’ve set, and call a developer only when they need new functionality or major design changes.
8. Squarespace 7.1 Constraints and Workarounds
It’s important to acknowledge the limitations of Squarespace 7.1 so we design our implementation plan within those constraints and avoid pitfalls:
No Full Template Access: As noted, Squarespace 7.1 doesn’t allow editing the raw HTML templates or uploading our own theme. We cannot fundamentally change the markup structure of pages – only inject CSS/JS to alter appearance and behavior
. Our plan uses code injection and minimal sections as a workaround to achieve a custom feel without template access. We must live with certain Squarespace DOM structures and class names, tailoring our CSS/JS around them.
Loading Order and Performance: Injected code (especially JavaScript) loads after Squarespace’s own content, which can cause slight delays or visual pops if our code rearranges things post-load
. To mitigate this:
We kept most layout styling in CSS (which applies as soon as the CSS file loads, hopefully in the <head> to reduce flicker). Critical CSS for initial layout can even be inlined in the <head> if necessary to avoid waiting for a file.
We use defer for JS so it doesn’t block the page, and design interactions (like the modal) that don’t affect initial paint. A user likely won’t click “Ask a question” the millisecond the page loads, so it’s fine if our modal script initializes a moment later.
We avoid heavy DOM manipulations where possible. For example, instead of using JS to move elements around on load (which would definitely cause a flash), we try to structure HTML in the editor close to what we need and use CSS positioning. If we do need to insert an element (like the AI modal container), we do it quickly at the end of body.
Squarespace Classes and IDs: Squarespace might change some auto-generated IDs or class names if content is edited. We have to be cautious not to overfit our selectors. For example, rather than targeting a very specific ID that could change if the section is recreated, consider adding a known HTML element we control (like wrapping a text in a span with class hero-headline) so we can target .hero-headline in CSS. We balanced this against keeping the editor clean for the client. In critical places, we might inject a <span class="cnb-hook"> via the HTML editor around certain text – small enough that the client likely won’t disturb it – to have stable hooks.
Third-Party Scripts: If any are needed (say, an analytics snippet or a chat widget beyond the AI concierge), they should be added carefully. Code injection can handle them, but too many scripts can slow down the site. We’ll assume for now only basic analytics (Google Analytics or similar) which Squarespace can handle through its settings or we add via header injection.
Testing Forms or Commerce: If the site has any forms (perhaps a “Work with Amanda – Contact Form” or a newsletter signup), we should use Squarespace’s native form block for reliability. Style it with CSS to match our theme. Avoid custom form code if possible (Squarespace forms integrate with their backend nicely). For membership sign-up, as discussed, we likely direct to Shopify or another method, so we won’t implement a custom form in Squarespace aside from maybe a simple “Contact us to request an invite” for Blind Dinners.
Known Bugs: Keep an eye on any known 7.1 quirks. For example, some Squarespace templates have issues where code injection CSS might not apply due to Ajax page transitions. If the site uses Ajax loading between pages (some templates do), CSS added via code injection might not reapply on navigation. A workaround is to disable Ajax loading (we can turn off the “Enable Ajax Loading” setting in site settings if it’s on) so that each page load freshly includes our CSS/JS. Alternatively, ensure our JS re-attaches styles on SPA navigation events. But simplest: turn off Ajax if present, to be safe, so each page behaves independently.
SEO & Performance: Using custom code means we take on some responsibility for SEO (Squarespace SEO settings will still work for basic things like meta tags, but if we hide content behind scripts, ensure that important text is still present in the HTML for crawlers). We didn’t plan any content to load via JS (we’re using static content in the editor), so SEO is fine. Also, optimize images and assets since we’re not fully leveraging Squarespace’s style editor optimization. For example, if we add custom background images via CSS, ensure they are properly sized/compressed. If using Squarespace’s image blocks, it will handle responsive image variants.
By understanding these limitations, our implementation avoids fighting the platform unnecessarily. We acknowledge we cannot achieve certain things on Squarespace that a fully custom site could (for instance, truly dynamic filtering of content without an API), but we’ve designed the site within these bounds so that it delivers the intended experience without hitting a wall. If a future feature request demands something Squarespace can’t do (e.g. truly interactive webapp behavior), at that point we’d consider other solutions or the planned integration with Shopify/other tools. For now, the approach is solid for the defined scope.
9. Previewing, Testing, and Launch Workflow
Finally, detail how to efficiently preview changes and deploy updates, given the code-first workflow:
Staging Environment: Because Squarespace doesn’t have a traditional staging server, we use the “safe staging/duplicate workflow” mentioned in the outline. One approach is to duplicate the site within Squarespace. Squarespace allows exporting the site or duplicating it to a new trial site. We can maintain a parallel “staging” site where we apply code updates first to test in a live environment (just not public). This staging site could be on a temporary subdomain or password-protected entirely (via site-wide password). Once we verify everything, we replicate the changes on the live site (or in some cases, swap domains). This is especially useful if the site is already live and in use – we wouldn’t want to disrupt it while overhauling the design.
Using Preview Mode: If duplicating is not feasible, an alternative is to do major changes on the live site but keep it unlaunched or behind a coming-soon page until ready. For example, Squarespace has a site-wide password option; we could lock the site while building, then remove the password on launch. We likely have an existing site URL to work with (as the outline says “built in the existing Squarespace site + URL”), so possibly the site is already in progress. We’ll coordinate a method that causes minimal downtime – perhaps create all new pages and sections alongside any old content, and only switch navigation over when ready.
GitHub Integration: While Squarespace can’t auto-deploy from Git, we can still streamline updates: Do all editing in local files or a code editor, commit to GitHub, review changes, and then copy-paste the final code into Squarespace. To make this easier, keep related code contiguous. For instance, if adding a new section style, group those CSS rules together so you can easily copy that block from the diff. Alternatively, if using external hosting for assets, simply pushing to GitHub (which triggers the CDN update) may be enough – the Squarespace site will then pull the new files on refresh. We must coordinate cache busting (maybe update the linked file URL with a version query param when we want to force update, or instruct client to hard refresh). Document this in the README: e.g., “After pushing a CSS change, update the injection link to main.css?v=2 to ensure Squarespace serves the latest version.”
Browser Testing: Before launch, test the site on multiple devices and browsers. Ensure the code injection works consistently. Pay attention to the special pages (password lock page, 404 page if styled, etc.). We should simulate a scenario: a non-logged-in user clicking a members-only story sees the lock page and it looks right; a logged-in (or password-entered) user can see content. Also test the membership signup flow: click “Join Membership” – does it go to the correct place (Shopify or Squarespace checkout)? This needs to be seamless.
Squarespace QA: Use Squarespace’s built-in checks (there’s a pre-launch checklist in their docs) for things like SEO descriptions, social share images, etc. While not code-related, ensure that our code hasn’t hidden any content that should be available for SEO (for instance, if we have text in images or in JS only – which we avoided). Fill in page descriptions and titles via Squarespace settings as usual.
Launch: If we used a staging site or password, coordinate removing that. If we had a duplicate site approach, we might need to swap the domain mapping from an old site to the new one (Squarespace support can assist or has guidelines for that). Make sure the live domain uses the new site and everything still works on the custom code after domain change (hard-coded URLs in code should be minimal; use relative URLs or robust selectors so domain change doesn’t break anything).
Post-Launch Monitoring: After launch, monitor site for any issues (broken scripts, layout issues, any Squarespace updates that might affect us). Because custom code is present, if Squarespace changes something in their platform, we should verify nothing is impacted. For example, if Squarespace updates their base template CSS classes in an update, our CSS might need adjustments (unlikely for core elements, but possible). Keeping the code in GitHub will allow tracking any fixes.
By following this testing and launch plan, we ensure a smooth deployment with minimal surprises. It also gives the client confidence – we’re not just designing in the blind; we are testing in a live environment to ensure the approach is solid.
References: The above plan incorporates the project’s content structure and goals as outlined in prior documents. We prioritized a code-driven implementation to meet the goal of easy owner editing with a clear structure. We referenced the navigation and copy documents to ensure the site’s pages (Home, About, Work With Amanda, Learn, Stories, Blind Dinners, Membership, Login) and their content blocks are accounted for. The need for lightweight gating was confirmed by notes that some stories/resources are for members only, leading us to suggest password-protected content as a Phase 1 solution. We also heeded known Squarespace 7.1 limitations, such as no template code access and injected code performance considerations
, to ensure our approach remains maintainable. By following this guide, a developer can implement the Cupcakes + Broccoli site in Squarespace 7.1 effectively, creating a flexible yet easy-to-update platform for the client.

B) js/ai-concierge.js (overwrite entire file)
/* CNB — AI Concierge (placeholder)
   Requirement: opens as overlay/modal; user stays on Home; close returns to same scroll pos.
*/
(function () {
  window.CNB = window.CNB || {};

  function createModal() {
    const overlay = document.createElement("div");
    overlay.className = "cnb-modal-overlay";
    overlay.innerHTML = `
      <div class="cnb-modal" role="dialog" aria-modal="true" aria-label="AI Concierge">
        <div class="cnb-modal-header">
          <div class="cnb-modal-title">Cupcakes + Broccoli — AI Concierge</div>
          <button class="cnb-modal-close" type="button">Close</button>
        </div>
        <div class="cnb-modal-body">
          <p><strong>Placeholder for Phase 1.</strong> This is where the “Ask a question” experience will live.</p>
          <p>For now, we’re proving the interaction model: overlay opens, no navigation, and closes back to the same scroll position.</p>
        </div>
      </div>
    `;
    document.body.appendChild(overlay);
    return overlay;
  }

  function lockScroll() {
    const y = window.scrollY || 0;
    document.body.dataset.cnbScrollY = String(y);
    document.body.style.position = "fixed";
    document.body.style.top = `-${y}px`;
    document.body.style.left = "0";
    document.body.style.right = "0";
  }

  function unlockScroll() {
    const y = Number(document.body.dataset.cnbScrollY || "0");
    document.body.style.position = "";
    document.body.style.top = "";
    document.body.style.left = "";
    document.body.style.right = "";
    delete document.body.dataset.cnbScrollY;
    window.scrollTo(0, y);
  }

  window.CNB.aiConcierge = {
    init() {
      // Only activate if the Home AI section exists
      const aiSection = document.querySelector(".cnb-home-ai");
      if (!aiSection) return;

      const overlay = createModal();

      const open = () => {
        overlay.dataset.open = "true";
        lockScroll();
      };

      const close = () => {
        overlay.dataset.open = "false";
        unlockScroll();
      };

      overlay.addEventListener("click", (e) => {
        if (e.target === overlay) close();
      });
      overlay.querySelector(".cnb-modal-close")?.addEventListener("click", close);

      // Hook 1: Form submit (recommended)
      const form = aiSection.querySelector("form");
      if (form) {
        form.addEventListener("submit", (e) => {
          e.preventDefault();
          open();
        });
      }

      // Hook 2: If you used a Button block instead of a Form, clicking it opens modal
      aiSection.querySelectorAll(".sqs-block-button a").forEach((a) => {
        a.addEventListener("click", (e) => {
          e.preventDefault();
          open();
        });
      });
    },
  };
})();
C) js/main.js (overwrite entire file)
/* CNB — bootstrap
   Adds stable classes to sections based on section Anchor IDs, so CSS stays clean.
*/
(function () {
  function findSectionByAnchorId(anchorId) {
    const el = document.getElementById(anchorId);
    if (!el) return null;

    // Sometimes the ID is on the <section>, sometimes on a child.
    return el.closest("section") || el;
  }

  function claimSection(anchorId, classes) {
    const section = findSectionByAnchorId(anchorId);
    if (!section) return null;

    section.classList.add("cnb-section", ...classes);

    // Helpful: add a stack wrapper class to whatever Squarespace uses as section inner container
    const inner =
      section.querySelector(".content-wrapper") ||
      section.querySelector(".section-border-content") ||
      section;

    inner.classList.add("cnb-stack");
    return section;
  }

  document.addEventListener("DOMContentLoaded", () => {
    document.body.classList.add("cnb");

    // Home anchors we’ll set in Squarespace
    const claimed = {
      "cnb-home-hero": ["cnb-home-hero"],
      "cnb-home-what": ["cnb-home-what"],
      "cnb-home-ai": ["cnb-home-ai"],
      "cnb-home-learn": ["cnb-feature"],
      "cnb-home-work": ["cnb-feature"],
      "cnb-home-dinners": ["cnb-feature"],
      "cnb-home-membership": ["cnb-feature"],
      "cnb-home-closing": ["cnb-feature"],
    };

    let isHome = false;

    Object.entries(claimed).forEach(([id, cls]) => {
      const section = claimSection(id, cls);
      if (section && id === "cnb-home-hero") isHome = true;
    });

    if (isHome) document.body.classList.add("cnb-home");

    // Mark secondary buttons (simple heuristic: 2nd button in hero + 2nd in membership)
    const hero = document.querySelector(".cnb-home-hero");
    if (hero) {
      const btnBlocks = hero.querySelectorAll(".sqs-block-button");
      if (btnBlocks[1]) btnBlocks[1].classList.add("cnb-btn-secondary");
      const ctaRow = document.createElement("div");
      ctaRow.className = "cnb-cta-row";
      // If Squarespace renders buttons separated, we just wrap visually by adding a row class to their parent container.
      // (CSS will still work even if this doesn't happen.)
      const parent = btnBlocks[0]?.parentElement;
      if (parent) parent.classList.add("cnb-cta-row");
    }

    const membership = document.querySelector("#cnb-home-membership")?.closest("section") || document.querySelector(".cnb-home-membership");
    if (membership) {
      const btnBlocks = membership.querySelectorAll(".sqs-block-button");
      if (btnBlocks[1]) btnBlocks[1].classList.add("cnb-btn-secondary");
    }

    // AI modal placeholder
    if (window.CNB && window.CNB.aiConcierge && typeof window.CNB.aiConcierge.init === "function") {
      window.CNB.aiConcierge.init();
    }
  });
})();

Are we avoiding Squarespace visual styling? (Yes: we’re relying on injected CSS)

AI “Ask a question” opens overlay and closes back to same scroll? (Yes: placeholder modal + scroll lock)

Thought for 4m 31s
You’ve got a lot of noise in that console log, but there’s one item that can absolutely block your progress:

The one error that matters
This is coming from your legacy injected script:

Uncaught TypeError: Cannot read properties of null (reading 'addEventListener')

In the legacy code, you attach an event listener like this (unconditionally):

document.getElementById("category-select").addEventListener(...) 
custom_footer_code_injected_leg…


If #category-select doesn’t exist on the page you’re testing (your new homepage/test page), that line throws and halts the rest of that script execution. If your new “load my GitHub JS/CSS” code lives after this inside the same injected block, it will never run.

Also, the legacy code assumes #category-select exists in multiple places (e.g., populateCategories) and will throw there too.

The Facebook/Statsig/YUI stuff is mostly Squarespace + adblocker noise and can be ignored for now.

Best way to make headway (fastest path)
Step 1 — Prove your new code injection is executing
In Settings → Advanced → Code Injection → Header, temporarily paste:

<style>
  body::before{
    content:"CNB HEADER INJECTION LOADED";
    position:fixed; z-index:999999;
    top:10px; left:10px;
    padding:8px 10px;
    background:#000; color:#0f0;
    font:12px/1.2 monospace;
  }
</style>
In Footer, temporarily paste:

<script>console.log("CNB FOOTER INJECTION LOADED", location.pathname);</script>
Hard refresh (Ctrl+Shift+R).
If you don’t see the green label + console log, stop and fix that first (wrong place, caching, editing a different site, etc.).

Step 2 — Defuse the legacy script so it can’t break new pages
If you still need that legacy code kept around, wrap it so it only runs when its DOM exists.

Where to change: inside the legacy injected footer code.

Replace the line that currently does this:

document.getElementById("category-select").addEventListener("change", event => {
  renderCatalog(event.target.value);
});
With this safe version:

const categorySelectEl = document.getElementById("category-select");
if (categorySelectEl) {
  categorySelectEl.addEventListener("change", (event) => {
    renderCatalog(event.target.value);
  });
} else {
  // Not the catalog page—do nothing.
  return;
}
That directly addresses the crash point in your legacy code. 
custom_footer_code_injected_leg…


Even better: move the entire legacy script off global injection and into the specific page that needs it:

Pages → (the old page) → ⚙️ → Advanced → Page Footer Code Injection
So it never touches your new CNB pages.

Step 3 — Load your GitHub-hosted CSS/JS the “Squarespace way”
Squarespace code injection can style/behavior your site, but it cannot “render” your repo’s HTML into the page. So the pattern is:

Build page structure in Squarespace sections (minimal)

Control look/feel in your own CSS

Add behaviors with your JS

To host assets on GitHub cleanly, use GitHub Pages (recommended) or jsDelivr.

Option A: GitHub Pages (recommended)

Repo → Settings → Pages

Source: main branch, /root (or /docs if you prefer)

Your files will be served at something like:
https://scampbe3.github.io/CNB/...

Then inject:

Header injection

<link rel="stylesheet" href="https://scampbe3.github.io/CNB/assets/css/main.css?v=1">
Footer injection

<script defer src="https://scampbe3.github.io/CNB/assets/js/ai-concierge.js?v=1"></script>
<script defer src="https://scampbe3.github.io/CNB/assets/js/main.js?v=1"></script>
(That ?v=1 is just cache-busting while you iterate.)

2) assets/js/main.js (replace the whole file)
(function () {
  "use strict";

  function hasCNBSections() {
    return Boolean(document.getElementById("cnb-hero"));
  }

  document.addEventListener("DOMContentLoaded", () => {
    console.log("[CNB] main.js loaded");

    if (!hasCNBSections()) return;

    // Wire AI concierge button behavior (modal/overlay)
    // We’ll look for a button that links to #cnb-ai-concierge (easy to set in the UI).
    const aiSection = document.getElementById("cnb-ai-concierge");
    if (aiSection) {
      const aiLink = aiSection.querySelector('a[href="#cnb-ai-concierge"]');
      if (aiLink) {
        aiLink.addEventListener("click", (e) => {
          e.preventDefault();
          if (window.CNB_AI && typeof window.CNB_AI.open === "function") {
            window.CNB_AI.open();
          } else {
            console.warn("[CNB] CNB_AI modal not available");
          }
        });
      }
    }
  });
})();
3) assets/js/ai-concierge.js (replace the whole file)
(function () {
  "use strict";

  function buildModal() {
    const overlay = document.createElement("div");
    overlay.id = "cnb-ai-modal";
    overlay.setAttribute("aria-hidden", "true");
    overlay.style.cssText = `
      position: fixed; inset: 0; z-index: 999999;
      display: none;
      background: rgba(0,0,0,0.55);
      padding: 24px;
    `;

    const panel = document.createElement("div");
    panel.style.cssText = `
      max-width: 720px; margin: 6vh auto;
      background: #fff; color: #000;
      border-radius: 16px;
      padding: 22px 18px;
      box-shadow: 0 20px 60px rgba(0,0,0,0.25);
      font-family: system-ui, -apple-system, Segoe UI, Roboto, Arial, sans-serif;
    `;

    panel.innerHTML = `
      <div style="display:flex; align-items:center; justify-content:space-between; gap:12px;">
        <div style="font-weight:700; font-size:16px;">AI Concierge (Placeholder)</div>
        <button id="cnb-ai-close" style="
          border:1px solid rgba(0,0,0,0.25);
          background:#fff;
          border-radius:999px;
          padding:8px 12px;
          font-weight:600;
          cursor:pointer;
        ">Close</button>
      </div>

      <div style="margin-top:14px; color:rgba(0,0,0,0.75); line-height:1.5;">
        This is a Phase 1 placeholder for the AI concierge experience.
        In Phase 2, this becomes the tailored concierge for Black founders.
      </div>

      <div style="margin-top:14px;">
        <input id="cnb-ai-input" type="text" placeholder="Ask a question (Phase 1 placeholder)"
          style="width:100%; padding:12px 14px; border-radius:12px; border:1px solid rgba(0,0,0,0.2);" />
      </div>

      <div style="margin-top:12px; display:flex; gap:10px; justify-content:flex-end;">
        <button id="cnb-ai-submit" style="
          border:1px solid rgba(0,0,0,0.85);
          background:#000; color:#fff;
          border-radius:999px;
          padding:10px 14px;
          font-weight:700;
          cursor:pointer;
        ">Submit</button>
      </div>
    `;

    overlay.appendChild(panel);
    document.body.appendChild(overlay);

    function close() {
      overlay.style.display = "none";
      overlay.setAttribute("aria-hidden", "true");
    }

    overlay.addEventListener("click", (e) => {
      if (e.target === overlay) close();
    });

    panel.querySelector("#cnb-ai-close").addEventListener("click", close);
    panel.querySelector("#cnb-ai-submit").addEventListener("click", () => {
      const val = panel.querySelector("#cnb-ai-input").value.trim();
      console.log("[CNB] AI placeholder submitted:", val);
      close();
    });

    return { overlay, close };
  }

  let modal;

  window.CNB_AI = {
    open() {
      if (!modal) modal = buildModal();
      modal.overlay.style.display = "block";
      modal.overlay.setAttribute("aria-hidden", "false");
      const input = modal.overlay.querySelector("#cnb-ai-input");
      if (input) input.focus();
    }
  };
})();
C) One tiny Squarespace UI trick to make JS wiring painless
In your AI Concierge section, set the button’s link to:

#cnb-ai-concierge

That makes it super easy for your JS to find the correct button without needing custom block IDs (and it matches the “opens as modal overlay” requirement).

ChatGPT said:

Thought for 1m 32s
Those console lines you pasted are all Squarespace/editor + adblock noise. None of them explain “my GitHub CSS/JS isn’t affecting the page.” The thing that does explain it is almost always one of these:

your <link>/<script> tags are not actually present on the page you’re testing

the URLs you’re using are wrong / returning 404 / not serving as executable JS/CSS

the browser cached the “blank” versions (you removed the ?v= cache-buster right after the files started as blank)

your CSS selectors don’t match Squarespace’s DOM, so the CSS loads but does nothing

Here’s the fastest way to isolate which one it is.

1) First: confirm you’re testing the live page, not the Squarespace editor
Your log includes config/… messages, which usually means you’re inside the editor shell.

Do this:

Open the page in a totally separate tab (not the editor), ideally an incognito window.

Hard refresh (Ctrl+Shift+R).

If you only test inside /config, you can chase ghosts all day.

2) “View Source” check (takes 10 seconds)
On the live page:

Right click → View Page Source

Search for main.css or main.js

If you do not see your <link rel="stylesheet" …> and <script …> tags in the page source, then your Code Injection isn’t actually being applied to that page (wrong site, wrong injection location, not saved, etc.).
If you do see them, move on.

3) Network tab check (this tells the truth instantly)
On the live page:

DevTools → Network

Filter by main.css then main.js

Reload the page

You want to see:

Status 200

And the response body contains your CSS/JS

If you see 404, it’s the URL.
If you see 200 but the response is empty/old, it’s caching.
If you see (blocked), it’s content-type / CSP / mixed-content.

4) Put the cache-buster back (this is very likely your issue)
You said all repo files were blank at first, then you filled them in, and you removed ?v=1.

That’s a perfect recipe for: “browser cached the blank file → no changes forever.”

Put it back and increment it every time you change code:

<link rel="stylesheet" href="YOUR_URL/main.css?v=2">
<script defer src="YOUR_URL/ai-concierge.js?v=2"></script>
<script defer src="YOUR_URL/main.js?v=2"></script>
Do v=3, v=4, etc. while iterating.

5) Use a “known-good” GitHub CDN URL (recommended)
If you’re using a normal GitHub “blob” URL, it will not load as a real asset.

The easiest reliable approach (if repo is public): jsDelivr
It serves correct MIME types and caches well.

Use this format:

https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/<user>/<repo>@<branch>/<path>

Example (adjust paths to your real repo layout):

<link rel="stylesheet" href="https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/scampbe3/CNB@main/css/main.css?v=2">
<script defer src="https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/scampbe3/CNB@main/js/ai-concierge.js?v=2"></script>
<script defer src="https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/scampbe3/CNB@main/js/main.js?v=2"></script>
If your folders are at root (you said they’re not under assets/), this will match that.

Important: repo must be public for jsDelivr.

Alternative: GitHub Pages
Also solid, but requires enabling Pages in repo settings. If you haven’t, the URLs will 404.

6) Canary test so we don’t depend on Squarespace DOM
Even if your CSS loads, your selectors might not match.

Temporarily add this as the first lines of main.css:

body { outline: 10px solid lime !important; }
And add this at the top of main.js:

console.log("[CNB] main.js actually executed");
Reload the live page:

If you don’t see the lime outline → CSS isn’t loading.

If you don’t see the console log → JS isn’t loading/executing.

This avoids any “maybe the selectors are wrong” ambiguity.

Most likely cause given what you described
Because your files were blank and you removed ?v=1, the most likely situation is: you’re still loading cached blank files (either in browser cache or CDN cache). Re-adding the query string usually fixes it instantly.

Second most likely: the URLs you’re using are not “raw/CDN” asset URLs (e.g., they contain /blob/).

Paste these two things and I’ll tell you exactly what’s wrong
The exact three tags currently in Squarespace injection (the <link> and two <script> lines)

The exact URL you get when you click the CSS file in the browser (what opens in a new tab)

With just those, I can tell you if it’s (a) wrong URL format, (b) 404, (c) cached blank content, or (d) injection not present on the page.
